THE RED PAGE.
H. H. Lusk, the barrister who for a short time
represented a Maoriland constituency in the
House of Representatives, and who also practised
in Sydney—and stopped suddenly—has found favor
with the editor of an American magazine, ‘ The
Forum.’ To its pages he has contributed several
articles on M.L. politics; and in the April num-
ber he is starred as ‘ Hon. Hugh H. Lusk, ex-
member of the New Zealand Legislature.’ While
in N.S. Wales Mr. Lusk competed for a prize
offered by the Education Department for
school history of the province. There were nine
other competitors, and a board composed of the
late Under-Secretary for Education (E. Johnson'
and two of the Department’s chief officials (J. C.
Maynard and F. Bridges) accepted Lusk’s history
and awarded him ,£450. This was in 1890. The
work was set up in type at the Govt. Printing
Office, and this type has been kept standing ever
since at great expense while the Department has
been tinkering at the book without daring to pub
lish it —seemingly because a gross blunder was
made in approving it as fit for use or publication
in the first instance. The total cost of the ‘ his
tory ’ to date is over <£looo ; and all the available
evidence goes to show that if it ever does reach
publication seven years of ‘revision’ will have
altered it so completely that Lusk will be unable
to recognise his own handiwork.
* * * *
The history of the ‘ history ’ is a pathetic recorc
of departmental incapacity. The adjudicators
appear to have been informed of their error soon
after the book was put in type, and various Minis-
ters of Education have had hard work to keep
within reasonable distance of the truth while ex-
plaining matters to objectionable Parliamentary
querists. The book was first ‘ revised ’by two of
the adjudicators, Messrs. Maynard and Bridges;
and one would like to know if it is true, as re-
ported, that these gentlemen received fees of
25g3. and lOgs. respectively for their trouble.
Then a schoolmaster, Mr. G. Metcalfe, was
asked by Minister Carruthers to read the work;
and on finding style and matter alike lamentable
this gentleman is said to have condemned it with-
out fee and utterly. Inter alia, Lusk’s ‘history’
is declared to have stated that no seals are now
found on the Australian coast (he had apparently
never heard of Seal Rocks near Port Stephens,
Seal Rocks in Western Port, or the seal rendezvous
at Julia Percy island and Houtman’s Abrolhos);
that the first fleet called at Cape Yerde islands
(instead of the Canaries); that Leichhardt, on his
Port Essington expedition, started from Sydney,
proceeded North and wandered about on the Dar-
ling Downs, eventually losing himself, but return-
ing to Brisbane after having completely failed ;
and so on. (Of course Leichhardt (lid start from
Sydney, but in a vessel, just as he had left Ger-
many in a vessel.)
* • * •
In the course of time Minister Carruthers was
succeeded by Miuister Suttor, who also declined
to approve the ‘ history,’ but took it away with
him to Bathurst for a few months, in the hope
that mountain air might invigorate its constitu-
tion. Minister Garrard, however, finds that the
invalid is not yet strongenou: h to bear publicity;
though it has meantime been under further re-
vision by Mr. Caldwell, a clerk in the Education
Department (did he also receive an extra fee of
25gs. ?), and by Judge Windeyer—who altered
one phrase by substituting 1 juryman ’ for ‘ judge ’
in the assertion that ‘ Every man may thus be a
judge.’ Now the book is understood to be under-
going its half-dozenth revision at the hands of
another schoolmaster, Mr. Finney. The other
day Minister Garrard declared that, as it origin-
ally stood, Lusk’s book was considered the best
school history of Australia existing. Yet three
successive Ministers have found it too bad to
issue to the schools, and after seven years of
‘revision ’ at the hands of a dozen formal and in-
formal critics it is still unfit for publication. If
this is ‘ the best,’ then what, in Australia’s name,
is the quality of the histories now in daily use?
* * » •
This little story throws interesting light on the
methods and capacity of an educational bureau-
cracy. It is a matter of common experience that
a schoolmaster is generally the most wooden-
headed doctrinaire anywhere to be found. He is
usually a slave to routine; his memory is culti-
vated at the expense of his imagination ; the con-
tinual study of small facts and dates and details
seems to incapacitate him from comprehending
and applying principles—from taking large views
or making wide generalisations; and his brain is
so bemused with books that it loses all freshness,
agility, originality. Naturally, therefore, as C.
H. Buzaoofcb (formerly editor of Brisbane
* Courier’) wrote the other day, ‘school-teachers
make very indifferent journalists, and no manu-
script which reaches a newspaper office ordinarily
needs more careful editing than that from school-
teachers. Most school-teachers are by far too
self-opinionated for press work, and are inclined
to treat their readers as though they were pupils.’
* * * •
Properly directed, this routine-doctrinaire force
is capable of producing excellent results ; for the
army of Australian school-teachers is composed
of most conscientious, hardworking, and estimable
men and women. It is a misfortune that illumin-
ation seems generally as lacking at the top as at
the bottom of the educational tree. The per-
manent heads of Australian educational depart-
ments, as a rule, are either men who have been
school-teachers themselves, or men who have un-
dergone school-teacher petrifaction in the course
of a long term of departmental service. Their
minds frequently appear narrow and hidebound,
incapable of welcoming an idea new to the system
in which they have been trained. The consequence
is that the plastic brains of the children under
their care are crushed beneath a weight of rule
and dogma which rarely consider the adaptation
of youthful means to individual or national ends,
but aim to cram and petrify the new genera-
tion just as it was the habit to cram and
petrify the old. Occasionally a theorising
Minister remains long enough in office to
do more harm than good by engrafting his par-
ticular fad on the system : the average Minis-
ter is unable to do even this. The depart-
ment ' machine is so complex that a talented man
hesitates to make his opinion practically valid
against the advice of the permanent heads ; and
a man of the type of Minister Garrard is wholly
at their mercy, and soon learns that he must do
as he is bid and reserve his energy for ft-less
speeches at school celebrations.
The glare, and noise, and tinsel—apt em-
blems of modern royalty—have left to New
South Wales only one substantial result of
Queen Victoria’s bread-and-circus jubilation—
John Farrell’s poem in the ‘Daily Telegraph’ of
22nd June. When he was younger, Farrell ran
to rhetoric, as behoved his Irish blood ; and his
early ballads, if a little crude in form, are full of
emotional force. In the process of years he has
lost fire and gained philosophy, and ‘ Ave Impera-
trix ! ’ is a poem conceived in the later Tenny-
sonian vein. Its fault is a too-great elaboration
of phrase at expense of the motion and mean-
ing of the whole, so that it is full of fine passages
without being precisely a fine poem. The trees
are so admirable that one does not get quite the
right emotion at sight of the wood. But the poem
is worth prizing, and will be prized the more
because stress of journalistic work gives the
Farrell of to-day few opportunities to recall the
pleasure with which one read the poet Farrell of
a day bygone.
* • » »
A third series of William Astley’s tales of the
convict era in Australia was recently published by
Geo. Robertson and Co. (* Tales of the Old
Regime,’ by Price Warung). Like those which
have preceded them, these tales were first
published in The Bulletin, and the collection
strengthens one’s sense of the author’s wonderful
industry and of his intellectual skill in treating the
matter thus laboriously accumulated. So remark-
able are these qualities that it seems pity he did
not endeavour to mass in a longer and climaxed
story the impressions he has recorded. ‘For the
Term of His Natural Life’, by no means ex-
hausts the field ; and a good long story has more
of the essence of permanent force than a good
short one, or even a series of good short ones.
Probably in emotional effect such a book by
William Asth>y would have fallen short of ‘His
Natural Life’: there is a hard male strain in
Astley’s mind which makes his pathetic scenes
somewhat mechanical : he seems to vision clearly
the result he wishes to attain without being
ever quite able to attain it. Despite all his con-
structive ingenuity a reader remains usually
cold : as a rule, he impresses without moving or
thrilling.
+
Kipling hasn’t improved his position by his latest
verses. One set, ‘ Our Lady of the Snows,’ is mixed and
artificial. The other, for Burne-Jones’s ‘ Vampire,’ is
artificial and dull; and the woman-parasite idea has been
much more forcibly put.
Miss Floral Shaw, the Lond. Times correspondent who
was carefully shown the picturesque side of Q. colored-
labor question, has left that journal’s staff as a result of
her too open espousal of* the cause of Cecil Rhodes, and
has become editor of a Rhodesian paper, the ‘ South
African News.’
Mrs. Ellis Rowan, a Victorian lady who has wandered
over most of Australasia painting wild-flowers, and paint-
ing them admirably, announces a coming book of reminis-
cent sketches. Whether these are drawings or writings is
not quite clear. If the latter, it is to be hoped the author
will include some remarks on the royal ructions in Q.
railway service when some high-up officers who appreci-
ated her charm (she fascinated even Sam Griffith, who
gave her a free rail pass everywhere, and another when
she lost that) permitted her to ride on a Cairns-line
locomotive, in defiance of regulations. The whirlpool of
agitation that followed drew even Commissioner Mathie-
son into its vortex.
Anyone who forms an opinion of Lucy’s journalistic
talents exclusively from his letters to ‘S.M. Herald’ will
form a wrong opinion. In English magazines and
periodipals, where he is kept upon his mettle, Lucy is
often bright and interesting. But he appears to have
welcomed the idea that ‘anything will do for Australia,*
and to use his * Herald ’ letters largely as a vehicle for re-
turning thanks to distinguished persons who have asked
him to dinner.

Homer continues to nod, and ... ‘in Sydney
Public Library there are two ‘ CEuvres completes de
Frantjois Villon,’ both in the printed catalogue ; and
Payne’s English translation is catalogued merely as
Poems of Villon, with nothing to lead one to believe the
translation to be a complete one. In 1878 Payne pub-
lished a limited issue of his translation, and finding a few
years later that the demand was greater than the
supply, he issued another edition, omitting three of the
ballads, and altering or suppressing a few passages in the
body of the Testament. He himself writes, “ These ex-
purgations are, after all, inconsiderable.” A copy of the
new edition is in the Sydney Library.’—Hu Rite.
Judging by some • Argonaut’ extracts from his letters,
Stephen Crane—the ideal war-correspondent—was a
failure in the real Graeco-Turkish field.
Arthur Galton, who came to N.S. Wales as private-
secretary to his uncle, Gov. Duff, is reported as return-
ing to England. Securing the favor of Premier
Dibbs, he was appointed by that politician to write a his-
tory of the province ; but Dibbs left office before the
appointment was confirmed, and Premier Reid cancelled
it. The work was unnecessary, since Government does
all that is required of it by issuing the ‘ Historical
Records ’ which F. M. Bladen is collecting. Galton’s his-
tory was apparently intended to proceed concurrently as
a rhetorical summary of the ‘ Records,’ with appropriate
m ml reflections ; but such a task may very well be left
to private enterprise. In any case, Galton, as a new-
chum of unremarkable capacity, did not seem the best
historian available. He turned to journalism, and in
that sphere appeared as a writer thoughtful and well-
informed, but heavy and unoriginal. And he is under-
stood to have collected materials for an Australian book
or two.
F.: ‘ Talking of literary coincidences—what about
H. Parkes and Robert Browning? P. writes of “The
Patriot ” (“FragmentaryThoughts,” p. 142) —
Fair women cast sweet flowers before his feet;
From all the housetops ’kerchiefs gaily waved . . .
The very flags flew out as wild with glee . . .
A year! and jibe and jeer and savage yell
Salute his ears; and missiles rank and foul
Fall thick about him where the garlands fell.
And R.B. (page 62 of the Is. “Selection”) has “The
Patriot. An Old Story.”
It was roses, roses, roses all the way. . . .
The house-roofs seemed to heave and sway,
The church spires flamed, such flags they had
A year ago this very day. . . .
And you see my harvest, what I reap,
This very day, now a year is run. . . .
And I think, by the feel, my forehead bleeds,
For they fling, whoever has a mind,
Stones at me for my year’s misdeeds.
You see, Browning practically admits the plagiarism by
labelling his alleged effort “An Old Story." ’
Apparently ‘Sea-Power’ Mahan has been attacked in
England for pandering to conventional views. Says
Admiral Colomb: ‘Surely no open-minded reader of
Mahan can doubt his devotion to history because it is
history, and the impossibility of his being diverted from
the pursuit of his quarry by any considerations as to who
will be pleased and who displeas'd.’ Precisely such a
doubt crossed the writer’s mind when glancing at Mahan’s
‘Life of Nelson,’ and noting how he slid oyer the Foley
manoeuvre at Abnukir, the shame of Naples, the Copen-
hagen armistice, and other ticklish points. Whatever
Mahan’s standpoint in regard to the ‘ Sea-Power ’
volumes, could he, with his present reputation and
friendships, tell the English the whole truth about a
popular hero like Nelson —if that truth ran counter to
conventional beliefs and prejudices? And, on the evi-
dence, has he told it?
J.: *An Australian poet complains that his pawnbroker
will insist upon describing him professionally as a
“printer’ on his tickets. Very few tradespeople cor-
rectly gauge the qualities of professional poets, jour-
nalists, reporters, or littbrateuis— mixing them mentally
in tlie disreputable category of “gents as writes for the
papers.” ’
REST.
We are so tired, my heart and I,
Of all things here beneath the Sky;
One only thing would please us best—
Endless, unfathomable rest.
We are so tired; we ask no more
Than just to slip out by Life's door)
And leave behind the noisy rout
And everlasting turn about.
Once it seemed well to run on, too,
With her importunate, fevered crew,
And snatch amid the frantic strife
Some morsel from the board of Life.
But we are tired; at Life's orude hands
We ask no gift she understands ;
But kneel to Him she hates to crave
The absolution of the grave.
Mathilde Blind.
A FANCY.
To J. Brunton Stephens.
The Earth, sore stricken with the Day's disease,
Lies dead, quite dead, beneath the Moon’s gray p a u ;
And sadly, from the dim adjacent stiore,
The Sea laments, and strikes her mournful rocks.
Listen! a tender echo in her train,
Timidly fluttering, like a fairy’s ghost
Upon the heels of some vast Brocken-shape,
Pauses, and strikes mournfully, and pauses even as she . .
It is iny,little child, my infant child,
Hushed in her bed, yet clapping dreamy hands
For father, as they teach her playfulwise.
A fancy seizes me : I think she beats
A plangent lamentation for my soul,
Slain in the stress of too-enthralling Life.
Notes and Queries.
4. — Marsupial Reproduction. — l7/4/97.
As in all mammals, the ovaries of the kangaroo
open into the womb; the only communication
between the womb and the outer world is by the
vagina. After a very short gestation the joey it
born as a little helpless blob of flesh, which the
doe lifts in her lips and places on the teat. The
joey’s mouth is at this stage a sort of sucking
apparatus which grips the teat tightly and sup-
ports the infant. Again, as the milk apparatus
in all mammals consists simply of a mass of secret-
ing glands having no connection .whatever with
any pelvic organ, it follows that the joey con-
ceived in the womb could not reach the teat by
any internal passage, but must travel by the exr
ternal route. Besides, actual births have been
witnessed in the London Zoo., and in the Zoo. at
Frankfort, Germany, and duly placed on record
by the scientists who observed the whole process.
An excellent description of the routine ia
given in Johnson’s Universal Cyclopedia (New
York, 1896). All the larger works on zoology and
comparative anatomy contain more or less full
descriptions of marsupial anatomy, any one of
whioh is sufficient to smash the theory that joey
is ‘born on the teat.’ —X.Z.
[‘ Mulga ’ and others write to similar effect; and a
valued contributor sends (in spirit) specimens of the joey
in uterus and as just born. But it will take a lone time
to stamp out the attractive ‘teat’ delusion.]
21. — Mixed Sex.
On Wilgena Station, N.S.W., there was for
several years a ewe with horns of the same shape
and size as a ram’s, but she differed from ‘ R.
Gildea’s ’ Bogadillon horned ewe in being intensely
masculine in appearance. Her condition was
always good, and her fleeces strong and heavy,
but, like Gildea’s ram-ewe, she was sterile. —X.Z.
22. — The Crow.
A drover of my acquantance, while bringing
cattle down to market, shot a sick bullock. There
being no timber handy to burn it he left the car-
case where it lay, but took the precaution to cub
the brand out and the ear off, and stuff them
down the dead bullock’s mouth to evade detection.
But a crow flew down after he had gone and
picked them out. Then a trooper came along,
and the drover was fined two pounds and costs
for leaving the dead bullock on the road.— Hugh
Harding.
23.— Borak.
I think ‘J.M.’ is right in his suggestion of
aboriginal derivation. Certainly the word was in
very common use 40 years ago, and was then
taken as aboriginal. But ‘ Bale ’ was the pure
negative. ‘Bo-riik’ carried the idea of good-
humored repudiation or denial—something equiva-
lent to our ‘ gammon ! ’ or ‘ no dash fear ! ” or our
still more offensive * Credat Judaeus Apella.’
The word, in this sense, was so familiar to my
own earliest notice that, honestly, the modern
expression, ‘poking bb-rak,’ still grates on my
ear as a new-chum incongruity of phrase, aggra-
vated by mispronunciation.— Tom Collins.
24.— Snaring Possums.
Being now out of that line of business, I can
recommend an excellent way of catching possums
for their skins. You want three or four dozen
small sixpenny mirrors and the same number of
rabbit-traps. At the foot of a likely-looking tree
you place a glass with the trap in front. A
possum is extremely curious, and seeing himself
in the glass—probably thinking that it is another
possum—he will walk up to sniff—and put his
foot in the trap. In this way I have caught hun-
dreds on the Murray. — Tindog. “Dew tell! ”
26. - First books printed in Australia.
The first book printed in South Australia bears-
the following title— ‘ No. 1, 7th William IY. An
Act for the granting of Licences, the regulating
the sale of Wine. Beer, and Spirituous Liquors, for
the Prevention of Drunkenness and the Promotion
of good Order in Public Houses. February 2,1837.
Glenelg. Printed by Authority, by Robert Thomas
and Co., Government Printers.’ The size is demy
Bvo., pp. 16. It was printed on the. Sand Hills
before the town of Adelaide was laid but.
The title of the first book printed in Westralia
is— ‘ A report of the late Trial for Libel, Clarke
versus Maefaul. Compiled by W. N. Clarke,
Solicitor. Fremantle, Western Australia, Sep-
tember, 1835. Printed at the Office of W. T.
Graham,’ The size is 22pp., demy Bvo., with
paper cover.
What are the titles of the first books, booklets,
or pamphlets printed in the other Australasian
provinces ?—G. Gerhard.

Various Verses .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MAID MARGARET.
(From a Soul in Pain to One in Bliss.)
My love, who underneath the sod
Hast lain these twenty years,
The wrong I did thee —(O, my God !) —
May not be purged by tears ;
My fire, that ever rageth,
No cooling stream assuageth.
Maid Margaret! I loved thee well,
And shrined thee in my heart,
Until another cast a sped
That tore us twain apart; —
Too gentle, thou, and tender,
To stand before her splendor !
I made that other one my bride ;
I told thee of my choice;
I bade thee, in my youthful pride,
Look on us and rejoice.
Few fell thy words, but sweetly ;
Thy soft smile vanished fleetly.
As formal friends we parted, love !
Then, ’midst my nuptial bliss,
They told me thou wert called above
To brighter worlds than this.
Bv’n then no voice reproved me
And told me thou hadst loved me.
Long time, wide distance, stand between
Those bygone scenes and now ;
Experience has brought insight keen
To make me know that thou
Wert loving, true, and tender,
In patient self-surrender.
And thou didst go to mouldy Death,
As to a bridegroom’s arms,
Resigned thy sweet and cheerful breath
And all thy budding charms,
Because "of love unswerving
For me, the undeserving ;
While I, a thoughtless, selfish boy,
Who nothing knew of life,
Exulting in my wedded joy,
Plunged in thy heart a knife ;
Yea, deemed the torture kindness,
And slew thee, in my blindness !
For crime of ignorance and youth,
Lord, doom me not to wrath !
The clear, illuming light of truth
Enough of judgment hath ;
Hell holds no greater terror
Than knowledge of my error.
Sweet love, thou art not ’neath the sod
These twenty weary years ;
Thou dwell’st a saint, at home with God,
Above earth’s smiles and tears ;
And Heaven has surely taught thee
More lore than Earth has brought me.
Bride of my Spirit! better part
Of what is best in me !
Though earthly love enchains my heart,
My spirit craves to be
Where thou art, virgin stainless,
In lands where love is painless.
If I could know thou dost forgive
My sin of long ago,
Then would I gladly cease to live,
Nor dread eternal woe.
Saint Margaret ! I pray thee,
Let thoughts of sway thee.
So may I gain that far, bright shore,
When earthly ties are rent,
And thou and she and I once more
May meet and be content;
No fleshly love dividing,
But heavenly love abiding !
Christchurch, M.L.
J. LIDDELL KELLY.
AFTER THE HONEYMOON.
Wake from thy sleep, O husband ! lam here
Who am the soul of her thou callest wife;
Look thou upon me ! Have no shame or fear ;
I am thy chosen, bound to thee for life.
Thou hast the right to look on me unveiled
Of that fair covering of warm white flesh ;
Gaze on me calmly, husband ! unassailed
By glance of eye, or gleam of silken mesh
Of hair, or rose of mouth or cheek—
Look ! I have laid my loveliness aside ;
An ugly naked self I stand, and weak—
Weaker than water—l who am thy bride.
But long ago—when, I remember not—
There was no whiter soul the whole world round,
And I was free as any ; but the lot
Fell unto me in artificial ground.
They weighed me down with iron bonds of rule ;
They bade me bow at Custom’s slightest nod,
Until I learned to love the gilded fool,
And hailed conventionality as god.
They set my feet upon the beaten way,
And bade me wander not to right or left;
“ Thus far, no farther, shall thy footsteps stray.’
Early was I of liberty bereft.
Nature sets bounds ; but Nature I defied,
And Nature hath revenged herself on me;
Fixed are the laws I lightly laid aside—
Poor slave of artificiality !
Time was when I rebelled against my fate,
And would have thrown my fetishes aside,
Cut mine own path, but for my iron’s weight—
And, crushed at birth, my new-born longings
died.
And now X cannot move without those stays
And props : since wish for freedom’s dead
I care not now to tread untrodden ways
Nor have I strength to walk unfettered.
Dost thou still wonder I am weak and small,
Deformed ? diseased beyond physician’s skill ?
Wonder that I had strength to live at all !
Wonder that any life is in me still !
I am thy wife ; flesh of thy flesh —that s well,
Since flesh is flesh and I, if foul, am fair
Soul of thy soul ? Ah, husband ! what if Hell
Were sweeter to thee than thy life-despair !
For thou shalt live, and I shall still endure,
A loathsome thing that hath no member whole,
Until the friction of a mind impure
Create a canker in thy healthy soul.
Wouldst thou rise upwards? I shall drag thee
back , .
To mine own footing on Gehenna s slope ;
And kill thy finer fancies, as the black
And nipping frost destroys the heliotrope.
Pity me—loathe me—still my place is here.
Beside thee, with thee, close to thee for life
Look thou upon me, husband ! have no fear,
I am the soul of her thou madest wife.
M.L.
DORA WILCOX.

SATURDAY, JULY 3, 1897.
Griffith’s Solution.
Samuel Griffith, Chief Justice of Queens-
land, supplies the latest and about the best
among many recent contributions to Federa-
tion literature. As a rule, no one looks for
any definite solution of a difficulty from that
quarter. Much learning and much legal
acumen and a profoundly microscopical habit
of mind combined to make Samuel Griffith
one of the most brilliantly useless among
notable Australian politicians. His insight
enabled him to see 12 different solutions to
every question, 11 of which would escape a
more ordinary and useful mind ; and 24 in-
superable objections to each of them, every
one of which would have missed the eye of
comparatively ignorant, rough and ready
politicians of the Seddon, or Turner, or
Reid type. And, having explained all these
288 matters in a long speech of the utmost
brilliancy, Griffith generally left things as
they were, merely showing in conclusion that
anything which could possibly be done was
just as disastrous as not doing anything at
all. Griffith as a critic was unrivalled in
liis political days, for he could show on good
evidence the utter inadvisability of every-
thing. But as a constructive politician he
was generally a failure.
* * * *
Nevertheless, Griffith offers the best and
boldest and most definite solution of the
great Federal difficulty that has been yet
advanced by any public man in Australia. The
difficulty on which most of the trouble hangs
is the division of assets and liabilities. The
proposed amalgamation of the public debts
fell through because some provinces are
more heavily embarrassed than others, and
some have borrowed at a higher rate than
others. All the railway consolidation schemes
collapsed because the lines don’t all pay at
exactly the same rate, and some provinces
have more railway than others, and each
province reckoned that the only object of
amalgamation was jdo . enable the adjacent
province to steal away its trade. The idea
4 putting the construction of new lines
under Federal control was scouted because
each province was convinced that every other
province would get an undue share of new
line. So far as the present assets and
liabilities were concerned, it was decided to
retain nearly everything under provincial
management and have, as far as possible, no
Federation at all, because each of the five
provinces represented regarded the other
four as thieves banded together to plunder
it. Then when the question of Federal
revenue and expenditure came on, the scheme
of .union and brotherhood further fell to
pieces. The attitude of everybody was that
“ our” province was to be robbed to enrich
the other provinces ; that “we” would con-
tribute more than “our” share of the
revenue and get less than “our” share of
expenditure ; that there was no guarantee
that the whole arrangement was not a plot
to rob “ us,” and much more to the same
effect.
* * * *
The result of it all was an elaborate
arrangement whereby the Federal Govern-
ment, if there ever is one, is to he strictly
tied up as, regards its expenditure to a ridi-
culously small sum; and it is to divide all
surplus revenue according to the cast-iron
system laid .down by the provinces. Also,
its power is so limited as to be hardly worth
mentioning. Yet, despite all this, in'the
only Parliament (that of New South Wales)
where the Convention Bill has been dis-
cussed, nearly everybody has risen to point
out that there is no knowing what .dreadful
things the Federal Government may not do
when once it gets started, and to demand
that it be tied up more securely than ever,
and that it shall have practically no power at
all, because the only way to make quite sure
that it won’t do anything bad is not to let it
do ’anything. Probably the other Parlia-
ments, when they get on to the subject, will
rave in a similar fashion. When every one
of the six States begins with the idea that
Federation means a league of the other five
States against it for purposes of highway
robbery, any kind of parochial foolishness is
possible.
♦ * * *
At a time when almost every other writer
and speaker on the subject is formulating a
new scheme for splitting the last halfpenny
with more deadly accuracy than ever, and is
producing huge masses of figures in defence
of his theory, Samuel Griffith comes out
with the singularly novel suggestion that the
provinces should simply trust the Federal
Government to he as honest as the average,
and drop all the schemes together. Also, he
proposes that instead of being puton an allow-
ance of the meanest and most cheese-paring
description, it should be given the same abso-
lutely free hand as the provincial Gover-
ments enjoy ; and that, instead of being tied
up in all manner of ways lest it should bite
or run away, it should be left as untram-
melled as possible. This idea has a lot to
recommend it. So far, nobody has suc-
ceeded in evolving a satisfactory method of
apportioning the surplus Federal revenue
among the States. The Convention pro-
duced several schemes and talked over several
dozen more, hut none of them really satis-
fied anybody. No one can tell in advance
what the surplus revenue will be, what propor-
tion each State will contribute, what the uni-
form tariff will he, or how many years it will
be before, under a uniform tariff, the revenue
per head of population will become the same
in each state ; therefore no scheme which is
evolved now, when there are absolutely no
data to go upon, can satisfy anybody. There
is not one chance in a thousand that any
scheme which can he formulated now will be
even decently equitable, seeing that it is a
mere groping in the dark.. Chief-J ustice
Griffith proposes to simply start without
any scheme. The Federal Parliament will
be able to ascertain with reasonable accuracy
how much each province yields in revenue
and oosts in expenditure, and being pro-
bably as honest and patriotic as either the
provincial Parliaments or the Convention, it
will be as likely as anybody else to do the
fair thing by everybody. Similarly he pro-
poses that it should be left to work out
the railway and public-debt problems by
itself, because no one can tell under the pre-
sent system what the various railways will be
worth under the entirely new system of
internal Freetrade. Also, no one can tell for
certain to-day what may happen to-morrow or
ten years hence to the public debt or the
public credit or the public finances.
* * * *
The people will create The Federal Parlia-
ment, and its honesty will be a reflex of the
honesty of the nation. If the nation can’t
rely on itself to make a Federal Parliament
which can be trusted with the public money
and the public property, then it must needs
put restrictions upon that body by means of
a Convention, but it naturally follows that it
can’t trust itself to create a Convention
either honest or wise enough to devise the
right kind of restrictions. If it can’t trust
itself to create a Federal Government that
can be allowed to take charge of the railways,
and to make the best use of the public
revenue, and to allot the surplus decently,
then these matters must be left to the
Provincial Governments ; but the same com-
munity can’t trust itself to create Provincial
Governments which will be any more reli-
able. The reason principally assigned for
making cast-iron restrictions about the divi-
sion of the Federal surplus is that otherwise
the provinces might be left in a state of in-
solvency, and have to default on their public
debt, or do something equally dreadful. The
short and obvious way out of that difficulty
is to hand the whole debt problem and all
the unprofitable railways and accumulated
deficits over to the Federal Government with
general instructions to find its way out of the
mess as best it can. Presumably there is a
way out. If there is, the Commonwealth
Legislature, as a gathering of the best politi-
cal intelligence in Australia, is better able to
find it than anybody else. If there is any
public insolvency in view the Federal Legis-
lature, as an institution with broader shoul-
ders than any mere provincial affair can be,
is best able to face the responsibility. All
the local politicians and the Federal Conven-
tion and the various Parliaments have
struggled to no purpose to strike an average
whereby the financial burdens, present and
to come, can be fairly adjusted among the
different provinces according to their circum-
stances, and the shortest way is to deposit
the whole problem outside the capacious
door of the Commonwealth, and leave it
there. If the Federal Parliament can’t solve
the difficulty then the people who create
it are responsible, and as the people are
ultimately responsible in any case this pro-
cedure can’t make things any worse than
they are.
• • * •
Put in the fewest words, Samuel Grif-
fith’s advice, in which The Bulletin
heartily concurs, is that the Federal Legis-
lature should be made as fairly representa-
tive a body as possible, and should be given
a free hand to do the best it can for Aus-
tralia in general. All the restrictions that
can be devised will probably prove useless,
as well as clumsy and incomprehensible. If
the Federal Legislature approves of the
course laid down for it, then that probably
means that it would have followed the same
course itself in any case. If it disapproves,
it will probably find some way of breaking
up the most elaborate agreement that the
Convention and the local Parliaments can de-
vise. In any case, all the restrictions and
compromises which, human foresight can de-
vise in 1897 will almost certainly be antiquated
through mere force of circumstances ten
years, or even five years, later If Federa-
tion had begun in 1887, when Victoria was
still the leading province of Australia, and
Westralia was a howling wilderness and a
mere crown colony, the best financial com-
promise that could have been devised then
would be of very little account now. And
the one that is devised now will probably
look equally ridiculous in 1907, or even long
before that date.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Municipal Grog.
When the two Chafeey irrigation settlements
were started at Mildura (Vic.) and Renmark
(S.A.) it was decided to run them both on
strict-Oold Tea principles. The scheme worked
only moderately well. The settlements yielded
no licensing revenue. They were a trifle
drunker, if possible, than the average. The
drink sold was worse than the average ; when
the retailer was liable to be fined just the same
if he was caught selling good liquor or bad, he
sold the kind that he made most profit out of.
The sly groggeries, in very many cases, com-
bined immorality with liquor-selling ; they had
no license to lose by misconduct, so they were
absolutely reckless in their methods of making
money. They sold liquor at all hours of the
twenty-four, also because they had no license
to lose through being found trading after
hours ; if a dealer is liable to fine for selling at
all he may as well sell for 24 hours a day as
not, and thereby make the most of his time in
between fines. They were largely run by
people of very bad character ; the law may
insist on the keeper of a licensed house being a
person whose record can stand inspection, but
tiie keeper of an unlicensed house can have any
kind of record he pleases. Also their premises
were often foul and unwholesome, for the fine
on the man who runs a dirty sly-grog shop is
just the same as on the man who keeps a clean
one. For several years Mildura and Renmark
struggled with the evil at great expense in
police supervision, and then the latter settle-
ment gave up the hopeless effort. By a special
Act of Parliament an experiment at State—or,
rather, municipal—grog-selling was established
at Renmark. The local pub is managed by an
elected committee, and it yields a profit to the
municipality of between £750 and £lOOO per
annum. The new establishment is clean and
well-conducted, sells good liquor, and has prac-
tically extinguished the local sly-grog industry
in one act. The experiment proves, so far as
it has gone, that State or municipal manage-
ment of the liquor business is possible. It also
proves that it is profitable. Renmark lias a
population of less than 1000 people, and its one
pub. pays handsomely. If Sydney or Melbourne
were run on the same principle of simply sup-
plying as much public-house as is actually re-
quired, they could do with far less than half
the present number of bars, and the natural
result would be that the survivors would pay a
great deal better than at present. And if these
survivors were run, at least so far as the bars
were concerned, under State or municipal con-
trol, and paid about half as well as the Renmark
experiment does, they would yield a profit of
.£150,000 or £200,000 a year. Even the sug-
gestion of such a revenue is calculated to make
a heavily-taxed municipality dream of Paradise.
Federation and Jubilation.
That very tired and indifferent old gentleman,
Premier Nelson, of Queensland, has announced
in England that Federation, according to his
opinion, is “premature.” Every man, as a
matter of course, judges the importance of
anything by the way he feels about it, and
Nelson feels this way about almost everything.
He is blankly indifferent about Federation be-
cause he is blankly indifferent about most other
things. He assumes that the public is indiffer-
ent, partly because he is indifferent, and partly
because he is too lazy to find out whether the
public is indifferent or not, and partly because
he is too tired to care. He assumes that
Federation is premature because nobody takes
any interest in it—he having previously assumed
that nobody takes any interest. The time when
it won’t be premature, in Nelson’s opinion, will
be when he realises that the public has wakened
up to real enthusiasm about it; but as he never
will realise that, being incapable of understand-
ing enthusiasm on the part of anybody about
anything, it will continue to be premature in-
definitely. Nelson, at all events, makes no
pretence of being friendly to the movement,
and the chief hope, so far as Queensland is con-
cerned, is that Tozek, K.C.M.G., is more
in earnest than his leader, and that the
rest of the Ministry may follow Tozer rather
than Nelson. Within the last few weeks
the prospects of the Commonwealth have not
improved. The N.S.W. Assembly has talked
drearily over one clause of the Bill—the clause
stating that the name of this Bill is so-and-so—
for two whole months, and the House seems to
have got completely out of the control of
Brunner and Carruthers and fallen in a heap.
Most of the time there has barely been a
quorum ; the rest of the time there hasn’t been
one Almost all the speakers have professed to
be enthusiastically in favor of Federation in
general, but they are almost all against the
present scheme for some reason which fills
eight pages of Hansard. No two of them
seem to be against it for quite the same reason,
but each one threw more or less cold water on
it in the presence of 20 or 30 other members
who were fast asleep, and a quantity of empty
furniture. The Legislative Council, at time of
writing, hadn’t even started to consider the
matter, and Attorney-General Want —almost
the only man of energy in that House of Slum-
ber—is bitterly and openly hostile. The other
four Parliaments have not yet fairly commenced
to talk about the measure, and unless they all
make better progress than N.S.W. has done up
to date it will ultimately be left abandoned
somewhere on the high-road. The departure
of the Premiers to scramble for titles and free
board-and-lodging in England has probably
done something to bring about this state of
indifference. Nelson’s absence certainly doesn’t
matter much, for Nelson never matters much
in any capacity, and Turner and Kingston
have each left a good substitute behind. But
Forrest’s understudy, Wittenoom, is a nonen-
tity ; and Acting-Premier Brunner has already
fallen to pieces ; and Fysh of Tasmania has
apparently grown indifferent since his financial
schemes were cold-shouldered by the Conven-
tion. And, even if each one of the absent
Premiers had left a better man than himself to
fill his place, their general abandonment of the
measure would create a bad impression. All the
Premiers agreed that Federation was the most
important event in Australia’s history, and then
they all left the event to take care of itself, and
went away to jubilate.
Licensing' Anomalies.
Writes “V.” to The Bulletin :
The absurdity of the N.S.W. licensing law was illus-
trated, a few days ago, when a Sydney brewery was
forced to take proceedings in one of the higher courts for
ejectment against the occupant of a hotel. Before the
amount of a publican’s license-fee can be received by the
Treasury the license-certificate of the previous year must
be produced.. It is hard to see the necessity of this pro-
vision. It would be just as sensible to enact that a land-
lord should not receive rent until he had obtained the
previous receipt. In England and other countries, where
the law is logically administered, the person authorised
by the magistrates is held to be the licensee, and, without
any further bother, pays the fee just as he would pay a
butcher’s bill. In N.S.W., not only is the magistrate’s
consent required, but the old certificate must be produced
before a new one can be issued. This condition makes
the holder of the old license master of the situation ; and
as there is often much friction between the occupant of a
licensed house and the brewers, as well as property-
owners, spirit-merchants, and others who may regard a
license as a valuable lien, some queer complications
result. It is generally found expedient to “ square ” the
old licensee, but when, through greed or revenge, he re-
fuses to listen to reason, the license is liable to forfeiture.
Take the famous Pratt case, for instance. The holder of
the old license retained it in his possession, and the
Treasury would not issue a new one, though Pratt, the
owner, was willing to pay the amount, and the magis-
trates made no objection. The old one having lapsed
through expiration, Pratt then applied for a new license,
but, the previous locai-option poil in that ward having
decided against new licenses, the magistrates refused.
It is ridiculous that important interests should depend on
these farcical polls. Pratt invoked the aid of the higher
Courts, but in vain. He was about to appeal to the
Privy Council, and a special meeting of the N.S.W.
Licensed Victuallers’ Association, reinforced bv brewers,
property-owners and others interested, was held for the
purpose of providing funds, but, instead of travelling to
the other end of the world to make an experiment in law,
Pratt found it cheaper to avail himself of a loophole in
the present Act. Houses exceeding certain dimensions
are independent of the local-option law. Consequently,
when Pratt added a few extra rooms and a wing to the
building, he was able to snap his fingers at the local
optionists, and obtain a conditional license, though, per-
haps, he is not now in'as favorable a position as if he had
obtained a transfer from the old licensee in the usual
way. Parkes and others used to allude to the present
local-option law as a democratic measure. Yet it may
lean heavily on the poor man, who cannot afford to en-
large his premises, while it has no terror for the property-
owner who makes light of the cost of extra bricks and
mortar. Another incongruity is that those interested
in existing hotels are as eager as the temperance party in
voting against new licenses which would interfere with
the present comparative monopoly. This expedient of
increasing the size of the premises was availed of in
another complicated licensing case—that of the Palace
Hotel, Haymarket, Sydqey. It is said that the gaunt bar-
racks, which now pass as hotels in Melbourne suburbs, were
not, as is generally supposed, so much the result of the land-
boom as of the concessions which the Vic. licensing law
also offers to large buildings. This is one of the curses of
the existing Australian laws. Tenants are induced, nay
compelled, to erect hotels beyond their requirements with
the result that they become “ tied ” to the brewer or
mortgaged to the bank. When the contractor’s paint
has worn off, these huge, half-furnished tenements
become an eye-sore, and serve no useful purpose unless
their cheerless ugliness drives the onlooker to drink.
How different the snug hotel one sees in England, where
the licensee can adapt the size of his house to the re-
quirements of his business ! The anomalies of the Aus-
tralian licensing law are so many that it would now be
idle to discuss their reform. The better plan would be to
make a clean sweep. In England a Royal Commission is
at present carrying on an exhaustive inquiry into the
whole subject, and it is generally believed that its labors
will result in a codification of the many existing statutes
and a fresh start in licensing law. When this takes
place, every province in Australia might, for once, advan-
tageously follow the example of England.

Be Patents.
Dear Bulletin,— ln this matter “ C.” puts the
ease of Brisbane patent-agents very well, but
of course, the mere public does not get much
consideration. As a Vic. resident, I know
nothing about Q. administration of patent law;


but it seems to me that “C.’s” attack is very
weak in this section :
Lately a patent was refused on the absurd ground that
“ it would interfere with one of the great industries of the
country.” Did not weaving-looms interfere with a great
English industry ?
If the Q. law upholds this decision—then prima
facie it is thoroughly democratic. It is mere
weakness or worse to sacrifice the people of
to-day on the altars of posterity and a patentee.
Patents “ against public policy ” should not be
granted—nor, on the other hand, should the
average patentee be circumlocuted and black-
mailed. If a patent should be granted, it
should be granted free of cost. But there
should be conditions, one being that the patent
should be worked in the country. I under-
stand that in France a patent is void if
not worked in France within two years,
or if it ceases to be worked for tw'o years.
In Germany a patent is revoked if its owner has
failed to work it there “ to an adequate ex-
tent.” The English provision (section 22 of the
Patents Designs and Trades Marks Act, 1883)
is peculiar :
If on the petition of any person interested it is proved
to the Board of Trade that, by reason of the default of a
patentee to grant licenses on reasonable terms (a) the
patent is not being worked in the United Kingdom; or (b)
that the reasonable requirements of the public with re-
spect to the invention cannot be supplied ;or (c) that
any person is prevented from w'orking or using to the
best advantage an invention of which he is possessed,
the Board may order the patentee to grant licenses on
such terms as to the amount of royalties, security for
payment, or otherwise, as the Board, having regard to
the nature of the invention and the circumstances of the
case, may deem just, and any such order may be en-
forced by mandamus.
A deputation recently waited on the Board of
Trade to ask for an amendment of this section
in as far as the word “ default ” is concerned.
It is held that the foreign patentees are hot in
“default” unless they “refuse”—and they
take good care to neither grant nor refuse.
The deputation represented the calico-
printing, dyeing, bleaching, and chemical in-
dustries, all of which claim to be hampered by
the devices of the foreign patentee who lives
up to his privileges without attending to his
responsibilities, and thus coal-tar dyestuffs, cer-
tain pharmaceutical productions, anti-pyrin,
etc., are kept at combination-prices in England.
I would make patents free, easy and accessible,
but would insist on their being worked in the
country —-without any of the roundabout rigma-
role of the English Act. Further, I would put
a tax on the profits of all inventions, reserving
to the State the right to buy up those inven-
tions at a capitalisation of those profits. The
present patent laws of the world are a menace
to the masses of the people. If some costless
force were invented to-morrow and patented,
there would be such a revolution against the
slave-trade that would then be possible for
the inventor, that all patents would be swept
away. All the homes of civilisation (those
of the patentee and his syndicate excepted)
would suffer in the multiplication of machinery.
This sudden assault on the vested interest of
laborers is not likely to happen, Keely not-
withstanding. The danger is worse than that.
A multitude of patentees are at work, under-
bidding the craftsman and the worker. In
place of two men, they put a machine run by a
small boy. The linotype in Melb. and Sydney
sends scores of comps, to the wallaby-track ;
&c., &c. I look forward to the time when
machinery will do most of the work, and the
people will have all the proceeds to themselves.
But, meanwhile ? Well,- we don’t want to stop
the inventors who are hastening on the Good
Times. But we don’t want all the Good Times
to be left to good old Posterity. So let us get
a rational system of patents right away.—
Gambler.
The telephone tunnels in Pitt-street, Sydney,
which were constructed by day-labor, are,
apparently, more or less of a failure. There is
no evidence, so far, as to the extent of the
failure; the work may be shoddy all through,
or there may be only one or two weak spots in
it, and even they may be greatly exaggerated ;
but apparently something is more or less wrong.
On the strength of the very little that is known,
or rumored, about the work, however, the
aggregate Fat Man has risen with beautiful
unanimity to point out that day-labor is a
failure, and that the only really reliable institu-
tion is the contractor. When the dreadful
revelations were made about the Bare Island
fort, and when the painful story of Sydney
Town Hall foundations came to light, and
during the recent trouble over the Sydney
sewers, the same person sang a different song in
a very different key. These things and many
dozen more proved that certain contractors
were dishonest, or shoddy, or inefficient, or
that the inspection was faulty, but no respect-
able individual pointed to them as evidence
that the whole contracting system was a public
robbery. On the other hand, a single failure,
or alleged failure, under the day-labour system
is held up as evidence that the day-labor
system is a failure all round. If the Sydney
telephone tunnels are as bad as alleged, they
certainly prove that the supervision by the
departmental officers must have been beneath
contempt, and that day-labour, with equally
bad supervision, can be almost as inefficient as
the contract system of which Bare Island fort
is so beautiful a sample. At the same time it
seems to be a good deal cheaper, and it is cer-
tainly less dishonest. The inspector may, and
does, receive tip from the single contractor ; he
can’t very well do so from 50 or 100 men
working on their own, for then the secret would
be too widely spread, and the hat would have
to pass round too conspicuously.
In his professional reverence for the estab-
lished order of things, the R.C. Archbishop of
Melbourne has fired off the jubilee doctrine
(an echo of Card. Moran) that “ any Aus-
tralian who advocates separation from Great
Britain must be either a knave or a dotard.”
The emblem of British rule is a Crown, and in-
asmuch as “cutting the painter ” means purifi-
cation from royal influences, social and politi-
cal, a Republican enthusiast in this country
must be either foolish or knavish. And, since
there are Republicans who can show reasonable
cause for supposing that Australia would not
suffer in public health, strength or moral tone
through separation—would not be more ex-
posed to foreign attack, or less likely to pull
through any trouble of that sort if it came, or
feel any the worse for haying a new and original
national sentiment, instead of a stale, second-
hand article which can never weld the rising
generation into a new brotherhood— The Bul-
letin would like to know where the knavish-
ness of the said Republicans conies in ? Roman
Catholic Carr merely talks through his archie-
piscopal neck the talk of every potentate
whose power, built on superstition, is strength-
ened by human nature’s grovel. He echoes
the parrot-cry of “loyalty” because loyalty to a
despotic form of government is R.C. religion.
Archbishop Carr’s little section of the Church
couldn’t exist apart from His Majesty the
Pope; therefore, any Catholic who talks of
breaking away from any ancient authority is a
damnable heretic, unless he be a pitiable ass.
It is well to enquire into the holy political
utterances of these good prelates of the Church,
and look for the milk in their cocoanuts. An
archbishop, discussing mundane affairs, is
generally accepted by his flock as a critic in
whom there is no tendency to error, and no
wilful blindness to facts His apparent un>
worldliness makes him a formidable enemy to
the cause lie doesn’t approve of. He seems to
stand afar off, and study the subject through an
inspired telescope And yet. all the time, he is
nothing purer and wiser than a red-hot adher-
ent of one monarch, preaching general devotion
to monarchies for private business reasons,
One of the gravest offences, and rightly so, that
can be committed under the new Vic. Factories
Act is for an employer to discharge a workman
on the latter giving evidence against him in a
court of law. No charge of the sort has yet
been laid in the province, though- a corres-
pondence which has been going on in the local
Herald certainly indicates that one prosecu-
tion should have taken place, and causes
people to wonder why none has happened so
far. A few weeks back, a fashionable restau-
rateur was fined for overworking his waitresses,
and six of the girls were subpoenaed by the
prosecution to give evidence as to their hours.
They told the truth, but, as is usual in such
cases, did their best for defendant by asserting
that they were as wpll treated as they ever
wished to be, and they had never worked in such
an easy place before. The girls left the court,
and before another week ended they had all
been told to find other places. This fact was
published in the Herald, and instantly pro-
voked a reply from the defendant stating that
two of the girls had. been dismissed before the
prosecution took place, and that the remaining
four had resigned out of sympathy. Next
evening a letter came from one of the waitresses
denying this, and averring that, immediately
they returned from court on the day of the
case, defendant told the result to her husband,
who cried loudly, “Then clear the lot of them
out of this.” A further denial closed the argu-
ment, but surely, in the face of such contra-
dictory evidence, the case is one worthy of
being investigated.
A New Reading of the N S. Wales
Defamation Act.
The decision of Mr. Justice Simpson in the case
of Allen v. Willis came as a surprise and a shock
to the legal profession. Ninety-nine lawyers out
of every hundred in N.N.W. would, a fortnight
ago, have given it as their opinion that, under the
Defamation Act, the printing-plant with which a
libel had been printed was, in the event of a ver-
dict being obtained against the editor or proprie-
tor, liable to execution, no matter into whose
possession it passed after the publication of the
libellous matter. In fact, that the liability to
seizure was an encumbrance on the title to the
plant, and that any purchaserboughtsubjecttothis
liability. The Bulletin, indeed, has known more
than one case where the purchaser, under such
circumstances, “caved in,” thinking it a waste of
money to test the question. This view is now held
to be erroneous; it is not the law, and Judge
Simpson has decided that it never was. In 1895
one Allen obtained a verdict for libel against
Truth newspaper, then owned by Messrs.
Willis, Norton, and others. For two years
the sheriff’s officers were kept at bay ; but
at last they succeeded in executing the
writ of ii. fa., and took possession of Truth’s
printing-plant. Plaintiff, however, was not yet
cpiite out of the wood, for Mr. John Norton put
in a claim to the goods levied on, and the sheriff
interpleaded. The matter came on for hearing
before Judge Simpson, and Norton proved that,
since the date of the verdict, he had become sole
owner of the property levied on ; Allen neverthe-
less contended that the plant was liable to execu-
tion in the hands of anybody, and based his
claim on the 13th section of the Defamation Act,
which provides
That whenever any person shall be convicted either in a
civil or criminal proceeding- of printing and publishing a
defamatory libel, the plaintiff, or prosecutor, in whose
favor judgment shall have been given, shall be at liberty
under his writ of execution to levy the costs damages . .
out of the whole of the type, presses, or printingmaterials
whatsoever belonging to the person whose types, presses,
or printing materials, or any part thereof, may have been
used in printing such defamatory article, as well as out
of the property of the defendant on the record.
At first blush these words seem to support the
plaintiff’s contention; but a closer examination
will show that they do not. To put the matter
briefly, the argument is that the words, “ belong-
ing to the person whose types, &c., have been used
in printing,” mean and should be read as “belong-
ing to any person,” &c. The alteration of the one
word gives an entirely different meaning to the
phrase. No authority was cited for thus altering
the plain words and meaning of an Act of Parlia-
ment, and such a procedure would be repugnant
to every principle governing the construction of
statutes. Whether Parliament intended to use
“any ” in lieu of “ the ” is a question that cannot
now be answered : the intention of Parliament,
for judicial purposes, can only be shown by
the language used, though it is probable that
the good old Tory crowd which passed the De-
famation Act did mean to make the path of the
newspaper proprietor as stony as possible. Judge
Simpson decided that, as the plant now belongs
to Mr. Norton, it cannot be seized, and that the
Act refers only to the person whose types, etc.,
were used in printing the libel—that is, to the
person owning at the time of the printing—and
not to a subsequent purchaser. No appeal lies to
the lull Court from the Judge’s decision ; but, in
this, case, that seems a small matter, for there can
be little doubt that his opinion is good law This
decision ought to do something towards stopping
speculative libel actions against newspapers, for
hitherto the “ plant” has always been considered
the very “safest asset” out of which to realise a
possible verdict and the inevitable costs.
The Story of Thomas Suffield.
The Bulletin has received this letter
Ripon Rural District Council.
0. P. P. Edmundson, Council Offices,
Solicitor, Clerk. Ripon, Yorkshire,
March 20th, 1897.
Dear Sir, —I have seen in the local press some verses
(taken from The Bulletin) said to have been written by
Thomas Suffield, who is (so it is said) undergoing a sen-
tence of 10 years’ penal servitude for a (supposed) criminal
assault on a servant girl. Some of Suffield’s old school-
fellows in Wensleydale (of whom I am one) are very
anxious to know the full particulars of this unfortunate
affair, and also in what way they can most effectually
give him a helping hand.
VVe knew Suffield for many years as a boy and man, and
we know that he was perfectly incapable of such an act as
that for which he was convicted, and nothing- short of the
most convincing evidence will lead us to suppose that
one who was always noted for his chivalry and gentle
behaviour to women could in so short a period of time
have so degenerated as to be guilty of such a crime.
I thought poor Suffield was dead years ago, and it was
quite a revelation to us to find he was still living. A reply
will greatly oblige.
Yours, faithfully,
0= F. P. Edmundson.
The Editor of The Bulletin,
Sydney, New South Wales, Australia.
And in response The Bulletin relates this story.
*.* * *
At a quarter to eleven in the morning of Sun-
day, 21st Dec., 1890, a young woman passed out
of the residence of Thomas Croudace, J.P., near
Lambton, N.S.W., where she “was employed as
housemaid. Her name was Ann Chambers. She
was 22 years of age, and is described as being of
medium height for a woman, strongly-built and
well-nourished, but of a rather coarse, strumous
constitution, with dull eyes and sallow complexion.
Her intelligence seems to have been sluggish, and
she had the docility of disposition implied in the
phrase ‘‘no will of her own.” Of characteristic
feminine type, in fact —very affectable, somewhat
sheepish, and attractive to many domineering
males Her reputation was excellent.
Ann Chambers walked out of the grounds sur-
rounding Mr. Croudace’s house and turned her
face towards Lambton township. She was going
to visit her mother, a miner’s wife living at Hamil-
ton, another township reached by tram from
Lambton. The family were English, and had not
been long in Australia. Ann Chambers had been
employed at Croudace’s for 17 weeks.
A few yards down the Lambton road, Ann
Chambers turned into a by-path which shortened
the distance by cutting off a corner. Within the
ground contained between the path and the road
was a cottage occupied by Croudace’s gardener
and his family. With them boarded Thomas Suf-
field, driver of a pumping-engine at the neigh-
boring Lambton colliery, of which Croudace was
manager.
Thomas Suffield was then 32 years of age—a
well-built, good-looking fellow, liked and esteemed
by all who knew him. He was born in Wensley-
dale, Yorkshire, England. During his youth his
family was well-to-do, and he was brought up re-
spectably and received a good education. It is
stated that he has a wife and child in England in
comfortable circumstances ; that his father having
been ruined by speculation Suffield was left depen-
dent on his wife, and quarrels followed ; and that,
being taxed with living on her bounty, he separated
from her and came to Australia some time in the
eighties. For a while he resided at Maitland, 1
where he was caretaker of the local cricket ground;
He came to Lambton with a surveying party,
which broke up owing him some .£5O or £6O for
wages He had been living with the Warrens
about five months.
* * * *
As Ann Chambers was passing aiong the path
by the cottage she looked up and saw upon the
verandah, a few yards away, the gardener’s
wife and eldest daughter, and Suffield. She did
not stop, but walked quickly on to catch the tram.
She did not know Suffield, had never spoken to
him, and that was the second time she had seen
him.
Suffieid had his dinner, and in the afternoon went
for a walk in Lambton Park. He returned shortly
before 8 o’clock, had his tea, changed his walking
clothes to white moleskin trousers and blue shirt,
and lay down in his bedroom before going on a 16-
hour shift at the pit. The gardener went to bed at
8.30. At 9.30, their usual bed-time, his wife
bathed two younger children, a girl of six and a
boy of two years, and the little boy went into
Suffield’s room to say good-night, Suffield was
lying on his bed, reading by candlelight, and the
boy remained about ten minutes playing with him.
An elder sister then went to the door and called,
the boy to go to bed, and he came soon after-
wards. It was then nearly ten o’clock.
The gardener’s wife and daughter stayed up till
a quarter to twelve, reading. Suffield had a bad
cold, and they heard him coughing at intervals.
At twenty minutes to eleven the wife, think-
ing he was asleep, went to call him to go to the
pit, as she frequently did by his desire at various
times, since he had no stated hour of departure.
His door was open, and he was still reading, so
she did not disturb him. Just before going to
bed she went to call him again. He was sound
asleep, and she had to knock several times before
he woke and thanked her. Shortly after twelve
Suffield went to his work at the pit, and did not
return till just before tea-time on the same day
(Monday),
There was a large window in Suffield’s room,
but the lower sftsh was kept closed because a
snake had got into the room a short time previ-
ously ; and, as one of the pulley-cords was
broken, this sash could not be raised without a
considerable grating noise—easily audible in the
next room where the gardener’s wife and daughter
sat. Further, Suffield’s boots were not in his
room, and he could not leave the room, by the
door without being seen.
* » » *
Nevertheless, Ann Chambers declared that at
about ten o’clock she was criminally assaulted, in
front of Croudace’s house, by Thomas Suffield,
the man whom she had twice seen, had never
spoken to, and who, according to the sworn testi-
mony of two independent witnesses, was at that
time lying on his bed with his boots off, between a
quarter and half-a-mile away.
Ann Chambers stated that she saw her mother,
returned to Lambton, and walked thence to Crou-
dace’s. The distance is about a mile, by a lonely
bush road, and between Lambton and Croudace’s
there was only one cottage beside the gardener’s.
When she reached Croudace’s gate she saw Suf-
field sitting upon it. He said “ Good-night ” ; she
did not reply, but passed through the small gate
adjoining and walked briskly towards the house.
When she had gone to a spot about 350 yards
from the gate and about 35 yards from the heuse,
he knocked her down and violently assaulted her.
Ihis was between 10 o’clock and 10 minutes past
10. Ann Chambers screamed, and Mr. Croudace,
who had just got into bed, heard her and
diately put his head oitt of the window to listen.
His bedroom-windows were always left open,
unless it was a wet night. He heard a second
scream, and at once coo'-eed clearly and loudly.
The young woman screamed again, and Mi\
Croudace coo-eed once more, shouted “I am
coming,” put on his trousers and slippers, and
rushed downstairs. His son Frank had also heard
the screaming, and came down with him. They
ran across the grass plot which extends for some
30 yards in front of the house, vaulted the low
picket fence, and found Ann Chambers lying
crouched up a few feet away, much frightened and
upset, but quite conscious and able to answer
questions intelligently.
* * * *
From Lambton to Croudace’s, according to tho
evidence of Thomas Croudace, J.P., at Suffield’s
trial, is “ about a mile ” —say, 20 minutes’ walk
for a woman—much of the way uphill.
Ann Chambers, in her evidence at the polioe-
court, swore:—
I reached Lambton about 20 minutes to 10 ; I got out at
the tram terminus at Lambton and went to Julia Scott’s
aunt, accompanied by Julia Scott, who was with me from
Wallsend. I only remained there for about five minutes.
We came out; left for Croudace’s; Julia Scott accom-
panied me as far as Croudace-street; she bade me good-
night, and I went on by myself. Mr. Croudace’s place
from there (Croudace-street) is about a mile.
Say five minutes to reach Julia Scott’s aunt,
five minutes there, five minutes to Croudace-
street, and 20 minutes home—and Ann Chambers
reached Croudace’s at 10.15 p.m.
Julia Scott, at the police-court—
The tram reached Lambton about 20 minutes past 9 . .
we were at Miss Scott’s about 10 minutes, and then went
to Croudace-street, where Ann Chambers left me
Say 5 minutes to Julia Scott’s aunt, 10 min.
there, 5 min. to Croudace-street, and 20 min.
home—and Ann Chambers reached Croudace’s at
10 o’clock.
James Campbell, at the police-court—
I passed Mr. Croudace’s place, going towards Lambton,
at about 15 or 10 minutes to 10. I met a female at the
junctioh of New Lambton and main Charleston roads ; I
was coming towards the town and the female was going
towards Croudace’s ; no one was with her ; she was going
at a middling pace. ... I got to my place at 5 minutes
to 10 by my time; I think I would cover the distance
from where I met the girl to my place in 6 minutes, and
certainly under 10 minutes.
Say, roundly, he passed Croudace’s at 9.45 ; met
Ann Chambers, 9.50 ; reached home, 9.55. Then
Ann Chambers reached Croudace’s at 9.55 p.m.
Ann Chambers at the trial, presumably after
learning J ulia Scott’s and J nines Campbell’s evi-
dence at the police-court—
It was about 20 minutes to 10 when I parted from Julia.
(Not when she “reached Lambton,” this time.)
So Ann Chambers reached Croudace's at 10 o’clock.
Thomas Croudace, J.P., said at the trial that
he heard Ann Chambers scream “ shortly after he
went to bed at 10 o’clock” —“and as I had just
got into bed,” he said at the police-court.
Frank Croudace, at the police-court, heard the
scream “about 10 minutes past 10.”
Say they took 5 to 10 minutes to get downstairs
and 5 minutes to carry her in. Croudace, J.P.,
said she fainted while they were carrying her,
and was unconscious for fully 20 or 30 minutes;
and when she came round, says Frank (p.c. evi-
dence), I went for Dr. Stapleton, who was called
(p.c. evidence) “about 11.”

Attention is directed to the remarkable conduct
of Thomas Suffield,
Who (the Crown suggested) kept a mistress,
Who (as he says himself) could have gratified his
passions in Lambton or Newcastle any night, of
the week without risk, with comparative comfort,
and with some reciprocity,
And who, nevertheless, being a man of the
highest character and high intelligence, risked
his liberty and his life to brutally assault,
Not on the mile-long lonely bush road,
Not near the gate where he met her.
But 35 yards from her employer’s house,
Almost under her employer’s open windows,
Just after her employer’s bedroom-candle had
been blown out at 10 o’clock at night,
A woman whom he had previously seen once in
his life, that morning,
Whom he had permitted to clearly identify him
as he sat on the gate
Continuing his assault after her screams had been
answered from the house,
Completing it and vanquishing the resistance
of a sturdy woman of 22 in less than 10 minutes, -
with coo-ees coming from the bedroom windows
and a man hanging over the balcony close by—
Then decamping and leaving no trace of his
presence,
Entering the bedroom he had left—"which two
women swear he could not leave or enter without
their knowledge, and did not leave or enter,
Walking or running, in order to accomplish all
this, threequarters-of-a-mile or more without his
boots on,
Wearing clean white moleskin trousers and
clean blue shirt, and not getting upon them one
speck or spot or stain, though the girl was stained
and bleeding terribly,
Not getting one scratch or mark or bruise, in
this terrible ten-minutes’ struggle with the girl,
who “ fought like a tigress for her honor’s sake,”
and was bruised all over,
And then turning out to do a 16-hours wrestle .
with a Tangye pump on top of everything i ! !

file a bill in such a case, and a jury to convict in it,
and a Judge to approve of the conviction, and a
series of , Ministers of Justice to support it and
burke enquiry regarding it—and Suffield went
to rot in gaol for 10 years with hard labor, where-
of he has rotted six.
