Miss Elsie Adair’s modest little vaudeville
show duly _ broke out at Sydney Criterion last
week and is still there. The most distinct im-
pression it leaves on the mind is that Miss Elsie
is a dashed nice girl—in fact she is the kind of
girl who cannot be described by any other adjec-
tive in the language. The stage variety lady feels
bound to smile upon her audience at all times ;
too often the smile consists of a tired, perfunctory
dragging of the mouth towards the ears, but in
Miss Adair’s case it looks bright and spontaneous,
and she creates an impression that she is bubbling
over with happiness and thoroughly enjoying her-
self. If she is really suffering anguish from her
corn, or is a world-weary martyr with a deformed
and malignant toe-nail, and that bright smile is
only hollow pretence covering a broken heart,
then Sapphira wasn’t a circumstance to Elsie
Adair. As a dancer the Amurrikan lady is neat
and graceful, and she lends a new aspect to the
weary skirt-dance when she makes her drapery
fly up to the ceiling in the form of a lily. Five
separate dances are set down against Elsie
Adair’s name on the programme, but one of them
—on the opening night, at least—was performed
by Miss Vivienne liagmar, who doesn’t seem
to get the credit she deserves. The show
Btarts with a small idyll about a little
girl and a burglar and a moral parent. The little
girl and her house-breaker deserve great credit,
but when the malefactor and the moral parent
start repenting together the tale grows somewhat
maudlin. The plain burglar getting away with
the spoons is understandable, but the contrite
burglar moved to tears by a child in her night-
arouses a certain amount of suspicion. The
remainder of the programme is reasonably good,
but not absolutely staggering, variety business,
including a song about a lost child, breathed by
some person invisible, and illustrated by limelight
views of a very small girl being found in the street
by an enormous policeman. It isn’t a very large
programme to fill up an entire evening, but it
manages to get through all right.
** * *
The Potter-Bellew season at Her Majesty’s
is already a good deal more than half-through, so
it is wasted energy to go into detail about it. The
most notable, new feature about this latest version
of “ Borneo ” was Cecil Ward’s Mercutio, a cheer-
ful individual who went down in good spirits
before an overwhelming Cyril Keightley as Tybalt.
Mrs. Potter no novel impressions be-
hind her. She is the same marvellous being
as of old, with the same capacity for rising
at one moment almost to greatness and then
dropping into shrill, eccentric mediocrity and
writhing with much jewellery on. No one knows
exactly what Mrs. Brown-Potter will do next ;
she is an original person wriggling over much fur-
niture and spreading two white arms to the four
winds of Heaven, wherefore her audience remem-
bers her afterwards as Mrs. Pale Totter. Yet,
even in her pallid totter, there are traces of a
capacity for better things. The season ends on
Friday,night with a final performance of “ Fran-
cillon,” (“La Tosca is put on for an after-
noon show the same day), and on Saturday
Paul ton’s co. opens in “A Night Off.” The
new co. arrives with a satisfactory refer-
ence from its last place in Melbourne to the
effect that it is clean, honest and not given
to. drink, understands its work thoroughly, and
can be trusted alone with the cold meat any time,
also that it invariably sweeps under the bed. The
new co. will be received with heart-felt gratitude.
With Mrs. Potter reviving old Shakespeare at
Her Majesty’s, and Farley reviving old operas at
the Opera House, and Robert Henry resurrecting
old horrors at the Standard, and the Broughs just
finished calling up old familiar comedies at the
Cri., there has been almost too much revival in
Sydney of late.
** # *
“ II Trovatore ” is doing a turn at Sydney
Opera House this week, and is, so far, decidedly
the best production of the Farley season. The
chorus has either been streng hened, or else it
isn’t so low-spirited as it was and contrives to look
more numerous, and is now a strong and capable
combination. The lady with the exceedingly thin
legs, alluded to a few weeks back, has either
padded them, or boiled herself down, or some-
thing ; anyhow, her scragginess hasn’t caught the
writer’s eye for a good while now. The lady with
the amazingly stout legs is also less prominent,
and consequently things don’t run to such sudden
extremes as they did. Some very good scenery
appears in “ II Trovatore,” and the co.—especially
Misses Glendenning and Maud Lita and Edward
Farley—does very good work all through. Farley,
dressed in red and purple millinery, rages through
the opera in deep bass and with a drawn sword,
and is always on the point of impaling somebody,
only a number of men with impossible halberds
generally get in the road. A halberd is a long
thing with a little blunt spike on top, and evi-
dently the bearer stood about ten feet off his enemy
and drove the spike in the top of his head, and
then ran round him to enlarge the wound till it
led to fatal results. Or else he entangled the spike
in his enemy’s clothes and shook him to death.
** * *
The breaking of many dishes goes on
nightly at Sydney Royal, where Maggie Moore
still raises Cain on the premises of Uncle Harris.
Further consideration only makes “Johanna”
more unfathomable than it was before, and all the
people who prove in the end to be the other
people’s relations by a former marriage who were
supposed to be dead, grow more shadowy and
tangled, like a confused boa-constrictor in the
twilight. This paper accordingly gives the matter
up as a problem that is past explanation. Busi-
ness keeps remarkably pood at the Royal, so far.
Some old-established whisky advertisements, how-
ever, have vanished off the drop-scene and been
replaced by announcements concerning some-
body’s Check Pills for Striped People, and some-
body else*B stuff in a bottle which is supposed to
be good for a sinking sensation at the pit of the
stomach.
# % -* *
Bellman and Lottie Moore have joined the
Tivoli co., the latter being the lady whose very
neat somersault aroused commendation some time
ago at the Palace. In those days Miss Moore’s
two little green shoes were wont to fly over her
little brown head in a tantalising fashion, dis-
playing about one inch of stocking and a quarter-
inch of white skirt, and though many people went
there regularly in hopes of seeing a little more
skirt, the event never came off. Therefore, it is
just possible that she won’t be so very much of an
attraction after all; when the public realises that
nothing is going to happen while the lady’s feet
are spinning over her back hair, where is the joy
of it? The Selbinis leave this week, and the
Avolos, described as horizontal bar and perch per-
formers, take their places on Saturday.
Hf * * *
James MacMahon has laid in a number of
war subjects at his Cinematographe in Pitt-street,
Sydney. One of them concerns a battle between
Spaniards and Cuban insurrectionists, and others
show some exciting scenes in the recent unplea-
santness between the Ottomans and Greeks.
These latter tend to confirm the opinion that the
sons of Leonidas would have been wiser if they
had. undermined their enemies with their more
familiar weapon, the 12 unripe bananas in
a bag for 4d., or the two sodden pieces
of fried jew-fish in brown paper for 6d.,
instead of taking .to the sword. The Aus-
tralian in general is cordially recommended to
study these views. Many of him has a vague
craving for war through seeing Major Neild in his
uniform ; and the fact that it is a dirty, and tat-
tered, and gory thing, and has no briiliantine on
its hair at all, can’t be too firmly impressed on his
mind.
** * *
Melbourne Princess’s is in a state of pre-
paration for “The Sign of the Cross.” Paulton
has proceeded on tour with his dry humor, which
will be none the worse for a little damping at the
outset. George Lauri took a farewell benefit, and
made a final appearance on Tuesday. To-night
(Thursday), the sassiety amateurs are producing
themselves in “Lelamine,” an opera that has
fairly earned, oblivion, for the last time, and if
theyeverdo .it again, The Bulletin will smack
them hard, in a manner of speaking. “ The
Sign of the. Cross” is advertised to set up its
Early Christian influence this Saturday (July 3rd).
Various religious denominations are understood to
lie awaiting the soulful spectacle with tears in
their eyes, whilst the unconverted classes likewise
look forward hopefully to an entertainment that
unites the Nonconformist conscience, the Salva-
tion Army, and Mossy Isaacs in the bonds of
brotherhood.
** * *
“The Hermit’s Bell,” a new romantic
military opera, in three acts, will be staged at the
Bijou three times next week. The Victorian
Operatic Society—-having no connection with the
. Lelamine ” faction, or the other Society which
is pretty good in “The Gondoliers”—takes this
particular charity scheme in hand. Therefore the
performance may count upon the patronage of the
two rival companies, who will certainly come
along and snort at it. Melbourne’s operatic
amateurism is just now an interesting study.
** * *
At the Opera House latest arrivals are
Allan and Hart, and Miss Myers, a nice young
soprano lady from the W. and M. company. . Phil
Newbury arrived early last week, since when the
average all tenors have mostly gone into retire-
ment for the purpose of tearing their hair.
** * *
a The one remarkable feature in a Jubilee
fete, at Melb. Exhibition building, was the broad
expanse of disgustingly dirty floor. The Exhibi-
tion building is a place where holiday mobs
assemble once in a way, and the assemblage is
usually so large, and so addicted to squeezing
together all the time, that nobody can see any
floor worth mentioning. Last Saturday the fete
happened to be a comparative failure, and the
floor was much in evidence. The dirt from half-a-
million boot-soles has been trodden into the boards;
a thousand expectoration stains, ancient and
modern can be counted from any standpoint; and
the battlefield is strewn with unpleasant-looking
bits of lf the Exhibition trustees have a
proper consideration for public health and a whole-
some objection to fleas, they may as well send in a
charwoman and a ton of soap.
** * *
Planquette’s pretty setting of “Rip Van
Winkle ” was revived at Melbourne Alexandra on
Saturday night. Two weeks of “The Grand
Duchess had called attention to Howard Ver-
non’s valour in attempting to work up a business
at the fatal spot aforesaid, so the audience on
Saturday covered more space than usual. A
manager who can get the Alexandra half filled is
probably on the road to success. Still, the road is
long, and the weary traveller spares himself a lot
of trouble if he stops early and returns home by
the woods. Rip Van Winkle ” was never a first-
favorite opera m Australia. The story is popular,
in its old, languid way, and the music harmonises
with the story, but th 're isn’t quite enough
music to keep thii gs lively, or sufficient of
a heroine to gi\e the prim a - donna a
fair chance. Devoid of a heroine, and
not at all comic in character, ‘ * Rip ” is an
opera that needs a clever performance all round.
Vernon’s company includes two damsels who have
no pretentions to play principal parts. One of
them appeared as Hans in the last act, and the
orchestra immediately fell into a state of disorgani-
sation, as though it had seen a ghost. These
defects, are inexcusable seeing that the chorus,
which is altogether excellent, could furnish capable
young artists in place of the novices. The chorus
is marked W. and M. on most of the links,
and the mountain scenery makes a respect-
able background, also Miss Fannie Liddiard
sings and plays in two capacities for all
they are worth. Howard Vernon’s performance
of Rip tills the stage, although his singing voice is
in tatters, and Frank Gromann, said to be a mere
beginner as an actor, is, in that case, about the
cleverest beginner The Bulletin can call to mind.
He takes two character-parts capitally. Little
Gus Gregory is an eerie goblin, and Miss Alice
Mitchell puts in a dainty little dance.
** * *
The last nights of “A Life of Pleasure”
are announced at the Royal, but Bland Holt’s
large, smoky drama shows no particular signs of
wanting to take itself off yet awhile.
At Poverty Point .
Alice Dunning Lingard’s death is cabled. She was just
about the handsomest woman ever seen on the Australian
stage. She was a charming actress, and narrowly missed
being a great one ; also, she was as good as she looked.
An Australian lady writing from London to Tiib Bulle-
tin “ To-day, May 19th, saw the final exit of ‘ Beauty
Charley,’ otherwise Edward Charles Rylev. He was
playing in ‘The Yashmak,’ at the Shaftesbury, some
three weeks ago, when he broke a blood-vessel, and
death followed. The first part of the burial-service
was held at the quaint historic Chapel Royal in the
Savoy, which was completely filled by professionals who
came to pay their last respects. The service was simple
and short, and the address from the chaplain quite a
blending of the pulpit and the stage, for he acknowledged
‘as a man and a clergyman ’ that the work done in Lon-
don by the theatres was ‘ noble.’ A little lad with a voice
like a silver-flute sang ‘O Rest in the Lord’ most ex-
quisitely, and then the chaplain and choir conducted the
coffin down the church and through the curious little
churchyard to the gate, Chopin’s funeral march being
played on the organ. In the special book with the burial-
service, which was given to each, ‘ln Memoriain of
Edward Charles Ryley,’ his age was wrongly stated—he
was only 39, instead of 50 as therein recorded. Among the
ladies present, the best-known to Australians was Miss
Emily Soldene. Elton was there, his face grave for once.
Likewise Joseph Tapley and Lonnen, Musgrove, Lionel
Brough, George Moore, S. Coleman, Sydney Brough,
Captain Dorrien, John Le Hay and J. A. Harrison, secre-

Dear Bulletin, —The ways of theatrical management
often unreasonable. Howard Vernon and Fanny
Liddiard started a season of comic opera at Melbourne
Alexandra the other Saturday, when, owing to some
accident. The Grand Duchess” was sent off with an
apparently poverty-stricken advt. Therefore, the theatre-
gomg public, who note preliminary advts. and are influ-
enced by them, naturally assumed that this was to be one
more addition to the list of rough, contemptible produc-
tions at the same cheerless house. “ The Grand Duchess ”
proved to be a very acceptable entertainment, and a suc-
cession of small audiences - composed mainly of inveterate
theatre-goers who rank as critics among their friends—
gave it a good word. Having expected little, they were
more than pleased with what they got. But the manage-
ment wouldn t wait for proof of the commercial doctrine
that merit, in any line of article, must make some
headway. Favorable comment on “The Grand Duchess”
was only beginning to travel around when a chan»-e of
bill was announced, whereupon the public at large would
feel confirmed in their assumption that the Alexandra
opera season was a hopeless failure. Are the people who
stay away from a first show because they fancy it isn’t
£?. r J h v i lSlt ‘u e y Jj° 1)6 fctc hed by a rapid change of
bill. Not much. They are rather confirmed in their
false assumption.—Melbourne.

wording of a telegram that passed over the N.S.W tele-
graph-wires, the other day, from a country Mayor to a
Sydney address. It subsequently transpired that, this

Rokitansky, the famous Viennese pathologist, had two
sons —one a medico, the other a distinguished lyric singer.
The Prof., being asked what professions his sons adopted,
replied—“ One heals and the other howls.”

Archer, Editor Massingham, and manager Sutro, prices
for four performances will be 325. for four stall seats, 255.
for four d.c. seats, and 10s. a time for an upper box.

During the last bull-fight season (six months long)there
W'ere 478 bull-fights in Spain, at which Were killed 1218
bulls, valued at £60,000, and 5730 horses, valued at
£40,000. The most famous matador, Gueritta, in 68
fights killed 174 bulls, and received for his six months’
butchering £10,200. The U.S. Consul at Barcelona
declares that, instead of slumping, bull-fights are on the
up-grade in popular favor.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The stupendous Ernest Terah Hooley’s scheme
for buying 1 up all the meat-freezing works in Australasia
and running them as one British syndicate is about the
shadiest device of the decade. Meat-freezing is the third
or fourth of our industries in point of importance ; it
represents £2,500,000 a year income, or rather more ; and
the idea of selling this bodily to J. Bull is alarming.
Nearly everything else is already sold or mortgaged to
Bull, and to sell everything to Bull seems rash, unless this
country proposes to be ultimately a nation of swagmen,
all humping a dingy drum towards the setting sun.
Hooley proposes to buy up the present companies, giving
them the full value of their assets and a bonus equal to
the last seven years’ dividends. As Hooley is thus willing
to give a lot more than the businesses are worth just now,
and as he proposes to take a huge bonus out of the thing
himself, and as there will be larger expenses through a
great and gorgeous London management and board
having to be maintained, and interest having to be paid
on a vast mass of debentures, the meat-freezing busi-
nesses, under the Hoolej' management, would have to pay
dividends on about three times their present capital—in
other words, they would have to earn at least three times
as much profit as they do now to pay as well as they do
now—-which is none too well.
There are just two ways of doing this. One is
to make the Britisher pay more for the meat. The other
is to make the Australian take less—a great deal less—for
the cattle. Hooley says that he is going to do the former,
and The Bulletin, for one, flatly disbelieves him. The
Hooley syndicate won’t have a monopoly of the British
meat-market; it will have to compete with the Yankee,
the Argentine, and others ; therefore, it can’t raise prices
as it pleases, or its competitors will cut it out and
damage its trade, and wreck the Hooley enter-
prise. On the other hand, the Hooley crowd could
cut down the prices paid to the Australasian pas-
toralist almost indefinitely. He must sell to the
freezing works, or, so far as concerns a very great part of
his output, not sell at all; and, when the Hooley enter-
prise owned every freezing works in the country, and con-
trolled all, or nearly all, the available shipping, and the
freezing accommodation at the other end, and the market
arrangements, and had a lien on the retailers—well,
there would be very little for it save to accept almost
any price the Hooley enterprise liked to offer.
Hooley’s cordial assurance that he intends making
the required profit by raising the price to the British
consumer, and his implied assurance that, even with
such great power in his hands, he wouldn’t dream
of cutting down prices to the producer, are too thin ; for-
tunes like Hooley’s are not made by showing needless
consideration to anybody. The Hooley freezing syndicate
would sweat the pastoralist down to the lowest point,
and make the pastoralist sweat everybody connected with
him ; and it would be the last straw on certain staggery
banks which are loaded down with unsaleable station pro-
perties ; and Queensland and M.L.— the two great meat-
raising provinces—would be practically Hooley, Limited,
* * » •
A wail comes "by cable from Westralia. Acting-
Premier Wittenoom, speaking at a Perth banquet, said
that he reckoned the year’s revenue to 30th June would
be £2,880,585, and that the surplus would reach £227,875.
Adding this to previous credit balances, the total surplus
would probably be £540,000. Then he added, tearfully,
that in the present state of the London market it would
be unwise to float another loan just now—the last one of
a million was onty floated in May—but “ the Government
could see its wayclear to finance the colony without
borrowing until the end of August.” Westralia has accu-
mulated close on £3,000,000 of new debt—something like
£2l for each inhabitant—within a year, and it actually
reckons it can get along for two months without any
further borrowing! !
» * • •
The British financier’s yearning to issue deben-
tures is unquenchable. The latest British idea is to com-
bine the City of Melbourne, Federal, Mercantile and
English and Australian Mortgage banks (all burst) in one
liquidation. This looks a decently sensible idea, as one
liquidation is less expensive than four. But though there
is absolutely nothing to be done except sell the assets as
soon and as profitably as possible, and pay as much as
possible in the £, it is proposed to start by establishing
a London office and board of directors, and creating a
nominal capital of £75,800, and issuing debentures to the
amount of £1,269,500 If the average British financier
were stranded alone on a raft in mid-ocean, the first thing
he would do would be to issue 3 per cent, debentures and
divide himself into preference and ordinary capital.
* * * *
The Modern Permanent Building and Invest-
ment Co., a big Melbourne concern which has been very
close in to a lee shore for several years, at last announces
that its difficulties are too much for it. For the year
ended 31st July, 1894, the Modern showed a loss of
£13,800. Then it persuaded its depositors to reduce
interest from 5 to 2£ per cent., and stated proudly that
the drift had been effectually stopped. For the .year
ended July, ’95, it lost £2630, and at July, ’96, there
was another loss of £11,431. Finally, in June, ’97, it
states that it has also lost its reserve-fund (£32,690), and
its entire capital (£198,897), and £96,176 of its-deposits,
and that, as property values are now, it is utterly broke.
The present value of the assets is set down at £375,824,
but as they only yield 3 per cent, net on this amount, and
as hardly anybody would buy land or bricks and mortar to
yield less than 4 percent., the chances are that they
wouldn’t sell, as things are, for more than £300,000. The
Modern proposes to reconstruct by giving the depositors
5 per cent, preference shares in exchange for their de-
posits. Any depositor who doesn’t yearn after preference
shares is to receive 12s. 6d. in the £ —not in cash, but in
3 per cent, deposit-receipts, payable in three, five, seven
and ten years. Present shareholders are to write off one-
half of the paid-up value of their shares, and are to come
in for profits on the other half when the preference crowd
has received three 5 per cent, dividends. If half the
depositors accept preference shares and get no dividend,
and things improve a little, this will just about stop thedrift.
Two-thirds of the depositors are in Britain, and everything
depends on them. The Victorian crowd expresses its
willingness, as usual, to take anything it can get and be
darned thankful. When any crowd of Australians goes
to a reconstruction meeting now, and gets away with its
clothes and its hat, it feels grateful.
Albury (N.S.W.) Borough Council is in the
midst of the periodical stench re finances. A committee
has dug out the fact that the town has been spending
£5OO a year more than its revenue for ten years past, ap-
parently without anybody observing the fact, and that
£3OOO of rates have been overdue for four years. The
utter pigheadedness of the N.S.W. municipal audit system
is mainly responsible for this state of things—which is the
normal state throughout the province. The auditor may
be a butcher or a cow-feeder or anything else; there is
absolutely no need for him to know anything about
accounts. He is elected by the ratepayers, most of whom
know nothing about accounts either, and are utterly
unable to judge whether he is fit for his billet or not.
And he is paid at a rate which doesn’t make it worth
while for an expert accountant to apply for the place ; in
other words, he is paid at the rate at which the butcher
or house-painter is willing to undertake the job. Munici-
palities want expert and independent State audit worse
than almost any other class of institution in N.S.W.

Peru announces that it has ceased to coin silver,
and that the importation of silver coin is prohibited. The
silver countries keep dx-opping out one by one. If the bi-
metallio theories are correct, when they have practically
all dropped out something so very dreadful will be the
natural result that they should all be converted and take
to bi-metallism right off. Meanwhile, the prospects of the
silver market are not bright.

The Victorian wheat - crop of ’97 pans out
7,000,000 bushels—less than half that of ’93 and ’94. Last
year it was still worse, being 5,600,000 bushels. The loss
by drought, even in the small province of Victoria, runs
to something like £4,000,000 in a year.
• * * *
Barlow, M.L.O. (Queensland), orating at the
meeting of the A.M.P. Society, said, in a lofty tone, that
the society need not be troubled with any fears about the
S.A. State Land Bank interfering with its mortgage busi-
ness. “It would merely relieve the society of the risk of
taking any of those questionable investments which, in a
weak moment, it might be tempted to accept. All doubt-
ful securities would naturally gravitate towards the State
institution because there the constituent with a vote
would have the member who wanted it to assist him.
From experience of politicians, any bank with which they
could interfere would be distinctly to the advantage of
so sound an institution as the A.M.P. Society.” Which
was a luckless statement, seeing that the S.A. State Land
Bank isn’t run by politicians but by independent Com-
missioners, while the A.M.P. Society is bossed by a poli-
tician and assistant-bossed by 14, or so, other politicians.
Possibly Barlow would like to support his case by some
remarks about the lamentable insolvency of the Post
Office Savings Banks, run by the State, as compared with
the well-known soundness of the Queensland National
Bank under private management ?

After Ist July all W.A. banks will make a half-
guinea charge on all current accounts opened. All
accounts that fall below £5O for three consecutive days
will be charged a half-guinea for that half-year—and the
same magic sum is to be the minimum charge on over-
drafts.

The Metropolitan Board of Works incubus is
beginning to be felt in Melb. The question of differential
rating for the sewered and unsewered parts is looming
ominous. The £6OOO law-costs last year is causing talk,
looks bad for the public, and well for the Board’s finn of
solicitors, which includes a Minister of the Crown. Those
who oppose the Board system in toto are earnestly striv-
ing to force this Board to declare a stiff rate over the
whole area, sewered and unsewered alike. This will burst
up the whole concern, with pieces of Fitzgibbon on top.
Strange that Victoria should adopt such a system imme-
diately after it had been tried and found badly wanting in
London.

• * * *
Chief Justice Madden has refused the “poor
mortgagee” leave to appeal to the Privy Council in
M’Cashill v. Commercial Bank. In this case the vendor
of certain land was in possession as a tenant under
an agreement with the purchaser, and the bank, as
mortgagee from the purchaser, strove vainly before the
Full Court to gain possession of the land clear of the said
tenancy rights. This is the case in reference to which
the Argus lately talked a half-column of bad law and
worse common sense, about the wrongs of the poor but
careless mortgagee under the provisions of the Torrens
Act.
* * * *
Wild-cat promoters have to study the fashions.
Just now copper is the favorite. Result : Hardly art
alleged assay in which copper doesn’t figure. Shows
hitherto quite guiltless of that metal have suddenly
developed it, and all late assays contain the line :
“Copper, a trace.”

Crookwell, N.S.W. : “ English capitalists are
prepared to buy up mines in Tuena districts if Govt, will
construct the Goulburn to Crookwell railway. Rich reefs'
of silver ore are known to exist at Mt. Costigan and Cor-
dillera, but the excessive carriage for coke, also for silver
‘ingots,’ prevents the mines paying.” “Also for silver
ingots” is indeed lovely. These mines went into liqui-
dation when silver was nearly double the present price,,
and all the railways in the world would not make up that
difference. Silver, and not a railway, is their chief want,,
and too well a number of Sydney people know it.

bored in pick-handles, at others sent up with the mullock,
and taken away at night, and that the shafts are now
being fenced with barbed wire. Pleasant news for Lordi
Kilmorey.
• • • •
Mb. Hope (N.S.W.) copper-mines are still at
work, though a big part of the product is eaten up in
carriage to Cobar. Soine day the Cobar line will be
extended through to Broken Hill—it was built as the first
section : big smelting works will be established at Lith-
gow, and several copper, silver, and gold mines will be-
found.
• * * *
A Bulletin correspondent wants to make oufc
that Rocky Bridge (N.S.W.) opal-mine, now to l e ex-
ploited by an English syndicate, is an old affair, which
promised well in the seventies, and has been more or less
shepherded ever Binoe. He says it was found bv the
Peasley, or Beazley, girls, who were shepherding on Rocky
Bridge Creek. The Peasleys had a selection on the Aber-
crombie River, to which stream Rocky Bridge Creek is a
tributary. Sir Saul Samuel, or his agent, gave the girls
£5O to show the place where they found “ the pretty
stones’’—opals—they exhibited. For all these years,
continues the writer, the mine has been locked up. The
story doesn’t look very symmetrical—few mines known to
be good stay so long locked up.
* • « *
A good-looking story : —“Dear Bulletin, —The
Golden Dykes Junction G.M. Co. has given the public
what has never been done in any W.A. flotation before—
consistent crushings. The average has been over 2oz. ;
another 200 tons are going through, and the whole body of
ore in sight has the same consistent appearance. A shaft
has been sunk 180 ft., exposing a reef from sft. on the
surface to Oft. at the bottom, all of which looks like l£oz.
per ton, and there is enough water to keep 15 heads going.
No. 2 shaft is sunk in a kaolin formation to 90ft. The
crushings have been taken from three shafts. As an old
Victorian, 1 have given the public every possible help to
see for themselves that this flotation is not a ‘ cat,’ hut a
square investment. The gold won from the mill in these
crushings has been placed to credit of the co., less crush-
ing and working expenses, thus providing enough to erect
the mill without touching the £lO,OOO working capital.—
T.R.T.”
* * * *
The directors of the Railway Extended (W.A.)
gold-mine “congratulate the shareholders upon the
alterations in the labor conditions of the Westralian gold-
fields regulations, whereby it is now necessary to employ
only one man to every six acres, instead of, as formerly, one
man to every three acres.” This co., one of an extensive
type, has in hand £lll with which to keep one man to
every six acres working until “ the boom,” comes.
• * * *
Hit or Miss Co., Westralia, gone into liquida-
tion in London, the new co. to have the same share
capital paid up to 175., leaving a 3s. liability. The chair-
man : —“ When we review the past, we can but admit
that we have been very grievously disappointed by our
managing engineer. . . . All we require now is a
good supply of ore of a profitable grade.” (Laughter.)
Capt. Penberthy, the present boss:—“l never had so
much faith, in the Water Block as I have at the present
time. In sinking we have struck a formation assaying
from 17dwt. to 22dwt. per ton, and it is my honest and
candid opinion that we are on the eve of a splendid dis-
covery. I hope you will be successful in raising the
necessary funds to operate on and make a fresh start. I
feel confident the co. has a bright future before it, pro-
vided you raise the necessary funds.” A shareholder
modestly asked what had become of the 2000 tons at
grass in Dec., ’95, said to be worth Boz. to the ton. Col.
Burton Brown wanted to know —seeing that one very
serious mistake had been made as to the value of the ore
what guarantee was there against another, and if Mr.
Oxley would hand over sufficient money, out of that he
had received from the flotation, to thoroughly test the
property. Some £35,000 had been spent on the mine,
and they were now told that they were to commence
sinking. Mr. Oxley, in explanation : “In his opinion the
work was carried out by the late manager in a most irre-
gular and disorderly manner, and it was that individual
who was responsible for the expenditure of the bulk of
the co.’s capital.” The writer will be truly sorry for
Oxley if he ever meets the former 17st. manager. “ The
first returns from the battery were very encouraging and
showed a value of 3oz. to the ton, over 2oz. being re-
covered. After the first four or five crushings the yield
gradually fell away, but this was because a lot of stuff
was crushed which should never have been put through
the mill.” The present writer happens to know that on
correct tonnage the first crushing went a little over an
oz., the second, third and fourth only a few dwts. Why
has the battery remained idle since if there is any such
good stuff around? Mine no good, blame the manager, is
an old game. The battery and manager proved the lies
of the prospectus, and nobody can get away from it.
• * * *
Hannan’s Reward battery is almost completed,
and, if the writer is correctly informed, it will be just as
likely to knock the bottom out of the mine as to make
anyone’s pile.
* * • •
Pro. Krause’s report on the General Gordon
mine, Westralia, has been issued simultaneously in Cool-
gardie, Adelaide, and London :—“ In contact with the
felsite porphyry, occurs a band from 20 to 200 ft. wide of a
greenish rock containing, in a felsite base, mystals of
orthoclase and plagioclase, a variable amount of viosite,
mostly in hexagonal plates, some blebs of quartz, a little
horneblende, and an abundance of pyrite.” There is a lot
more of this sort of thing : “ I have had up to the present
neither the leisure nor facilities for making microscopic
analysis.” The Lord be praised! “The high charges
made for freight and material have added enormously to
the current expenses of the mine. For instance, freight,
£6 a ton from Kanowna, 12 miles of by no means rough
road ; iron plate, £3 to £5 per cwt.; hardwood timber, £2
17s. 6d. per 100 super, feet; spars 25ft. long and 6in. to
Sin. diameter, £3 to £4 10s. each. With bricks at £23 a
thousand, rubble stone had to be substituted. Drinking-
water, IT 10s. per 100 gals To estimate the
capabilities of this mining property, it is essential to ob-
tain positive information on three points. The first is
with respect to the value of the auriferous gossan, which
can be most speedily ascertained by an exhaustive series
of sampling and assays. This important duly I was par-
ticularly anxious to engage upon at the outset, but have
been prevented from undertaking owing to the want of
some of the most ordinary laboratory requirements, for
which I have made timely requisition. . . . With the in-
formation now available there is reason to look forward
with confidence to a successful development of this pro-
perty within a reasonable time.” To sum it all up, there
are some bags of high-grade ore left by the prospectors,
and there are 31,000 tons in sight, of the value of which
not the slightest idea is given. The battery is in the
wrong place, and it is proposed to shift it. There is very
little water, but hopes of getting plenty in the sweet by-
and-by. Mistakes are admitted all round, and the
development work has been practically left at a stand-
still. The only ray of hope is “ that the mine is permis-
sive of becoming self-supporting whenever the necessary
dead work shall have been completed.” Still on a long,
long string!
* * * *
From Peak Hill, Westralia, to The Bulletin :
“Be your par. about the Peak Hill mines, I can substan-
tiate the statement that it is the only place in W.A. that
has done without outside capital. From gold won the
prospectors have been enabled to erect a 10-head battery,
which has already treated a good many tons from different
claims. The average from the different mines varies
from loz.—this from a big reef called the Christmas Gift
—to 12oz. to the ton. No capital is required to develop
the mines, as all the owners are pretty well independent.
Hence for a co. it would be the most genuine field in
W.A. at the present time. The original prospectors of
the field are W. Wilson, a native of Shoalhaven (N.S.W.),
•and G. Woodhouse, of M.L., and nearly all who followed
have stuck to the field and have interests good enough to
keep them there."—G.W.H. Nevertheless, it seems that
the claim-owners have listened to the voice of the charmer,
G. Darlington Simpson, who has bonded all the mines for
“ considerably over ±lOO,OOO, and has left for London
taking over 2000 oz. of gold and some lovely specimens.”
He has, however, declared that he will not take a single
penny in promoters’ profits, with the object of having the
working capital as large as possible and the nominal
capital as small as possible. Then the chances are he will
have no Lords on his prospectus.
* * • •
Declared that the lode struck recently in the
Kalgurli South, at Hannan’s, is a new reef never opened
in any other mine, and has an important bearing to
another group of mines, and will further add to the rich-
ness of the field. Tiib Bulletin sincerely hopes so. Rich
as it is, Hannan’s can stand a lot more richness to bear up
against its own “ cats,” to say nothing of the virulence of
that pest in other parts of the country for which it has to
suffer.
• * * •
Astonishing how mushroom-like are the news-
paper idols of Westralia. Last year George Gray was the
mining monarch of that province. To-day he is never
mentioned, and if at all vain he must feel like a badly
beaten dung-hill rooster in heavy moult. G. W. Brook-
man is the mining king, while the telluride crown is worn
by Modest Maryanski. According to M.M., the causes of
the slump are—war clouds, the South African depression,
“ and last but not least, the rascally reports and misre-
presentations made by so-called mining experts, had well-
nigh ruined mining in W.A.” Well, who are they ? A
list of names would give a warning for the future ?
* * * *
Says a Kalgoorlie paper: “In the course o!
time investors will realise that, in their eagerness to
acquire mining properties here during the boom, they
have had foisted on to them many ‘wild cats.’ Un-
scrupulous men invariably take advantage of such oppor-
tunities, and purchasers must admit that, they themselves
have not been altogether blameless in not making fuller
inquiries as to the worth of what they were receiv-
ing in return for their money.” But not a word
about unscrupulous newspapers taking advantage
of their opportunity. Did that Kalgoorlie paper
ever isssue one note of warning ?—or did it bar-
rack for the boom ? The “ so-called experts ” were
bad enough, Heaven knows, but it was the lying done by
the united press of Australia—which was blind to the
most glaring of mining swindles—that did the most mis-
chief. To shriek for reformation after the robberies have
been committed is childish. If the
press desires further proof of its culpability let it reprint
5 per cent, of the “ news ” it published concerning the
great and enduring richness of Westralian mines, which,
it has since been proved, never had any gold in them, and
have been abandoned as worthless, after hundreds of
thousands were paid for them, and other hundreds of
thousands spent on them. Many of the worst of W.A.
“ cats ” were worked off on the English public without
reports from any experts, but by means of prospectuses
entirety made up of paragraphs from Australian news-
papers, and many a good yarn could be told of the
tricks played to secure the original publication of those
paragraphs.

A Cairns (N.Q.) man who visited the Dead
Horse lead : “ Clermont has a nice, cool clin ate. Most of
the men there are as fat as whales.” By the yvay, he was
a “black-leg” who discovered the lead named. Where-
upon the “boys” christened his claim “ Soab Hill.’’
When the warden came to lay out the p.c., he objected
to that name. The “boys” compromised with “S'jrtejr
Hill,” and Blister Hill it remains.
* * * •
A Clermont (Q.) visitor’s private opinion;—
“The rush is overdone already, unless the Dead Horse
lead is traced further. The lead runs North and South,
and is known for a length of from three-quarters of tkipile
to one mile. I doubt if more than 60 claims aye
getting wash-dirt. There are a few' lucky ones, but yoju
could count them on your fingers. Three or four are
getting from lOoz. to 18oz. to the load. On the
ridges, the wash takes 30ft. or 40ft. of sinking; on the
flats, there is little or none. Perhaps rather more than a
dozen claims on Blister Hill are doing fairly well, but it is
very little good. Out of 1800 men on the rush, 1100 are
getting nothing. There are no wages to be earned, but
living is cheap : Flour, 50lb. bag, 9s. 6d. ; best beef, 2d,
per lb. ; English potatoes, 281 b., 2s. 6d. ; sugar, 61b., Is, ;
fresh butter, Is. per lb.”

SOCIETY
THE DROUGHT.—ON THE BARWON RIVER, N.S.W.
Pulling out and skinning bogged sheep.
The Sydney official “functions” in honor
of Victoria’s sixty-years incubation were fairly ac-
cording to precedent. All of them save one—to wit,
the official attendance in state of the Governor and
the Judges at St. Andrew’s Cathedral. That
“ function ’’was repugnant to thfe Constitution of
the province. There is no State Church in N.S.
Wales. Why, then, should the cathedral of one
of the sects have been deliberately selected as the
scene of a State ceremonial? In his private -capa-_
city a Judge or a Governor can attend what place
of worship it may please him to patronise. But
when it comes to bis betaking himself, inside his
State livery, to any particular cathedral, chapel,
or joss-house, lie is violating an essential principle
of the Constitution.
* * * *
“ Ain’t it sickenin’,” the newspaper gush
about Jubilee feeds to the old, the young, the
lame, and the blind ? Eeed the hungry by all
means, but why not spare us the greasy enthu-
siasm of the benevolent reporter and his blanky
good-natured condescension when he descends to
joyful details about the loyal avidity with which
the guests wolfed masses of juicy beef and great
hunks oi bread, and their devotion to the Throne
and Person over solid blocks of plum duff ? It all
reads painfully like a British description of the
Squire’s free feed to the rapacious Hodge. Has
it indeed come to this, that there are thousands of
Australians reduced to such straits that they re-
joice gluttonously over a gob of beef hacked from
a half-cremated ox, and mumble a rhapsodical
grace to Eat Man over a wedge of indigestible
dumpling ?
* * * *
Melb. Argus turned its boss liar on to the
Jubilee illuminations, and that functionary suc-
ceeded in discovering an entirely new order of
Australian fauna—the loyal larrikin. Describing
how one of “the talent” was about to stoush a
Chow, the Argus Ananias proceeds to show how—
His companions grasped him by the shoulders and held
him still. “ Knock off, Jack ; remember what the nig h*
is,” said one ; and as he spoke “ John ” approached fear-
lessly, and,-smiling widely, said, “You no ha’m me ; me
allee same Englisman. Gau savee Kawean.” When last
seen East and West had fraternised, and West was endea-
vouring to lead East into a convenient hostelry to drink
Her Majesty’s health once more.
“ Knock off, Jack, remember what the night is,”
is a truly pathetic touch. What a satire on the
humor of a people !
* * * *
The paltriest of all the Melb. illuminations
were those of that most enthu. .astic loyalist, Soft-
goods Sargood, who at Jubilee festivities and in
Parliament has always been ready with a fistful of
grease. With the exception of Sargood’s ware-
house, the whole of Flinders-street, Melb., was a
blaze of light, and in the middle, of it this one
huge block of buildings stuck up like a reproach.
* * * *
The German firms in Melb. were con-
spicuously absent from the general illuminations ;
in fact, with the churches, which had too keen an
appreciation of money matters, they were the only
people in the southern city who did not give way
to the prevalent grovel.

Dear Bulletin, —Re
Melbourne’s emptiness.
Within a stone’s throw of
me here in Richmond are four

4s. 6d. They contain 20, 14,

61. Out of that 3,1, 10 and
1 are let —46 of the lot empty

* * *
Love leads to strange
consequences. A Gore (M.L.)
young man lately arranged to
meet the Mary Jane of his
affections in the cemetery by
moonlight. He was there,
but, despite long waiting
among the damp grass and
the tombs, no Mary Jane
turned up. Then he gave
way to a burst of rage, and
broke a number of tomb-
stone ornaments, and severely
walloped the epitaphs with a
big stick, in consequence of
which he had to pay a fine and
heavy damages and costs.
“ Some descendants of
Wackford Squeefs must have
found their way to Maori-
land, judging by the experi-
ence of a friend of mine,”
writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent. “He got a billet as
assistant-master at a board-
ing-school on the East Coast,
and had not been there a
week when his chief informed
him that he objected to his
assistant eating bread, butter
and treacle. He might take
bread and butter or bread
and treacle ; to combine the
three was a wicked waste,
which would surely lead to a
woeful want. My friend left.”

Sundry Mayors of Australian cities and
townships were disappointed at not securing
titles. Hobart Mayor Davies is understood to be
particularly angry at being overlooked. In which
connection a Bulletin correspondent notes the
fact that at a Jubilee ball given by the Mayor no
preparations were made for the reception of Chief
Justice Dobson, the Tas. Lieut.-Governor, who
had to find his way into the hall unheralded and
unsung. . .
Some Queensland newspaper grovel :
The rain is coming this way from Sydney, and will
doubtless be here about Jubilee day. Let us hope it will
not oome, and that we may have “Queen’s weather.”
Just so. Let the lambs die and the crops wither,
but God grant that we may have dry weather to
stuff and guzzle in honor of Queen Victoria. The
crawling toadswallower who wrote —
Let wealth and commerce, laws and learning die,
But leave us still our old nobility—
left a lot of descendants, with less excuse for their
worminess than the elder blot on manhood.

Sydney City Council, after all its Jubilee
grovel, has indeed been badly left, illoo for an
address, Town Hall brilliantly illuminated, the
new Markets and a city street royally branded--
but the Mayor is still plain Mr. Ives. Truly,
Jubilee “ honors ” are not easy.
The choosing of children from the Sydney
Public Schools to sing at the Jubilee E6te seems to
have been conducted, in many cases, with great
unfairness ; some of the best singers were passed
over and the well-dressed children of those who
can give them many pleasures were chosen. A
large number of the scholars in the public schools
have parents who could well afford to pay more
for their education, yet this class, it is complained,
is always favored when any festival comes round.
* * * *
The record illumination of a Sydney book-
store (Geo. Robertson and Co.) was the single,
sensible exception to the ruck of “V.R.’s” and
“Godsaves.” Round a set of purely Australian
emblems, designed by A, R. Pullin, ran the dis-
loyal legend :
Fame of families is all a cheat:
’Tis personal virtue makes us great.
And the composition included three caps of
liberty and not one head of Queen Victoria !
• * * •
The private illuminations at Brisbane (Q.)
were of the poorest and sickliest order. The
wealthy Q. Club broke its heart to the extent of
a “ V.R. ” gas-fixture, not made for the occasion.
A few hotels and shops sported various caricatures
of Her Gracious, that on the Courier buildings
being positively disloj T al in its hideousness. Ac-
cording to that work of art, Her Gracious has an
Oriental nose, fat and lumpy at the nostrils, a wo-
fully long and Milesian upper-lip, and a receding
chin, while the downward curves of her mouth ex-
hibit a temper almost amounting to ferocity. His
Ex., of course, had a grand illuminated arch fixed
up towards the main entrance to his blessed
worth of fireworks, but who is going to pay for
that has not transpired. Yet Brisbane was
rushed by bush-folk.

(Q.), on Record Insanity N.ght, were, all the press
gusli to the contrary notwithstanding, a melan-
choly failure. The “ set pieces ” were only a few
feet above the river’s-level, and four-fifths of the
massed thousands in the Govt. Domain could not
see them at all, while to those in the very front
the supposed inscriptions were illegible. Many of
the fireworks were damaged. Bombs went cronk ;
rockets declined duty ; catherine-wheels refused

Beloved Queen
How drunk we’ve been
On loyalty and ’lumina-
tion !
We are so glad
That you have had
Your reisrning-record de-
monstration ;
For once again
Our sober brain <
Can comprehend the situa-
tion.
We sang a song
Qf Liviqg Long
A Life unruffled by the
baker,
Whilst grocers’ bills
And such-like ills
Sent weary millions to God’s-
acre ;
You deigned to live
That we might give
Loud praises to our mon-
arch’s Maker.
By statesmen, who
Have charge of you,
The Hempire’s interests are
tended;
Your formal “ Yea ”
From day' to day
Approves of what those
gentlemen did.
You merely smile
And reign the while
No wonder that your health
is splendid.
Could mortal ask
A softer task
Than queening over docile
nations ;
Ordained by fate
To sit in state,
August amongst one’s own
relations,
For sixty years,
Receiving cheers
And shirking social obliga-
tions ?
The Queen exists,
Therein consists
Our vain excuse for bogus
gladness;
By deed or word
You ne'er have stirred
Our public soul to joy or
sadness.
We starve or thrive,
You —stay alive,
And bless us for our grovel
madness.
Well, let it pass ;
We burnt our gas
And made some fleeting
moments fleeter;
We’ve winked our eyes
At ancient lies
Repeated by the Press re-
peater ;
We’ve spreed our spree,
And Jubilee
Is “ off” (thank Goodness !)
at the meter.

* * * •
Oxford-street, Sydney, was the most pathe-
tic-looking spot in the city on Jubilee night. It
had gone in almost exclusively for Chinese lan-
terns. By 7.30 the rain had extinguished nearly
all of them, and they hung like hundreds of
sodden dish-cloths from their respective lines..
The showers came down heavily. Trams both ways
were crowded to suffocation and refused to stop
lest any addition to the load should make the
bottom fall out. At every stopping-place about
70 people in sodden waterproofs were waiting for
any kind of vehicle to come and take them out of
this Jubilee, and when each tram labored past
they would rush out, and hang on, and-dropoff, and
say things beginning with “ blanky,” and then go
back to the pavement to wait events. There wasn’t
very much extra tram revenue collected; most of
the people rode free—after 10 o’clock, anyhow.
There would be about 24 people in each compart-
ment, and a line of them on the footboards, and
some unlawful marauders on the roof. The
conductor would put his head in with difficulty at
a compartment, and say “Fare, please.” Some-
times the 24 people panned out for 6d., or even
Bd. ; sometimes 3d. was handed out; occasionally
there was only an intimation that the conductor
had better mind his bloomin’ eye. The fares
which were handed along were mostly embezzled
in passing, and never got so far as the doorway.
The crowd on the roof as a rule flatly refused
to pass down anything, and the crowd on the
footboard looked so aggressive and so ready
to throw the conductor off on his head that
it was seldom asked to pay up. And in addition
to riding gratis the population emphatically de-
clined to get down and push when the tram stuck
on an incline, and often the vehicle would struggle
half-way up the hill by Victoria Barracks three or
four times, and roll back again among the hoorays
of its passengers, along with loud cries of “gimme
back me tuppence ! ”
* * * *
The Jubilee illumination at Melbourne’s
marbleous Commercial Bank was pathetic in its
simplicity. A crystal V.R. and crown of pre-
boomtime size, shining at long intervals across the
front of the building, and awakening reflections
on the gorgeous design that might have been there
if the event had happened eight years ago, when
banking was reckoned pre-eminent among the fine
arts, and Gyles Turner was boss of the Financial
Academy. The Australasia made a considerable
display, but, taking them altogether, the banks
seemed very conscious of their reduced circum-
stances. The E.S. and A. was content with a
small transparency, suitable to the church-like
appearance of the head-office. The E.S. and A.
is severely pious in its scheme of architecture,
which was designed to suggest that the Lord
loveth the cheerful depositor. On the trans-
parency aforesaid, Britannia figured as a sort of
divine female exhorting the public to lay up trea-
sures in a reconstructed Heaven and wait till the
olouds roll by.
The late E.M.G. EDDY, N.S.W. Railway
Commissioner.

Melb. ; u The pensioners, in red coats, in
an ignoble-looking, van, got more cheers than any-
thing over the Jubilee military procession. Poor
old fools ! What a consummate ass is the private
soldier. The glory.is all his, but the Upper Crust
pockets the dollars. Then it pats him on the
back—fine fellow ! grand old relic ! After he has
been starving about a generation, they stick him
in a go-cart, and exhibit him to a shouting popu-
lace, not one per cent, of whom would give him
threepence , for a bed. But, damme, he’s as
honest as the horse that goes, for the Melbourne
Cup, while statesmen are a crowd of villainous
pulling jockeys, trainers, and owners. Chuck
up your hat for Tommy !’

* * * *
The other day, a life-policyholder with an
Australian insurance co. lost a leg, and, though
she wasn’t insured against accident, she asked the
co. to do something for her ; whereupon the co.
bought her a <£2s cork-leg, which is described in
the press as “a generous gift.” Alas, if you
looked in the company’s books, you would, no
doubt, find that £25 charged to advertising
account. That company pasted its little poster
on poor Mrs. Brown’s cork-leg. Which is exactly
what the loyal public has been doing in the matter
of its Jubilee illuminations—-pasting proclamations
on Queen Victoria’s cork-leg, as it were, and
charging up same to reclame. For instance, how
diligently each Sydney bank went to work to find
out how much the other fellows meant to spend in
gas and flags, and how cruelly the big bank dished
all the others!

* * * *
Hobart Mercury, describing the W. J.
Clarke funeral: —
This terminated the service. In a moment the members
of the Rupertswood Battery had collected round the flag-
covered bier. Gradually the heavy coffin was lifted on to
their shoulders, and to the solemn strains of the “ Dead
March” in Saul, the. remains of the dead man passed from
the dimly-lighted chancel down the altar steps, through
the reverent congregation, and so, in measured steps, out
of the central door and towards the crowd without.
Thus does a corpse walk at its own funeral.
Which is quite up to S.M. Herald vice-regal
wedding description form.
* » * «
At least one Sydney undertaker mixed up
“ V.R.” and “ In Memoriam ” on Record Night,
and scattered fairy-lamps amongst the corpse-
cases, and got festive electric glitter on to the
coffin-plates and plaster-wreaths, and the planting-
procession-photographs in the window —and made
of himself a most loyal and hilarious and incon-
gruous ghoul generally.
• * * *
Melbourne people, admiring the beauteous
outline illumination of Government House, which
was effective for miles around, gave Gov. Brassey
credit for “shouting” the display. Brassey’s
loyal lavishness was a popular Jubilee delusion,
however. In the first instance, he had made a
show of intending to illuminate on his own
account, but his ideas were so str.ctly economica
that the Government stepped in and did the thing
handsomely. Brassey paid for some fireworks.
* C » *
At Cambridge, the Public Orator, in pre-
senting the Australian Premiers on the occasion
of their being given doetorships of law, “eulo-
gised each separately in a Latin address ’’ —which
was pure Greek to almost every man Jack of
them. Likely enough that the Orator, disgusted
with the proceedings, expressed his private
opinions of the Premiers in a language they
couldn’t understand —this to save trouble, especi-
ally with Digger Dick and the fiery Kingston.
Which recalls an incident in the life of Daniel
O’Connell, whom the British Govt, was, on one
occasion, specially anxious to catch napping in the
matter of sedition. Dan. had arranged one Sun-
day afternoon to address an open-air mass meet-
ing, and to this meeting Govt, sent three special
reporters. Daniel knew their mission perfectly
well,-and received them with great cordiality—saw
them made comfortable on the platform, and
would not begin till they assured him they were
quite ready. When they gave the word to start
he, with a bland smile, turned to the big crowd
and commenced his speech—in Irish ! Now, how
much better off were the Australian Premiers
than those English reporters?

Drought item from Kaneira (Vic.) reported
in a Melbourne paper :
A farmer here, fearing to leave hisl seed wheat in his
shed, removed it into his house, which is a solid brick
structure. His hungry stock attacked his residence,
broke through two doors, and got at the wheat. When
this supply was exhausted the animals pursued their in-
vestigations further, and, getting access to a bedroom, ate
the straw mattress that was upon the bedstead.

« * * *
The Argus did a rather scrubby thing
lately while reporting rather fully a sermon of
Baptist preacher Clifford. It gave his allusion to
Kant’s noble maxim that in all things we are to
act to every man as if he were an end in himself,
and not as a mere means to an end, but it care-
fully omitted the one stirring and effective pas-
sage in the lecture, the application of this maxim
to the conduct of the Argus’s especial clientele
towards their sweated clerks and seamstresses.
The Age, for a wonder, reported it fairly fully.
THE OLD AGE OF THE STAGE.
“Charley Bur ford."
The Brisbrane (Q.) W.C.T.U., as one man,
assaulted Tozer, the other day, in favor of repeal-
ing the C.D. Act. Some of the arguments were
criss-cross, as, for example : (a) The Act in France
has brought the population there to a standstill;
(6) The Act increases illegitimate births. The
place where the ladies landed Tozer one “on the
point,” as it were, was when they declared that
the C.D. Act “ interferes with the liberty of the
subject.” If you think all round this point you’ll
see a lot of conclusions attaching to it.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
„ „ _ Sydney, June 29, 1897.
Mr Dear Mtee—
It was a wet and weepful jubilee, after all. The
rain filled the colored cups of the Hyde Park fairy-
lights to overflowing, pulped the Oxford-street
paper-lanterns, and utterly drowned the Conti-
nental. The average jubilator who beheld the
Continental on Monday, 21st, was loud in its
praises, and if asked to describe it, began: “ Well,
there was a long pole in the middle,” &c. I my-
self heard 15 descriptions, and they all had that
long pole in them and nothing else. The “ fiery
dragon,” which was to shoot flames and smoke all
around the harbor, was a hollow fraud of disap-
pointingly early habits, for it retired to its long
rest at Cockatoo Island ere half the saturated
multitudes had done feasting their eyes on the
other glories.
Govt. House verandah, on Jubilee night, was
thronged by* a crowd of macintoshed sightseers.
With the object of seeing the abbreviated firework
display they remained outside until the last
search-light was thrown around, and then drifted
under shelter, dropped rain into the light refresh-
ments, and saturated the cakes. At the clubs,
which were thrown open to ladies, there were also
rations served to all, and little china souvenirs
presented to guests. Outside the Herald office a
crowd, collected to read two notices, posted up as
one without a break : “ G. H. Reid has been made
a Privy Councillor. In case of accidents ring up
2030 and the ambulance will attend without
charge.” The electric lights were ahead of gas as
an illuminator, and one of the most brilliantly-lit
warehouses was illuminated by a row of candles in
every window. By eleven o'clock the gas was
turned out everywhere, the Star whirligigs ceased
to revolve, on the P.O. tower the jewelled crown
began to play “ peep bo ” with the crowd waiting
for the message which never came, and the show
was practically over.
The Town Hall illuminations were encored on
Friday night, when the Mayor and Mayoress
were “ at-home.” The red, white and blue incan-
descent lamps shone on a large number of callers
considering the weather. A civic group strolled
about and rattled tea and coffee-cups agitatedly
while singing was going on. The guests on arriv-
ing shook hands with Mayor Ives and family, and
chatted about the illuminations and the rain, but
there were large, silent blanks in the conversation
where congratulations should have come in. It
was an all-comers’ event, and the dressing was ac-
cordingly mixed. Mrs. Ives looked well in black ;
her daughter’s gown was rose-bud besprinkled. A
rose-pink gown attracted notice, also a trailing
brocade with lace and jewel puffery. It was a
night-out for black frocks and second-best evening
cloaks —a “ second-best ” evening cloak is invari-
ably of nun’s-veiling and swansdown As a wind-
up, the organist thundered forth two introductory
chords which brought all the company to their
feet imagining God-save had begun, but at the
third note they all flopped again, discovering that
they were listening to the beginning of Luther’s
hymn.
The monotony of “ God-save ” had to be varied
somehow last week. To make a change, it was
sprung on the audience at the Amateurs’ Orchestral
concert, in the “Jubilee” overture, into which
the composer Weber incorporated it. Introduced
in the middle of the programme, it made the un-
musical prick up their ears and fancy they had
heard that tune somewhere before Signor Hazon
conducted, to great applause, a brilliant “little
thing of bis own ” —a Jubilee piece, which repro-
duced faithfully all the impressions of a National
Lighting-up, even to the falling of the ’bus-horses
on the wet blocks, and the cursing of 'the Potts
Pointman when the rocket-stick wrecked his roof.
A crowd gathered on Mrs. Macquarie’s chair to
see the fireworks which, for some of the jubila-
tors in the background, were blotted out by um-
brellas. When asked to shut them up, the owners
of the gamps furled—unanimously except one
fashionably-garbed woman, who continued to
shelter her frills undisturbed by protests, and
turned a deaf ear to personal remarks. “She
carn’t shut it, the man wot mends the gamp
hasn’t been round this week.” “That ain’t it, it’s
one of them shilling gamps that won’t shut.”
“ Say, mum, kindly turn yer umbreller round so’s
us can see the fiery dragon thro’ the ’ole in the
other side. ” Which last fling brought down the
brolly.

The march of troops through the city on Jubilee
day was the only excitement of the afternoon, ex-
cept the function in the Palace Gardens where a
mildly-interested throng watched H.E., sur-
rounded by magnates on a purple-draped platform,
twitch the bunting off Gov. Phillip. By the time
they reached the city the soldiers seemed tired,
but pulled themselves together to get a cheer
which happened when the pipers came along. Of
all the garbing the unpretending khakee won most
applause. One of the most imposing sights in the
procession was Colonel Neild, as he sat with in-
flated chest on his fiery charger. The populace
seem somehow to regard the gallant Colonel as a
military joke ; but the fact of the Colonel’s magni-
ficence remains—so far as appearances go he might
be a Marshal of the Second Empire. An old lady
in black marched alongside her son, or some other
fire-eating relative, and was part of the show for
onlookers.
Jubilee oratory would lead one to suppose that
V.R. is a benevolent despot who has, for the past
00 years, been running the Hempire on a system
of her own. The discovery that she lacks even
the royal power to accept a purse of sovereigns or
a new pony-cart from any unknown admirer comes
upon ns with a shock of disillusionment. Last week
the Colonial Secretary cabled from London : “In
view of the large number of Jubilee offerings to
the Queen, I have to give notice that the Queen
regrets that the rule respecting offerings from
private individuals, with whom she is personally
unacquainted, prevents her receiving such pre-
sents in commemoration of her 60 years’ reign. ”
And the newspapers publish this sad news under
the heading, “Refusal of Personal Gifts by Her
Majesty ” ! The venerable lady is simply pro-
hibited from grabbing at them.
While the preparations for the Jubilee were
being made in Perth, Messrs. Vickery and Co.
wired to the Mayors of Coolgardie and Kalgoorlie
“ 10,000 yards black muslin suitable decorations.”
An hour afterwards came the reply, “Say who is
dead.”
“ People can talk as they like about the talent
and capacity of Australian politicians who have
gone to England,” said a man to me, the other
day, “but how many of those politicians were
clever enough to leave their wives behind them?
If you want a standard of diplomatic ability, there
you are ! ”
Elsie Adair, the new vaudeville dancer at the
Cri., possesses the secret, known only to women
of the Yewnited States, of parting her front hair
without adding ten years to her appearance. For
a girl of any other nation who is on the wrong side
of five-and-twenty it is fatal to have the hair
neatly brushed away each side of the centre part-
ing and bunched over the ears with sparkling
combs. Ladies from the U.S.A. can alone manage
it successfully. Contemplating Elsie, the printed
rapture on the programme seems a large order, for
though she is smart and graceful, she isn’t strik-
ingly original. A night or two after her opening
she made melodious love in her encore song to an
elderly, white-haired potentate in a front stall,
which made the audience laugh while the victim
wriggled.
The shiny, plastered style of hair-dressing which
up-to-date Man now considers the correct notion
is catching on in Sydney. A fashionable person
at the Record Orchestral Concert, on Thursday
night, had his hair as glossy as his Sunday hat.
The new mode is not to be attained without
prayer and fasting. Some ’Murkan johnnies
(whence the fashion originates) tightly bandage
the scalp at night to make the thatch lie flat and
smooth all day. The hair must be allowed some
length—a bristling summer crop wouldn’t lend
itself to grooming.
The first night of “ Romeo and Juliet ” was wit-
nessed by a full and fairly enthusiastic house.
The curtain was raised many times, but there were
few flowers —I only saw a lonesome little bunch of
roses which whizzed from the stalls. The “ Sign of
the Cross ” co. was dotted about the d.c. in the
same grouping this co. always affects. The groups
are so invariably the same that it would cause a
small sensation were the members to play “ family
coach ” and change places with each other. A
beauty actress, backed up as always by aristocratic-
looking “Momma,” decorated the centre box.
On the stage, Mrs. B. P’s recent recruit, Miss
Muret, showed a disposition to follow Cora’s lead
in the matter of clinging attitudes and graceful
drapery.
At a Sydney Anglican church, the other day,
a special meeting of parishioners was called to see
what could be done towards paying the arrears of
the poor parson’s salary—some <£9s. A fashion-
able youth suggested that half-a-dozen young
ladies should be appointed to take the places of
the apathetic males who now show people to their
seats and take round the plate. Whereupon the
old gentlemen present put their glasses on and
stared savagely at the business-like young man.
Nothing came of the meeting, so the poor parson
may have to strike.
It is the prevalent craze of the moment to buy
the new N.S.W. postage-stamps. Philatelists—
awkward word—are gloating over the new blot-
ting-paper-tint penny stamps, but the special
favorite with everybody seems to be the Jubilee
2|d., which peoplesayis veryartistic. The large,ob-
long adhesive plaster which is issued at a penny and
sold for a shilling, for the Consumptive Hospital,
is having a boom of its own. It recalls the enor-
mous stamp issued for the American Centenary.
Of two sets of these stamps, one showed the
landing of Columbus with a clean-shaven face,
the other Col. parleying with the natives. In the
short interim he had grown a healthy beard.
A new grievance against cabmen, arrived at
last meeting of the Sydney Transit Commis-
sioners, is that cabby is cruel enough to leave his
horse slumbering in the shafts all night while the
driver slumbers in his cab, wrapped up in bags
and rugs. Most Sydney cabs look it. Now one
understands the weary up-all-night aspect of
many of those vehicles. At present Sydney cabs
are a misfit, too large for one and much too small
for two; the roof in the old-fashioned sort of cab
is too low for soaring flower-sprays and billowy
plumes on hats, and there isn’t room for two pairs
of exaggerated sleeves.
Jubilee music is simply pouring in. De Beau-
puis’ “ Queen’s Reign March,” arranged for piano,
makes a satisfactory noise, but some of the
passages don’t seem to give enough elbow-room to
the performer’s fingers. E. Balharry writes a
waltz (Allen and Co., Melb.), of the twinkling-
fairy-footsteps kind, and calls it “ Her Majesty,”
which is ironical. There isn’t a fairy-footfall in
the whole Guelph clan. “ Reve d’amour,” by
Lardelli, in many settings, is a pretty concert
piece. “Swanland,” a patriotic song, by J.
Summers, represents Perth s shout in
Hallelujah.
The worth of Melba as a valuable advt. for
Australia is only beginning to dawn on her com-
patriots ; she may always be relied on to keep her
native country in the public eye more attractively
than cargoes of frozen-mutton or royal-crested
carriage loads of Premiers. Melba is the best
Australian production up to date, and the only
Australian person who has got into the world’s
very front rank. As such, she is a sound asset,
and one which cost this country nothing. The
choice of the Victorian nightingale to sing at the
Jubilee Command night makes the fact of the
kawlinies being able to do one thing better than
any other country sink deep into the British
public’s soul. Nobody living can sing “ Lucia ”
like Melba, and to sing in Covent Garden on a
special night is about as great a triumph as a
woman can have in this world. It is a much
greater feat than making a record in reigning,
because the prima-donna whose voice soars through
the enormous building at about <£2oo per soar,
scores entirely on her own.
Since Florence Schmidt’s sudden leap into
notice, every girl with a voice is ambitious of
rivalling her, and diligently practises trills, runs
and shakes to the strengthening of her vocal
chords and the despair of her neighbors. All
sorts of recipes are dug up—such as sleeping with
a silk handkerchief around the throat a la Patti,
and a morning gargle of cold saltwater, guaran-
teed to clarify the voice and make it crystal-pure.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
, -A- weird, peculiar story is going round to
the effect that one of the daughters of a Melb.
dancing-academy has promised her hand in mar-
riage to a Great Unknown of noble Hinglish birth
and vast family possessions. Whether the allega-
tion came, in the first instance, from the lady
herself, or simply grew from a grain of gossip-
seed, is a mystery as dark as the identity of the
bridegroom-elect. Also there is no discoverable
suggestion of a reason why the matter should have
any mystery about it, considering that the heroine
talks of making her invisible Boy happv before
the end of the year, and must, consequently, be
up to her eyes in trousseau schemes. This is really
a phenomenal case of female secretiveness—to have
a brilliant marriage looming in the near future,
and yet to keep the name of the ardent lover a
secret, as though he were a sealed tender !
The Rechabi tes and sich should certainly
recognise Gov. Brassey’s services to the Cause.
It is true that when cyclist Carpenter brought a
despatch from Govt. House, Sydney, to Govt.
House, Melbourne, he was thoughtlessly provided
with a glass of beer, colonial beer; but when 300
jubilee firemen arrived at Brassey’s domicile in
torchlight procession, the other Monday night,
they were gratified to find that H.E. refrained
from offering them accursed drink of any sort
whatever. Had he tempted those 300 firemen,
many of them might have fallen. As it was, they
went back to duty strengthened by a practical
lesson in total abstinence, and feeling truly thank-
ful that they were travelling with a full band. At
such moments there is much consolation in a full
band.
“In the present scarcity of marrying Melb.
men it is painful to hear that Mr. L. A. Chaffey
(son of John R.) will presently meet his doom in
Maoriland. He has fallen captive to pretty Miss
Maud Butterworth, of Dunedin, and intends to
settle down at W ellington.
“An unfortunate little accident happened to
Lady Holled Smith on the occasion of the open-
ing of Vic. Parliament. The massive, golden-
haired lady left her bemedalled and plume-hatted
warrior on the steps to wait for Gov. Brassey,
while she went up towards the Council Chamber.’
But at the top of the steps she tripped over the
door-mat, and measured her full length upon it,
amidst the abominably rude laughter of a crowd of
smartly-dressed women near the door. She strode
off to the gilded Chamber looking very red and
angry. None of the men standing around were so
boorish as to laugh ; only the women. It takes a
woman—especially a well-dressed one—to malici-
ously rejoice at an undignified mishap to a better-
dressed woman.
“One of the weirdest of Melb. Jubilee decora-
tions was a transparency outside a leading
drapery. It professed to represent Britannia and
Victoria shaking hands; but the Victoria was ‘ a
caution.’ She was a young lady in short frocks,
with a chestnut-colored face; orange hair done up
behind ; and abnormal frontal development. She
had beside her a howling monstrosity for a kan-
garoo, with a scarlet back and a white paunch.
Britannia looked rather scared about the shaking
hands, and no wonder. But Art takes a back
seat when Loyalty appears.
