“ At the vice-regal reception on Jubilee day,- five
thousand persons—most of them heavily dressed
and befurred—struggled in through a narrow win-
dow, and every now and then were checked in
their furious endeavor by a functionary who barred
the way with a short cane. Why the Governor,
considering all the fine entrances which the V.R.
abode possesses, should have gone to the trouble
of haying a window removed from its frame and
insisting on his guests entering that way, very
much in the style of sheep in the drafting-yard, is
still a mystery. When entrance to the drawing-
room was at last gained by force, the V.R. party
was ‘discovered’ hoisted on a raised platform
and surrounded by all the official and ecclesiastical
dignitaries and their wives. Lady B. and Mrs.
Freeman Thomas departed from the custom firmly
held by the two previous V.R. ladies, and appeared
bare-headed with elaborately-barbered coiffures.
Lady B. was smartly dressed in her favorite’ black
moire antique with some rare lace forming a full
vest. Mrs. Freeman Thomas, who shook ex-
tended hands in a perfunctory way without
glancing at their owners, was attired in a gown of
black brocade which would have looked elderly on
her grandmother but for a smart little corselet of
pink satin covered with jewelled lace.
“ A band stationed aloft in the ballroom served
out all the entertainment offered to the guests that
afternoon. The women with hardly an exception
were well and fashionably dressed, but their hus-
bands and brothers stuck to the blue serge and
pot-hat they were used to ; not more than 50 in
de rigueur frock coats and silk hats to be
counted amongst the crowd. Magnates from the
backblocks on their first visit to Government
House were to be distinguished by the crochet
cravats wandering up the back of their collars,
the heavy, shapeless boots which resounded on
the polished floors, and the wildly bewildered gaze
they threw on the surroundings. Beauties were
rather abundant—“all women look well in furs ”
—but a young matron, in deep blue silk with an
antique beaten-silver belt and several other Pot-
teresque fixings, made a decided bid for her former
position of belle of smart gatherings.
“On Tuesday refreshments were plentiful and
decidedly more substantial than usual. The sand-
wiches and cakes were of the sort that are served
round in paper-bags at a Sunday-school picnic,
and the tea and coffee served in cups of an “un-
genteel ” vastness, but as many Melb. folk seem to
wish to make a meal at all official entertainments,
these facts were counted unto the Brasseys as
righteousness.
“A popular way of ‘doing’ the illuminations
was to make up a party and hire a furniture-van,
fitted up with forms in the manner sacred to
Sunday-school treats. Every available van was
pressed into the service, the vanmen asking and
getting readily a couple of pounds per evening: for
the vehicle. One lady in a fashionable suburb
was arranging for the hire of a van, and she asked
the owner how many it would seat. ‘ Well, mum,’
he replied, ‘it depends upon the company. If
they sits adjacent, the van will hold 20, but if
they sits familiar, it will hold 26.’
“Margined by empty chairs and with yards of
floor-space to spare, the official commemoration-
ball at the Town Hall was indeed a frost. His
Ex., as usual, put in a disconsolate and tardy ap-
pearance, after which things livened up a trifle
until the last trams left, and then ensued the ab-
surd spectacle of two sets trying to spread them-
selves over a desert of dancing area. The hop
fully deserved its pretentious title, for none but
officials were present, and city fathers, in knee-
breeches and point lace, were plentiful. Their
solemn splendor seemed to intensify the prevail-
ing depression. Many swell new frocks were
wasted on this wretched function, although the
viee-regals had evidently come prepared for the
worst. Lady Brassey, in a dowdy black garb,
sported a cream larnb’s-wool affair round her neck
all the evening, and waved an ugly green fan.
Mrs. Freeman-Thomas was in ditto-ditto, bright-
ened just a little in gold, and the Mayoress jubi-
lated in pale green and touches of red velvet.
“Suburban housekeepers look with suspicion
these days on any insinuating damsel, with her
hands full of tickets, whom they find on the front
doormat, for such a visitor almost invariably
makes an eloquent appeal for coin in the cause <if
charity. Yet there are humorous exceptions to
the rule. One seaside resident tells ruefully that
in answer to the unexpected query from one fair
stranger as to how many children she had between
the, ages of 5 and 16, she briefly replied : ‘ None at
all, ’ preparatory to closing the door with a bang.
Then when the questioner proceeded to say ‘ The
Council is giving a free entertainment to all the
children in this suburb, and I am just goinground
leaving the tickets,’ the mother of nine could only
smile vaguely and wring her hands behind her
back. For the past week she has lived in daily
dread that her defrauded olive branches will find
her out, and publish their grievance to the
neighbors.
“ Sympathetic journalists (of the sex that
smokes a pipe) are waxing eloquent over the
recent father-to-child cruelty cases reported in
Melb. and Sydney. An Akgus writer suggests
that the inhuman fathers be handed over to a jury
of. women for justice. As if honors were not
fairly even between the sexes in this matter.
What about that Irish vixen who tied her little
girl by the hands to a pig in the family wardrobe,
and left her there till she died from the mingled
effects of utter darkness and dread of being
nibbled by the gintleman that pays the rint !
And that Australian heroine we read of lately
who disciplined her darling offspring bv frizzling
sections of its flesh with a red-hot poker. In
these cases it seems highly advisable that the lady
torturers should get their deserts, or as much
desert as the law permits, from a jury of decent
average papas.

Every hanging Judge has his ghost.
Launceston, probably the best electrically-
lit town in this hemisphere, made a magnificent
Record Reign illumination. Electricity is cheaper
than gas, and loyalty is very largely a question of
cost. Even in Tasmanian towns the advertisers
made the grandest display.

That Dublin shindy was nothing more,
after all, than a Record-Reign celebration, trish-
fashion, wrong-side-up ; and Patrick—having re-
joiced exceedingly with a thick stick, and con-
siderably bashed head, and £5 or three months—
rests satisfied with having demonstrated that, at
all events, the “60 years' progress” has made no
difference in him.
N.S.W. Govt, has bought a statue of
Parkes from a gent in “rejooced circumstances.”
Name ? also, price ? The pedestal—it is to be ex-
posed to public horror, in the Centennial Park •
why not in the Zoo. ?—costs solus. “As the
price asked for the statue was low, the Premier,
Mr. G. H. Reid, obtained reports in regard to the
artistic value of the statue. From these reports it
appeared that though as a work of art it was not
perhaps first-class, still it was a good representa-
tion of Henry Parkes and suitable for erection
in the Centennial Park. It was, therefore,
purchased.” What a grim—and appropriate-
revenge on poor Parkes, the silly old dreamer,
who was always dabbling in, and paying huge
sums of Treasury money for, all sorts of artistic
nightmares !


Commerce depends on two governing fac-
tors : the sale of commodities, and the receipt of
the cash (or other commodities) which they repre-
sent. In times of depression like the present, both
processes are difficult of accomplishment—espe-
cially the latter ! But merchants who can sell
their goods need not be apprehensive of getting
paid for them if they will hand their accounts to
the Victorian Debt - Collecting Agency (Holds-
worth & Co.), 101 Swanston-st., Melbourne.*

The most inveterate practi-
cal joker in Vic. Assembly is
Alfred Deakin, among the tri-
umphs of whose life is reckoned
his elaborate hoaxing of J. L.
Dow, of Melb. Age. When Dow
and Deakin landed in ’Frisco,
about eight years ago, the fact was
chronicled in the ’Murkan dailies,
with the result that a constant
stream of peroxided damsels called
to interview the Australian poli-
ticians. One portly dame, handing
in a card bearing the name “ Mrs.
Muriel Donley,” unfolded to Dow
a scheme by which she intended
exporting Yankee girls to Austra-
lia, trusting in the novelty of na-
tionality for a special attraction.
The man of potatoes seriously in-
formed Mrs. Donley that the Vic.
Govt, would probably grant her a
bonus as the pioneer of a new in-
dustry, and there the incident
ended. But the wily Deakin,
who had been taking in the whole
thing with a twinkling eye, se-
cured the pasteboard for future
use, and three years later, when
both were sitting once more in
Vic. Parliament, a messenger en-
tered and gravely handed the
card to Dow. For a moment the
politician regarded it with a
puzzled air ; then light broke
upon him and he cleared out per
back door in the middle of an
important division. Every other
night for a fortnight, one of the
officials would tiptoe up to Dow
and tell him that a stout lady was
waiting to see him on urgent busi-
ness, and the member would melt
silently away with the utmost re.
gularity. At last, during an
afternoon sitting, came a telephone
message, ostensibly from David
Syme, stating that a lady from
■abroad had called at the Age office
to see Mr. Dow, and that, not
succeeding, she had installed her-
self in the proprietor’s private
office, absolutely refusing to leave
until her much-desired interview
eventuated. “Pray return at once,” concluded the
message, “and release me from this embarrassing
poseetion. ’’ With a cold perspiration on his brow,
t>ow took first tram to the Age office, and tremb-
lintrlv made his way to the sanctum of King
Dawvid. “Ah doan’t know what yeer talking
aboot Mr. Dow,” blandly remarked that deity ;

John Downer, the lazy leader of S.A.
Opposition, will be 53 on July 5. He is a native
of Adelaide and has sat in the Assembly con-
tinuously 7 for Barossa since April 1878, in which
year he became Q.C.

was made a Privy Councillor on June 22, which
was the 56th birthday of Srichard Baker, the man
he wanted to kill five years ago. Was this meant
as a reflection on Baker ?
The oldest bishop and the oldest actor in
Australia —Archbishop Murphy, of Hobart, and
Joe Raynor, of Surry Hills—were born on the day
of the Battle of Waterloo, 1815, and are now as
physically well as can be expected at 82.
Son of a nobleman, and great-grandson of
his celebrated military namesake—the Hon. Ralph
Abercromby, who “ passed-in ” the other day, at

Thomas Moore, the murderer, whose head
was judicially dragged off recently at Dubbo, was
a “ grave - and - reverend - seignior ”-looking old
fellow, with the features of an “eremite” and
the benevolently-flowing beard of a Sheikh-ul-
Islam. “ They run in all shapes ” —again !
Ayers’ Rock, that strange, smooth, gigan-
tic dome of rock in the Centralian desert, so well
described in Prof. Spencer’s “Larapinta Land,”
will shout old Henry Ayers’ name across weird
Amadeus to her sister sublimity, Mount Olga, long
after his paltry title and ephemeral legislatorship
have lost their virility.
There are rumors that David Syme (who,
Oy the way, is said to have acquired a big interest
in a Sydney paper) refused knighthood because he
deemed himself worthy of a baronetcy. The
style of the Age office illuminations would cer-
tainly not make it appear that David is, just now,
over-enthusiastic about Her Gracious Majesty.
Rupert Clarke took the seat of his father
in Vic. Leg. Council amid a salvo of “yah-yahs ”
from the Fats. Rupert is very much like Clarke
11., except for the beard and the bald head—which
latter feature is coming along rapidly. Srupert
has nice manners, and is well-intentioned and
good-natured—and will vote Fat on all occasions.
“Jimmy” Tyson’s confirmed celibacy is
regarded as a reaction owing to the persecution
his father suffered through having married against
his parents’ wishes. The elder Tyson was practi-
cally driven from pillar to post, finally finding his
way to Australia, where luck and he remained
strangers ever. “Jimmy” took his dad as a
lesson.

had a call from Gov. Hampden, very likely de-
clined a Jubilee K.C.M.G. - ship. He was
probably not offered a P.C.-ship (which he
Sresumably would have taken), because that gift
ad been reserved on the present occasion as an
ex-officio decoration for those who happened to be
Premiers.
A beautiful (and genuine-looking) Queen
Victoria yarn told to The Bulletin by a Maori-
land correspondent: —
As this is the season of “ Record Reign ” an anecdote
of a personal interview with Her Majesty may be of pass-
ing interest. I was invalided home from Burma in the
spring of ’B4, and during my residence of three years in
that country had been on more or less intimate terms
with a certain commissioner in whom H.M. happened to
be personally interested. This commissioner’s proceed-
ings (especially some of an unofficial character) gave rise
to a number of stories of a rather humorous nature, a
few of which I was in the habit of relating to my friends—
one story in particular, which we will call “The looking-
glass story,” generally having the effect of producing
much merriment. On my return to England some of
these yarns were, I found, vastly appreciated in
semi-official circles ; but I was certainly surprised and
rather non-plused when I received a communication
“ commanding ’’ my presence at Windsor on a certain
day of the following week. A court official called on me
beforehand, and after instructing me in the procedure to
be followed, intimated that the Queen would like to hear
from mv own lips an anecdote of the commissioner, suggest-
ing (as I understood him) “The looking-glass story” as
the one likely to be appreciated. In due course I arrived
at Windsor, was driven to the Castle and given lunch,
after which I was ushered into an ante-room, and
given further hints on procedure and etiquette
while waiting the Queen’s arrival. In a few minutes
entered Majesty herself accompanied by a royal
princess. I was graciously received, asked a few ques-
tions about Burma, and was then asked by the princess
if I would relate to H.M. an anecdote of the commissioner.
I proceeded at once to tell “ The looking-glass story.”
About half-way through I glanced up and saw, to my
dismay, a look of set sternness on H.M.’s face and con-
sternation engraven upon the faces of the bye-standers.
I at once saw that a mistake had occurred, but it was now
too .late to draw back, and I finished the yarn. A frigid
(it seemed eternal) silence greeted me when I had con-
cluded, broken at last by these words from the Royal lips :
“We are not amused ! ” lam afraid that my subsequent
withdrawal was performed in a manner more hasty and
less dignified than is prescribed in the court code of
etiquette.
•‘The boys about Cobram,” writes
“ Scotty the Wrinkier,” “are glad of
Rupert Clarke’s accession to dignity.
Among all the whalers I never heard
anything but good of him. They say
he’d rather eat a slice of damper and a

a levee.”
Apropos the Record Reign —
Queen Vic.toria was once fined 7s. 6d.
in the police-court. She didn’t appear
personally, and, the fine being paid
with costs, Her Gracious didn’t have
to take it out. The crime on that
occasion was something about “non-
registration of birth.” If she gets
into trouble this Jubilee year, however,
through letting the palace chimney
take fire, that past conviction will
doubtless come up against her, and
she will be severely dealt with as a
hardened offender. N.S. W. Judge
Gibson, under such circumstances,
would probably give her three years
to prevent her “preying on society”
any further. That good old phrase it
so beautifully comprehensive that pre-
sumably non-registration of birth is
preying on society.
If a vestige of “ Big ” Clarke’s
canniness remains in the family line, his
grandson, Rupert, will presently start
upon another long tour in the East,
also the West and North, leaving his
Victorian properties to be nursed back
to vigor. Then, after a while, he
might return and play the part of
public and private benefactor for all it
is worth. It is worth a good deal to
any reasoning mortal. Sirupert, who
probably couldn’t cut out much of a
career himself, has an attractive one cut
out for him—to do nothing in parti-
cular until he is in a position to dis-
tribute heaps of money every year,
after which a statue to his memory.
A junior arguing before the late Mr. Jus-
tice Innes, (N.S.W.), cited a case as “3Q.8.D.”
“ That is not the way to quote references to me,
sir,” said the Judge. Counsel protested that he
was only using the ordinary formula for the third
volume of the Queen’s Bench Division Reports.
“I might as well,” retorted his Honor, “ say to
you, U.8.D.”
The execution of ex-hangman Moore recalls
the official strangler in “ Barnaby Rudge,”who
used to poke his patients in the ribs, and slap
them on the back, and exhort them not to be such
cowards about a mere bagatelle like hanging ; and
who died such a miserable cur when his own turn
came to be scragged. Moore wasn’t taken that
way, however.
Chainey, the long-haired individual, pane-
gyrist of Walt. Whitman, and traverser of the
whole gamut of belief and unbelief from rabid
orthodoxy to Theosophy (through Atheism), who
visited Victoria in the boom times as a Spiritualist,
had, at latest, settled down into the ranks of the
“Christian Scientists” or “Mental Healing”
people who are now exploiting Australia.
Josiah Beard, convicted of the Streaky
Bay (S.A.) murder, and self-confessed assassin of
another mate of his, is entirely uneducated and of
a very low type. It is a strange feature in the
cases that, had this South Australian Butler
chosen to keep his mouth closed, the police would
never have unearthed the bodies, which were in
each case buried in the swampy quicksands in the
western scrub country, miles from any habitation.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
A Valuable Manuscript
has been discovered, and no doubt, its contents
will throw a clear light upon a question of great
interest and importance. This manuscript came
from H. E. P. McDonald, Esq., Brisbane, ex-
Mayor of Clermont, Queensland, and was evidently
written for the benefit of Australians. It reads:
—“Eor two years I suffered from indigestion,
and various medicines and doctors could give me
no relief. My appetite fell away, and I dreaded
taking a meal. I lost strength and energy ; passed
sleepless nights ; and was compelled to re-
sort to opiates and stimulants in order to
fulfil my daily duties. Life contained no enjoy-
ment for me, Then one day, about a year ago,
Warner's ‘ Safe ’ Cure was recommended by a
friend as a certain cure. I had little hope, so many
medicines having failed, but decided to give it a
trial. After taking one bottle a change for the
better became apparent. I continued taking this
health-giving preparation for about a month ; then
the world changed for me. The sun shone brighter.
Sleeplessness vanished, and life was a torrid
zone once more. I consider by recommending this
medicine that I am conferring a boon, not on its
proprietors, but on suffering humanity.” *

Cynical statement by Rupert Clarke
in submitting himself for election as a
Victorian M.L.C. :
I feel that it is not necessary for me at the
present time to enter into a detailed statement
of my political views.
Rupert Clarke, being a hereditary politi-
cian, has no need for political views, any-
how.
Maoriland is coming to the conclu-
sion that prohibition orders against drunk-
ards are a failure. When a person is pro-
hibited, he can’t get good liquor at any
hotel in the province if the hotelkeeper
knows him. But he can get bad grog at
any of the sly-liquor shops, and the num-
ber of prohibited persons is now so large
that it is worth while to run slygrog-
geries principally for the purpose of sup-
plying them with the worst kind of fusel
oil at exorbitant rates. This new feature
of the case breaks up all previous calcu-
lations, and leaves the inebriate as bad a
problem as ever.
The history of N.S.W. seems to
have had a lucky escape from Mr. Arthur
Galton. Comparing “ England’s Two
Great Queens,” Mr. G says that Eliza-
beth’s policy was always followed, and
that, where her Ministers didn’t agree
with her, events always proved them
wrong. This ! —in the face of accumula-
ted evidence that Burleigh and Walsing-
ham had a heart-rending job in steer-
ing their pig-headed and parsimonious
mistress clear of the idiotic courses she
wanted most of the time to follow. Eliza-
beth even wanted to lay up the English
fleet to save expenses, just when the Ar-
mada was about due ; and if she had had
her way the Spaniards might have reached
London practically unopposed. The only
“ greatness ” about Elizabeth was the
greatness of her advisers, and Victoria
doesn’t have even too much of that —but
the man who can sum-up a woman with-
out losing his head is even scarcer than
usual when the woman happens to be |a
queen.
Bundaberg (Q.) Guardian (on boss Tozer;:
He is at present the man in front; it was what he was
working for for many a year. He vanquished the Bris-
bane schoolboy, Tomjoe Byrnes, as M‘llwraith vanquished
Hume Black. And he now hungers for the golden apples
of success. This is the one and sole aim of the Queens-
land political Mephistophele3, and woe unto those who
stand in his way ; also, woe unto anyone else who places
any trust in him while he is pursuing his quest. Electoral
reform from Tozer ! Mercy from an avalanche !
The Jubilee ' ‘ honors 1 bring the seven
.Australasian Premiers to a level. They are all
titled grovellers ; some are K.C.M.G.’s and some
are P.C’s, and some are both, but everyone seems
to have grabbed with both hands at ali the title he
could get. “If you ever hear of me accepting a
title,” said Premier Reid a while ago, “you may
be sure lam a bit off.” And, as Reid has accepted
a title, the country thus has his own assurance that
he is “a bit off.”
Queensland Labor-party was once almost
exclusively Republican, but the recent Parlia-
mentary division on the grovelling address to
H.M. showed that it boasts that honorable Demo-
cratic distinction no longer. Northern and
Western members were mostly staunch as of yore,
but the Southerners . . After all, they were
even as their constituents. Nine-tenths of the
Queensland Democrats are in the North, Centre
and the Backblooks.

M.P. that Glassey was not really “re-elected
unanimously ” leader of the Labor-party Yet he
was actually so elected ! “ There was an intention
to propose another name ; noses were counted,
and it waS found that Glassey was ahead by one
or two. Thereupon, the second name was not
proposed, in order to make it appear that Glassey
was re-elected unanimously. ”
Mark Twain proposed to ridicule the
royalty and knighthood of the fifth century, by
making advertising media of the royal and loyal
processions, and rejoicings, and mail-clad warriors
of the time. The Record Reign illuminations of
last week, suggested the idea that Mark Twain
planted better than he knew, or else that all sense
of humor has long since died in Australian towns.
The Bulletin hears of several gaslight designs
like, “God Bless our Queen” and “Use——
Soap ” being shown on one shop-front.

Tory, Chinojapanesy province ought—scoops far
more than equal share of record-reign baubles.

son. Kissing goes by slaver
Melb. Post Office had a blazing “ Long to
reign over us ” on one of its facades on Tuesday
evening. Have the people who are responsible for
these things no sense of humor, or do they wish
the Queen to outlast the Empire ?

“ Unsolicited Offerings.—A Message from, the Queen.
She regrets that the rules respecting offerings from
private individuals with whom she is not personally
acquainted prevent her accepting such presents in
commemoration of the sixty years' reign.”
Vic. : • ‘ The Imperial authorities must feel
nasty over refusals of knightage. Here is Deakin
refusing long ago, under advice of Service —and
Berry. One thinks of the forty-first fauteuil of
the French Academy, with its Moliere and Balzac.
We muse over the fish that have escaped the net,
with such big, staring names as Higinbotliam and
Lalor. If it would not give added lustre to Higin-
botham ; if he lost nothing, if he gained nothing
. Why? A parlous question for the Eng-
lander.”
A stock remark amongst re-
cent arrivals from Africa is that
Johannesburg will be a fine place
for Englishmen “after the war.”
Nobody seems in any hurry to ex-
press an opinion as to what kind
of a place Johannesburg will be for
Englishmen during the war.

One of Queen Victoria’s most
marked interventions in politics
was the racking of old “Pam”
for expressing approval of the ’5l
coup d’etat at Paris. But no
sooner was it evident that Louis
Napoleon was going to remain oh
top than he was warmly invited to

cess of success !
N.S, Wales passes annually,
in round numbers, 45,000,000
“ newspapers ” through the post;
Victoria passes something loss
than half that number. This
practically illustrates the working
of free postage. Numbers of
papers go in N.S. W. as newspapers
which are not so in any proper
sense, and would be called by their
proper name if they had to carry a
stamp. Free postage allures every
trader into the realms of make-
believe journalism.
Yic. Labor-party doesn’t seem to be quite
a happy family. There are four rifts, anyhow,
represented by Trenwith, Prendergast, Hancock,
and Hamilton.
During the Address-in-reply debate in Q.
Assembly, the nose-ringed Ministerialists followed
the policy of silence that was so successful when
party points were being worked towards the end
of last session. Labor - member had to follow
Labor-member, and only at long intervals did a
Ministerialist stand up.
One outcome of the recent scatter of P.C.'s
among Australian Premiers will be that future
Premiers will stand out for similar recognitions.
A P.C. is supposed to have confidential inter-
course with fellow-councillors in all parts of the
realm, and thus, by one stroke, the Australian
outlook becomes as wide as the world.
England has been engaged, during the
Record-reign, in 38 fights—counted from the first
Afghan campaign to the recent Benin trouble,
and ranging in magnitude from this latter up to
the Crimean affair, which cost over £100,000,000 —
the figure for all more than doubling that
amount. “ God gave her peace—her land re-
posed ! ”
The S.A. representative on the Federal
Convention who spent least money on his election
was Glynn, 8.A., who disbursed £l6 17s. 4d.
The delegate who spent most was Solomon, tenth
on the poll, who officially returns his expenditure
at £595 12s. 2d. The direct Labor candidates, who
ran very close to the last man elected, spent
£5 7s. 7d. each.
Q. Labor-members, although invited to
Gov. Lamington’s Jubilee-day dinner, did not
respond. Par better this, than attending and
refusing to “ toast ” Her Gracious. The Brisbane
dailies are, nevertheless, slating them for not
having attended. As if a man, even in a slave-
country, can’t go without his dinner if he so
chooses ! The appetite o’ Douglas, also, is his
own.
The first good consequence of Queens-
land’s coming into the Sept. Federal Convention
should be the extraction from the assembled dele-
gates of definite opinions on the colored-labor
question. So far, .elegates have talked generali-
ties on the subject ; some have let slip opinions
which they afterwards tried to explain away.
Queensland in the Convention should dispel these
ambiguities.
The Argus treatment of Gillies is precisely
similar to its treatment of Patterson before the
last general election. It disowned Patterson
and refused to be associated with his failures; and it
now does the same with desultory Duncan, and
will not hear of him as a possible leader of the
Vie. Stupid party. The Argus reminds one of a
demented sow rending its own litter. It helped
to create Gillies, and now it tramples on him.
Australian Natives’ panjandrums are mov-
ing with a view to coming Vic. elections. Hain
is variously allotted to eastern suburbs and
Toorak; Watt is to throttle Prendergast at N.
Melb., much to Crosbie’s disappointment, who
has now to be content with W. Melb. and the
little doctor; Heide and Cleary are both spoken of
for S. Yarra against Surplus-tax Rogers. Just as
well; the A.N.A. conceit has been swelling visibly,
and a pin-prick or two will do good all round.
Horace Tozer, of Queensland, has got a
K.C.M.G.-ship, while Byrnes isn’t even men-
tioned as having got a good view of the Jubilee
procession from a window. Byrnes’s hungry
cadging for an invitation has, so far, brought him
neither title nor honor nor advt. Among the sort of
crowd which supports the Nelson-Tozer-Byrnes
combination a title really has some value, and
Tozer has distinctly got a start of Byrnes. Still,
the latter will get his revenge in good time. His
title compels Tozer to wear his front name of
Horace in full view, and no community trusts a
man with a front name like Horace when it is
constantly reminded that his name is Horace.
PROOF POSITIVE .
“ The cyclist always gets the worst of the collision. Why, the
last Johnny I upset only lost three teeth, and I had
no less than six spokes knocked out.”
Probably a majority of Q. Assembly will
insist on the establishment of schools of mines as
parallels to schools of agriculture. Also, the patri-
archal agricola, Daniells, M.P., wants central
flour-mills as parallels to central sugar-mills.
MAYOR IVES .
Q. Labour-leader Glassey is playing the
game of “ soft-sawder ” on the Government and
Ministerialists. His champions aver that he is
working a long-headed, cautious scheme; others
of the labour party, especially among those out-
side of the House, “ have no patience with him.”
N.S.W. Postmaster - General Cook ia
opposed to the system whereby newly-ap-
pointed Cabinet Ministers have to go before
their constituents for re-election. Cook himself
experienced a little awkward resistance on this
point. His case was just one of those against
which the Act was directed. When a man severs
his party allegiance to take a post the publio
should have some check. The trouble is that the
present check doesn’t amount to a row of pins.
“Nevermind” Nelson declares that the
series of good Governors that Queensland has had
forms “ the link between the people of Australia
and the Throne.” Humph ! The giddy, debonnair
Bowen, the n.s.f. Blackall, the stingy Cairns, the
mediocre Kennedy, the presumptive Musgrave,
and now—Lamington ! Whew ! Norman was
the only really respected Governor Bananaland
ever had, and he was deservedly idolised. If the
series is the “strongest link,” it would take an
eternity to make a chain. And this paper is glad
to know it.
Worth noticing that Boss Tozer, In Q.
Assembly, paid Labor-leader Glassey the courtesy
of immediately rising to reply to Glassey’s first
sessional speech. Premier “ Nevermind ” Nelson
was too “orty ” ever to recognise Glassey’s status
by such public observance of etiquette. If pre-
sent, Nelson would slumbrously corrugate himself
on his upholstery, and never say anything but a
sardonic “Ha, ha!” of sudden laughter—just
when nobody expected it. More frequently he
was amusing himself elsewhere with a clay-pipe
and a whisky.
The Vie. Trades Hall people find Railway-
Commissioner Mathieson “ a whale for boy
labor,” and begin to see that if the V.R. deficit is
to be wiped out under the present regime it will
be at the expense of the moiler. The Depart-
ment is advertising for laborers, porters, carriage-
cleaners, from 17 upwards, pay 2s. a day, with
promised increments that will bring the wages
up to .£1 4s. a week in four years’ time ! These
are what the Argus calls “commercial princi-
ples.” After all, sweating is the fundamental
commercial principle. It must be remembered
that Vic. Railway Department has already hun-
dreds of “passed” applicants on its books who
are supposed to be waiting for openings.


Messrs. Callaghan and Son, 395 Georg e-
street, Sydney, the reigning house for 50 years for
good boots, holds the record in their business at
this time of record reigns. The firm, to com-
memorate the event of the hour, has struck off a
bright and shining medal and attached thereto the
blue ribbon of a blameless life. By this means it
records its own record for good work, thoroughness
and age, and Victoria Guelph’s record for age. #
Grime and Smith, late of Beaupin, Mel-
bourne, ladies’ and gents’ hair workers, have a
private room for ladies at their attractive estab-
lishment, King-street, Sydney. Specialty fringes,
toupets, scalpets and everything of the hair-
worker’s ingenuity is made a study by the firm.#
The fact that “Our Chief” tobacco has
been placed upon the Australian market by the
progressive firm of A. Cameron, 10 Bligh-street,
Sydney, is guarantee of its being the finest tobacco
manufactured in America.#

A Boon to Sufferers.— A well-known stock-
dealer and storekeeper announces that, if written
to confidentially, he will reply and state the plan
he pursued by which he was restored to health
and vigor after years of suffering from Nervous
and Physical Weakness, Loss of Vigor, etc. He
writes : “I have no scheme to extort money from
anyone whomsoever. I was victimised by quacks
and empirics until I lost faith in mankind ; but,
thank heaven, I am now well, vigorous, and
strong, and anxious to make this certain means of
cure known to all. Having nothing to sell, I
want no money.” Address—J. KILIAN, Store-
keeper, Daveyston, S.A.*
The general feeling is that the best rail-
way man available in the world should be ap-
pointed, at whatever cost, to succeed Mr. Eddy.
The matter is not one involving £3500 or <£7ooo, or
even .£lO,OOO a year; it concerns the efficient
management of a capital of 38 millions sterling.
After two months wasted in N.S.W. Par-
liament making speeches for or against the
Federation Bill in general—what is it all about ?
The Parliament is to suggest amendments only.
It has no authority at this stage to either accept
or reject the Convention Bill, and members’
opinions on Federation in general are as utterly
useless and irrelevant just now as their opinions
about the lost grave of Moses. The correct course
was to go into committee on the details of the
measure right away, and all the oratory so far is
mere gas and wasted jaw and loose perfunctory
howl upon the desert air.
To enable people «o pass judgment on the
recent extensive sale, at an average of less
than a shilling a copy, of books from N.S.
Wales Public Library, it will be necessary
for an official list of the books in question to be
published. if, as is currently averred, the
volumes disposed of at the figure named included
complete old-time files of the S.M. Herald and
other Australian papers, together with copies of
the rarest old Australian books, the transaction in
question will need much explanation. It is stated
that many of the volumes have found their
way into the hands of the keenest and richest
Australian book-collector, whose library, the envy
of all connoisseurs, is so complete that he now
seldom buys any save the rarest of books.
“At the Colonial Club dinner,” writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ none
looked better or younger than old Sir George
Bowen, the ex-Victorian Governor, of gay reputa-
tion. The Marquis of Lome, now Governor of
Windsor Castle, was bloated, but eloquent; Sir
Saul Samuel, a picture of good nature framed in
his chin-and-jowl haloing beard, resembling the
frizzed hair of the stage Irishman, spoke tersely
and well, and looked decidedly Krugerish; Sir
Andrew Clark was portentously diplomatic, as a
long-winded pensioner must be; Tom Playford
and Reeves were absent; and * Rennie ’ Windeyer
—well, well—he sat all the evening, with much
starched shirt pushed out over his plate, jaws
clamped, and lips set like a fine-steel toothless gin-
trap, and dined unmoved. At last his turn came,
and he rose. He spoke for a Jubilee trinket, and
maybe got it [No, Sir.—Ed- B.], though he half
sounded the only harsh note —compared to the
monotonous chorus of everlasting loyalty—of the
evening. ‘lf England did not treat her colonies
of Australasia considerately ’ —the threat was not
completed. ”
“ There is much dissatisfaction at the Aus-
tralian Agents-General offices over seats for the
Ju bi lee processi on,” writes The Bulletin s London
correspondent. “It was hoped that a stand would
be set apart for colonial dignitaries and indignita-
ries, so that a specially topical ovation might burst
on the Empress-Queen’s ear-drums. But the
Home office feared the wildly loyal yells of the
Coo —ee colonists would so upset the old lady
that she might turn back or refuse to go on
or order up the gory heads of the two chief
offenders on a fish-plate. So they sold—are you
listening, sentimental, imperial-federationists?-900
seats to the combined ‘colonies,’ including Canada
and the Cape. These seats are spread along the
route, some here and more there and several
nowhere in particular. Many of the stands are
already erected (21st May) and, thanks to the
L.C.C., fine, solid structures they are; but they
won’t accommodate half the people that would pay
to get on them.”
George Westinghouse tried to sell his patent
railway brake for .£6OO, but could not find a pur-
chaser. In the subsequent ten years it brought
him in three millions of money. There are many
other things still to be invented just as valuable as
a railway brake. But many inventors have lost
their patents through defective specifications,
claims, etc. This risk can be avoided by inventors
consulting Mr. Arthur Griffith (M.L. A), Patent
Agent, Sydney, who will not only do the work with
exactitude, but at rates within the reach of every
inventor. For instance, .£2O will secure Pro-
visional Protection in the whole of the seven
colonies of Australia and Great Britain, and
Foreign Patents can be obtained through him at
similarly reasonable rates.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*

SPOUTING NOTIONS.
The V.R.C. Grand National meeting, which
opens on Saturday, should draw big’ger crowds than have
patronised the Flemington winter carnival for some
years past. Final acceptances for the big hurdle-race,
due to day (July Ist), promise to leave nearlv 30 candi-
dates on the card ; maybe a field of 25 will actually start
I° r i , u ’’ ll . ue journey. It is a long journey, and the
enci thereof seems more than usually difficult to guess at.

disappointed backers (for the hurdle-race) saw him win
the steeplechase handsomely.
Ihe Manifolds’ stable will have Soult for its
champion this time. A good-looking 4-year-old, with
a fairly-light weight to carry, Soult is bound, bar acci-
dents in the meantime, to start favourite for the G.N.
Hurdle Race, but his recent winning-form gives no
guarantee that he will stay the distance. So-and-So, a
black gelding from Ballarat, seems quite as likely to see
the battle through to the bitter end, and if he should
prove as stout as the betting-market suggests, the pace
that enabled him to best Aurum in the autumn will bring
him ahead of Soult and everything else. The mighty
Miller stable doesn’t look very dangerous for the Hurdle
Race. Hive has not done well of late, and Ripple is sore.
All the same, the mare, if started in preference to Hive,
may give followers of the red and white a good run for
their money.
Owner Glasscock reckons old Larrikin fast
enough to carry list. 81b. victoriously over the hurdles,
so the brilliant ’chaser is pretty certain to cut a good
figure at the finish. The Bulletin, expecting anything
to romp home, will give special chances to So-and-so,
Borderer, Larrikin, Exchange, Soult, Ripple, His Grace,
First Ascot, Carbonate, and Legs. Boiling these down to
So-and-so, Exchange, Borderer, Larrikin, and Carbonate,
this journal votes for them scepticallyin the order named.
Consideration of the G.N. Steeplechase chances will keep
until next week. Meanwhile, The Bulletin may as well
repeat its hint of two or three weeks ago—that Zouroff is
a good one when ripe. And they are getting him ripe.
Considering the present dearth of really tried
stayers, the bookies are fairly liberal in laying 1000—1 the
two Cups, and already several supposed “ rods in pickle”
have been snapped up; O’Trigger and Positano being
especially voted “ good things ” by the man at the
corner. In Caulfield Cup the N.S.W. contingent has been
very fairly dealt with by the handicapper, and True Blue,
Positano, Hopscotch, Sayles, Valiant and Hindoo will
have most to fear from The Chevalier, Mora, Resolute
and Response. In Melb. Cup, Reka, True Blue, Toreador,
Amberite and Hopscotch should worthily represent the
mother province—having most to fear from O’Trigger,
Pivot, Alabaculia and YVait-a-Bit, who will perhaps im-
prove even on his Australian Cup form.

Mr. T. N. Fitzgerald, the eminent Melbourne
surgeon who races as “Mr. T. Naghten,” is one of the
jubilee knights.
* * « «
At length the starting-machine (Gray’s) is to be
given a fair trial at the headquarters of the English
Jockey Club. Vexatious false starts, the bane of English
racecourses, will soon be but a memory of the past.
* * * •
The Tas. stayer, Benedict, who performed
creditably at V.R.C. Autumn meeting, has done a good
preparation “on the quiet,” and is whispered as a good
thing for to-day’s (Sat.) V.R.C. G.N. Hurdles, in which he
is weighted at lost. 91bs., not prohibitive considering his
fair performances on the flat.
♦ * *
Re “Surcingle.” “They run in all shapes’’
was a hackneyed expression at ’Ome long ere Don Juan’s
Cup year (1873). It is generally attributed to the famous
trainer, John Scott, of Whitewall, the wizard of the north,
but doubtless Noah made the same remark to Mrs Noah
when the animals scooted from the Ark.
* * * *
The reciprocity as regards disqualifications,
which is supposed to exist between all Australian racing
clubs, is evidently a sort of fiction, for one of the most
prominent figures on N.S.W. courses to-day is a man re-
cently warned off Flemington by the V.R.C. The scribe
of one of the Melb. dailies who chronicled this fact was
met one night by an irate friend of the man standing
down, and was emphatically stoushed by him as a protest
against the freedom of the press.
* * * *
During the N.S.W. parliamentary discussion on
betting and racing nothing was so common to hear as the
wisdom of leaving all to the A.J.C. But has that body
proved worthy of such confidence ? Is it not a fact that
the whole evil arose from the club’ssupinenesswith regard
to the pony business ? We are told that the clubites would
spend all the money that went into their coffers on racing.
Judging by the present state of the Distressed Jockeys’
Fund, they would take their time over it. The club, as a
club, makes no money directly out of racing, but, unless
rumor lies, individual members do so by crooked means.
Then why should a representative body like Parliament
implicitly trust a proxy-recruited executive? If the
reform of racing is to be left to the A.J.C., that body
itself will have to be reformed, and in no piecemeal fashion
either
* * * *
One of Sydney soup-piave courses recently
gave forth that it would allow all sorts and conditions of
disqualified mokes and owners to run, a statement that
brought forth the ukase from Kensington that it would
disqualify all ponies running on a course which recog-
nised the cronk ’uns. This settles the matter, as a pony-
club tabooed by Kensington might as well shut up shop.
Dear Bulletin, — Is your sporting man correct
in saying that “ Tasmania is the first Australasian pro-
vince to give formal Parliamentary sanction to gambling
for private advantage ” ? Queensland has had legalised
totes for some years now ; the winners get their “ private
advantage,” the “toters” theirs, the club that owns the
ground its, and the Government its.— Dipso.
* * * •
While Prince Tummy and a Prime Minister
keep on winning Derbies, the saints have “Buckley’s”
show of suppressing horseracing and gambling, on which
racing admittedly depends.
* * • *
Australian boxers had a bad time last week in
Eng., and the U.S. “Starlight” went under to one
Chrisp, on a foul ; Tom Williams was beaten by Tom
®y al ? m two rounds; and the hitherto invincible
Griffo” succumbed to Young in three rounds. No
details are to hand, but Qriffo, although kept off grog by
quod,” could not have been in the best of trim after
months of prison diet. Griffo’s hitting was never of the
smashing sort; but his phenomenal cleverness has always
hitherto enabled him to make a draw of it, even on
top of the maddest “ burst.”
* * * •
The disqualification of stousher Starlight on a
foul, during his fight with Chrisp, at Newcastle, Eng.,
recalls the nigger’s many Australian contests which
ended unsatisfactorily. Whereas the average pug. con-
tents himself with pounding his adversary’s jaw as often
as possible, Starlight was addicted to introducing floor-
wrestling into the game. He would also frequently prod
opponents in thestomach with his copious foot and haul
on their ears during these sand-scrambles, and, when the
ring had been rushed and the parties were pulled apart
with a pop, Starlight would invariably come forward to
the ropes and say he was only a poo’ iggerunt nigger,
who wanted to fight fair for a livin’. He would add that
the cullud man cud’nt get fair play, and would almost
burst into tears as the referee declared him guilty of a
foul. Ten to one Starlight delivered the same old speech
at Newcastle
* * * •
It now appears that poor Charley Cavill’s stop-
under water records were made with the assistance of an
inverted barrel sunk before the show began, and it is
supposed the air in the cask became contaminated with
gas, the baths in which he performed being filled from
some natural-gas wells.
* * • »
Yankeedom kept up its reputation for wooden
nutmegs by rigging a bogus kinetoscope of the Fitz-Cor-
bett fight. Two bruisers as near like the two men as
could be attained were readied-up as Fitz and Corbett
and went through a “schlenter” fight, finishing with
Fitz’s historic heart-blow. The show was said to be for
Australia, but the crowd gave itself away by show-
ing first in the States, where some men who had seen the
real Roger exploded the fairy tale. The promoters de-
served a better fate.
* * * *
Norwood beat youth Adelaide on 22nd June —
5 goals 6 behinds to 4 goals 6 behinds. Attendance esti-
mated at 12,000—a record for three years. Adelaide foot-
ball is apparently being made to order. Norwood and
Port are level for top, the allegedly invincible “ Souths”
second.
• * * •
Another new sculler. Tressider, of Newcastle,
N.S.W., is anxious for a match with the burly Jem Stan-
bury. Tressider, under Chris. Neilson’s tuition, is said to
be a coming man, and was anxious to row Towns before
the latter’s departure.
* « * *
Ere Towns had time to get on a match with the
minor talent, and thus win a little English gold, he was
challenged by his countryman, Wray.
* * * *
People in Newcastle (Eng.) think Wray the
greatest sculler seen on the Tyne since Hanlan in his
palmy days. Those who know say that there is much in
common between the two—that Wray is phenomenally
fast for a mile or so, but no stayer. Of course, his great
speed would enable him to cut down any ordinary man
who tried to live beside him, but a sticker like Beach
would eventually find out the weak spot. It is said that
Towns can stay for a week.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League is losing all
sight of the goose that lays the golden egg. The league
won’t pay top-notchers to race, therefore many of these
won’t race ; consequently the public, which pays every-
body, won’t pay to watch a 19-event programme consist-
ing mostly of second and third-class handicaps. The very
name “second and third-class” would down anything.
Nobody wants second or third-class sport, or whisky, or
honeymoon, or tobacco, or anything else. At present the
league’s best prizes are annexed by the aged dodderer or
third-class derelict whom somebody calls Tommy or
Jorgey or Jim or Bill. The public has quite enough of
the dodderer and the derelict and of Tommy and Jorgey
and Jim and Bill in its own immediate family, and
therefore it is not going to rush miles to Sydney Cricket
Ground for the sake of forking out 2s. to see its own
family derelicts come out and wobble and wobble and
wobble. The public always avoids its own relatives when
it wishes to enjoy itself.
The above remarks just about say all that’s
worth saying concerning the League’s V.R. meeting. The
relieving feature, when it did come, usually arrived in the
form of a race that was more third-class, or more aged, or
more derelict, or more Jim or Bill than a preceding race.
For instance, the “ Ordinary Handicap.” What a name 1
The starters were Harry, Charley, Bill, Jack and Fred.
Fred, rode well until he got a pain in the stomach, when
he fell off and called for Bob or Tom. Thereupon Bill
spurted after Harry; while Jack and Charley spurted
after Bill. It was no good. Harry, an aged wood-
chopper, won with a lot in hand.
The riding of Body and Payne deserves praise.
Both made a wretched error of judgment in the Victoria
Wheel Race. The pair could have nipped first and second
prize had they engineered each other. But their error,
bad as it was, did not reach the badness of the errors of
Jim and Bob and Bill. Body and Payne rode a magnifi-
cent finish in the International Scratch Race, the race of
the day ; Payne won by a few inches. Altogether, the
League is in a worse way since its secretary, Percy, gave
place to Freddy.
* * * *
The League of Vic. Wheelmen made a painful
display of grovelling injustice re an enquiry into the
Williamstown club case, a month ago. The club held an
unregistered meeting, whereat five leaguists raced in de-
fiance of a warning from the Council. Four of the cul-
prits were accordingly disqualified for three months—a
bogus sentence considering the time o’ year—whilst the
fifth, after “admitting; that he had ridden with a full
knowledge of the rule, and the warning of the League
executive” escaped censure. The favored one was Geo.
Turner, son of the Vic. Premier. The League couldn’t
bring itself to disqualify a rider whose papa had gone
’Ome to play a representative part in the blessed jubilee.
* * * *
Constable McQuaker, who helped to look after
Butler during the trial, is about the biggest man in the
N.S.W. force, and as good-tempered as he is strong ; he
was considered no end of a wrestler in the Old Country.
The first capture McQuaker made after joining the
Sydney police was a typical five-foot Bst. larrikin. Mac.
was leading him along by the collar, when the little ’un
wrenched himself free, and catching the big man by the
legs, flung him clean. The giant was so flabbergasted
when he rose that he stood awhile speechless, looking at
the midget, who did not attempt to b( It. “ Well, I didn’t
think there was a man in Australia could put me fair on
my back, and that did it. I’m sorry, my boy, I have to
take you along. I’d really like to let you go.”' And when
before the courts he told the story, and asked the bench
to let the prisoner down easy.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Gun Club's quarterly handicaps
are out, and Mr. Herbert Garratt is placed at the top of
the lists on 32 yards with Mr. D. Mclntosh, who is just
now carrying all before him in Europe. Mr. Garratt,
whose health has not been the best of late, intends
going to England later in the year, and as he is sure to
take his gun with him, he will probably show the
Britishers that the doughty Donald is not Australia’s only
star shot.
* * * *
In England, professional running is looking up.
The reasons are that last year several of the most pro-
minent “amateurs” were disqualified and have now
joined the other branch. Perhaps the disqualification of
a few Australian amateurs, who, if rumor be true, would
be none too harshly treated, might revive the departed
glory of Botany and Carrington.
* * * *
A tip for peds with vitality enough left to raise
a trot. In Eng. the pros, instead of running for backers
—usually bookmakers and promoters get up small
handicap sweepstakes and divide the gate-money as per
arrangement. The gains are small but sure, and the
elimination of the gaffer element ensures public con
fidence.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —The disparity in weight be-
tween the English and Australian anniversary eights,
which your correspondent infers is an example of Austra-
lian decadence, is probably more an example of British
pig-headedness which the Australian Universities are just
beginning to shake off. If there is any proverb which the
athletic Britisher sticks to it is that a good big man is
better than a good little man, and in his dreary reitera-
tion of it he falls into the error of thinking that a bad
big man is better than a good little man It only needs a
glance at any athletic sport going—cricket, football, row-
ing, cycling, lacrosse, running or gymnastics—to prove
that the list, man is, in 99 cases out of 100, better than
the 12st. man In boxing, the authorities and experts
have acknowledged this within 41b. by fixing list. 41b. as
the middle-weight limit, and, by so doing, declaring that
if a man weighs list. 4lb. he is fit to fight anything that
can don a glove. The supremacy of Fitzsimmons and
Corbett, comparatively light men among the heavy-
weights, is enough to prove this. But calmly ignoring
men’s ability in the boats, the Universities of Australia
have, for years past, been lumping eight 13-stoners
into a boat, under the blissful belief that they were pick-
ing their eight best men. And all the remaining oars-
men in the different provinces have been laughing in their
sleeveless arms and sending out their nine-stone maiden
eight men to show the Universities what a little speed
was like. In England, apparently, the same course is
pursued, for, year after year, the weights of the Oxford
and Cambridge crews are such as would equal four ordi-
nary Australian senior eights, yet, in rowing circles at
’Ome, the ’Varsity oarsmen are reckoned a very second-
rate crowd —albeit they have here and there produced
some top-notehers. Here, the University rowing clubs
are “mug” clubs pure and simple, and their starts for
regatta races merely serve to keep down the exes by the
contribution in entrance-fees. The last year’s interpro-
vincial University race, however, appears to show that
they have tired of the old English beef-worship, and have
determined to follow the other clubs in picking smart,
young, agile. 11-stoners in place of the sluggish jumbos
that have hitherto acted as ballast. Probably the two
best eights Australia ever produced were the Yarra Yarra
Champions, with only one man touching the 12st., and
the Wendouree “snipes,” in which every man was list,
or under.— McG.
* * * »
The old belief that a racing cyclist is an old
man at 25 has been shattered by the experience of the
past racing season. Bilmartin, whose age is best repre-
sented by X, was looked on as a veritable phenomenon
until the arrival of Lesna, who breaks records with a
third of a century behind him, and who never crossed a
machine until he was 28. A glance at the cracks of
Europe and America shows men of all ages between 17
and 35 performing equally well.
• • * •
At Lancaster Park (M.L.) recently, a new form
of bicycle-racing was introduced, the wheels being more
or less oblong instead of circular, thus producing an effect
mightily like racing on buckjumpers of the most virulent
type over ploughed fields sown with brickbats.
* * » •
Cyclist C. O- Tebbutt has been talking to a
pressman, and amongst other items stated that he was
not an Australian, and was unashamed of the fact. Teb-
butt is the pattern racing-cyclist of this land. He is a
Rechabite, non-smoker, non-bettor, and a Sunday-school
teacher. He comes from England. No Australian ever
possessed so many of the cardinal virtues at once.
* * * •
A new bike-ppdalling system, invented by an
Ashburton (M.L.) man, is said to have been tested satis-
factorily. The process is something after the style of
operating a sewing machine. It is not so tiring, and
enables one to travel a longer distance.

At the N.S.W. League Carnival, the Victoria
Wheel-race and the ten-miles International Scratch-race
were won on Dunlop tyres.*
* * * *
At Perth four finals in one day were won on
Beeston-Humbers, and Payne last Saturday, at the
N.S.W. Wheelmen’s meet, won the 10-mile International
Scratch-race on a Beeston-Humber.*


Gov. Ranfurley has also been dubbed
K.C.M.G. Originally, that title was intended to
crown a life of colonial service. Of late years,
however, it has been conferred on men who had
never performed any signal service in the empire.
Gerard Smith and Lamington received the title
almost simultaneously with their appointment to
Australian posts, the assumption probably being
that such men must distinguish themselves, and
that to pay in advance was a mere matter of form.
Roberts’ Hotel, George and Market Strs.,
Sydney, always in the front rank, has come a
large step more for’ard with the recent fine altera-
tions. The reception and ladies’ and gents’ coffee
rooms are a big draw in themselves. Likely
visitors to Sydney should take careful note and
unlikely visitors careful note also.*

The Hotel Australia, Sydney, does not
trade upon its visitors with “ extras.” The morn-
ing cup of tea or coffee is not an “ extra ”; the
afternoon cake and tea are not an “extra”; the
supper which is on hand till midnight is not an
“extra.” It is a great comfort for a visitor to
know that the wing of a fowl can be picked at
11 p.m. without incurring the vexatious “extra”
charge. The rate, 12s. 6d. per day upwards, is
inclusive of all meals and attendance. A hungry
man can, practically, have six meals a day at the
Australia while paying only for three.*

Hobart City Council has a corporation
snuff-box which is passed round at all aldermanio
meetings. If ever an alderman shows signs of
protesting against the old-established order of
doing nothing, the Mayor passes the snuff-box
with a courtly grace, and the “ ayes ” have it be-
fore the objector has done sneezing"
From Richmond River (N.S.W.) Ex-
press
A cattle-slut had pups recently, and suckled them for a
time. Meanwhile, a sow on the same farm had a litter,
and, as is usual, there was a runt amonast them. By
some means this runt became acquainted with the slut,
and the owner was astonished to observe the little
grunter placidly sucking the slut, which appeared
thoroughly satisfied. Being interested, he watched the
progress of affairs and observed that the slut would not
permit her natural offspring to partake of what by the
law of nature belonged to them ; but encouraged and
permitted the young pig to imbibe, and the latter has
developed and made rapid growth since obtaining the
whole of the slut’s milk to his own cheek. The owner
says that the only way he can account for it is that the
mother received very rough usage from her progeny,
whereas little jjjggie was gentle in manipulating.

Two young people on the M.L. West Coast
lately struck a patch of ill-luck from which they
will never wholly recover. They applied to a
parson to get married, and the reverend sent them
to the registrar for the necessary license. The
license was obtained, but in their harmless ignor-
ance they imagined that it constituted a marriage
and lived happily together for a month on the
strength of it. 'They might have continued doing
so for years, only the pastor made inquiries after
the pair that never came back, and explained the
awful situation to them, and, as soon as the young
wife could be recovered from her faint they were
rushed off to the church as unobtrusively as pos-
sible, and matters were put right.
X.Z, : “Coincidence. Collins, the first
who tried to make Sorrento, Vie., a local habita-
tion worth inhabiting, abandoned it 24th Jan.,
1804. On the same day this year I read on the
Ozone a notice that Coppin, the second to try to
make the glaring white limestone hog-back habit-
able, has given up the attempt, and is selling off
all his Sorrento properties. Collins, by the way,
gave out that he abandoned the attempt at settle-
ment for lack of water, yet springs are common as
sand-flies, shallow wells all over the place fill with
fresh water, and every southerly or westerly squall
brings rain. Wherefore the lack-of-water business
is too thin. But when making the Nepean-road—
the military road from Melbourne to the Heads
on the east (Sorrento) side of the bay—the road-
makers dug up numerous human skeletons buried,
lyrnn on their backs. At first these were duly
inquested, but the numerousness of inquests got
so ungovernable at the site of the Collins settle-
ment that, to save delays, orders were given to
laborers that when they found bones they had
better re-bury them, as too much bone-finding
would result in ‘the sack.’ The blacks in that
district in those da,ys never buried their dead on
their backs. Putting Collins’s desertion alongside
those skeletons, the most probable conclusion is
that Collins tried the ‘ dispersal ’ system and got
‘dispersed’ himself.”

Some of the S.Q. sugar districts are ter-
rible examples of the effect of the denudation of
trees on climate. Regions which formerly boasted
mild winters are now ravaged by frost, and the
hapless planter finds that the scrubs he so ruth-
lessly destroyed were really the grand modifiers
of the intense cold of June and July. A few belts
of scrub left standing here and there would have
made all the difference.
Reply of Royal Humane Society's official
to parson, who wrote complaining that society’s
medal had been conferred upon a man of criminal
antecedents:—
Sir,—The person you mention has no antecedents. He
wasn’t born, as far as this society is concerned, until he
performed the act for which he has received its decow
tion.

Fitzgerald Brothers Combined

THEIE lIsTTEUTIOUS.
I3ST WIUTEE QUABTEES.
(From the Albany Advertiser, W.A., Saturday, May 29, 1897).
The season 1896-97 of this well known circus
ceased at Albany, on May 25, after an 18 months
tour of Australasia. It will be remembered that
these enterprising Australian showmen set sail
from Auckland, New Zealand, in June, 1895, and
travelled round the world in search of novelties
for their Australian patrons. The success of their
new London Company which they brought back
with them was tremendous. Everywhere they
were greeted with highest encomiums from both
press and public, and among the artists engaged
were—Mdlle. Antonio from the London Aquarium,
Professor Peart, “ The champion high diver of the
world,” the marvellous brothers “Eclair” from
Paris, La Belle Rose, the acrobatic Altons and
Mahomet “The Talking Horse.” These attrac-
tions in the Australian Colonies proved the judg-
ment of the energetic brothers, being met on all
sides with unbounded enthusiasm. Their name is
a household word throughout the Australias, and
well do such managers deserve it. The last few
months have been spent in a tour of Western Aus-
tralia ranging from Fremantle to Coolgardie, Kal-
goorlie, Bulong, Kanowna, Broad Arrow, thence
to the Murchison, Cue, Mount Magnet, the inter-
vening towns being visited with undoubted success.
In fact, the whole trip has been a triumphant
success. Money flowed into their coffers and the
public generally expressed themselves as highly
delighted with the combination brought to their
doors. To come right down to the present, Mr.
Dan Fitzgerald, the senior partner of the firm,
left Albany on Saturday, April 17, by the R.M.S.
Ophir on another trip round the world in order to
secure the best artistes and artists obtainable for
an extended tour of Australasia. Any novelties
or sensations, which money can purchase, are to
be arranged for. Mr. Fitzgerald landed at Naples
on May 14, thence he proceeded to Vienna and
Berlin where he is at present located and where
he is advertising throughout Germany and the
Continent for talent. His intentions are to engage
nothing but the very best talent available for the
forthcoming Australasian tour. From Bei-lin he
will next proceed to Copenhagen, Paris, Belgium,
Spain and locating for a time in Madrid, later on
will travel through Barcelona, Valencia, &c. In
fact, wherever a performance of any description is
going on, whether circus, theatre,' continental or
outdoor exhibitions, Mr. Dan will be on the spot
looking for the latest attractions. From there he
will proceed to England, travelling the provinces,
Scotland and Ireland, then back to London, where
his headquarters will be the Era office. From
this well-known centre he will send to Australia
the pick of English and continental artists ; and
should he succeed in perfecting his plans, he may
also send performing elephants and other animals,
which he thinks he may be able to secure from
Haughenbeck, of Hamburg. The whole of the
combination will first arrive in Albany, where a
general reorganisation will take place and the
initial bow to the people of Australia will be made
here. Mr, Tom Fitzgerald is located here in
winter quarters. A farm has been purchased H
miles from Albany, and during the residence here
of Mr. Fitzgerald and his trainers fresh horses will
be broken in, new waggons and animals’ cages
built, saddlers, harness-makers, wheelwrights,
blacksmiths, painters, decorators and upholsterers
will have their time fully occupied in making the
necessary preparation for the opening of this
entirely new combination. In the initial opening
of a huge concern of this description—or we should
say re-opening—everything will and must be new,
bright and attractive. Mr. Tom Fitzgerald will
personally superintend the whole of the arrange-
ments, so tlmt we may expect to see the show of
1897-98 a bigger success, and of even greater
magnitude, than that recently provided by these
energetic caterers. It is proposed to inaugurate
the season on or about September 1,1897, and with
such an amount of energy and vigor displayed
and capital used freely, the combination should
prove a tremendous draw for the amusement lov-
ing public of Australasia. The general intention
is to play throughout the colonies, New Zealand,
and Tasmania, the Fitzgerald Bros, using their
own special trains and steamers to accomplish the
proposed tour, so that when it is announced that
the Fitzgerald Bros, show is coming it may lie
expected with confidence that it is a show which
has never been equalled, bristling with novelties
from every country. No abler man than Mr. Dan
Fitzgerald could possibly be selected to engage
talent from the old world, as his knowledge of the
tastes of the colonial public for which he has so
long and successfully catered should be a sufficient
guarantee of ability in that direction. The band
next season will be under the able conductorship
of Herr Von Der Mehden, the popular cornet
soloist, and will be in every respect better than
ever. The agents department will again be under
the supervision of Mr. John Morris, undoubtedly
the “ Prince of agents,” with a well qualified and
additional staff. The printing will also be a
feature of next season, all new pictorial and suit-
able matter being specially imported from England
and America. Mr. Frank N. Jones will retain
his old position as press representative, and we
feel sure that all the old friends of the press and
a goodly number of the public will do all in their
power to further the interests of these leading
Australian showmen.
A Sydney Washerwoman’s .trials,
HARDSHIPS MET WITH IN COLD
WEATHER.
HANDS IN HOT WATER, THEN IN
COLD.
GETTING HEATED, THEN GOING OUT
OF DOORS IN ALL SORTS OF
WEATHER.
COUGHS, COLDS, AND RHEUMATISM
THE INEVITABLE RESULTS.
In this inclement weather, when so many are
suffering from colds, coughs and rheumatism, the
story of Mrs. Mary Brown, of 71 Simmons-stivct,
Enmore, will be read with devouring interest.
When a newspaper reporter called at this lady's
residence, he found her busily engaged at the w ash-
tub, what she called doing a “day’s washing.”
It was indeed difficult to believe by the amount of
“ elbow grease ” she laid into her labours that this
interesting personage had lately been informed by
medical men that hers was a case which would
probably develop into chronic rheumatism.
“ I have called, Mrs. Brown,” said the reporter,
“to ascertain the truth of a rumor that has
reached me of your having recently suffered from a
severe attack of rheumatism.
“ It is quite true, but I am now happy to relate
that I feel as well as I ever did. I have been
suffering on and off from rheumatism for the last
five years, and the slightest change in the weather
used to give me most awful twinges. It chiefly
affected my arms, and I can assure you, sir, that
it took me all my time to do my hair. As to lift-
ing this tub, that was a matter of sheer impos-
sibility. If it had not been for some of my friends
I could never have gotten through my household
duties; This complaint appeared to affect my
entire system, and, as to the money I have spent
in doctors’ advice and so-called remedies, why, it
makes me positively disgusted to think of. I
began to think that if my sufferings continued, life
was not worth living. I remember on day, as I
was sitting by the fire and endeavoring to employ
my mind by reading the Evening News, that an
advertisement caught my eye headed ‘ Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills for Pale People ’ cures rheu-
matism, sciatica, &c. Now, having wasted so
much money already, I was at first loath to try any
more advertised medecines, but I thought in for a
penny in for a pound, so I invested in three boxes
of this specific. ”
“I hope you were careful to ask for Dr. Wil-
liams’ Pink Pills ? ”
“ I took particular notice to do that, for I had
read of so many worthless imitations that I was
careful to ask for and obtain the genuine article. ”
“We think it highly probable, Mrs. Brown,
that before long some of the compounders of these
worthless imitations will be spending time in
seclusion, with the accompanying eccentricities of
diet, official garb, and escort. ”
“And, indeed, it would only serve them right.
Brown (my husband) laughed at me when I told
him I was going in for a course of Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills, and said, ‘ Oh, you are going to chuck
away some more money, are you ? ’ ”
“ I said ‘Yes, I am,’ and right glad am I that I
ran the risk of * chucking away some more money,’
for I am happy to tell you that they have done
me all the good in the world. Talk about the new
woman, why I feel now a brand new woman, and
my husband only this morning said to me ‘ Well,
old girl, if you were “ off color ” a few weeks ago
fou certainly look the “ pink ” of perfection now.
suppose it is your Pink Pills that have done it. ’
And so it is. Not only have I lost my rheumatic
pains, but my appetite has returned, and I can
now enjoy my food. In fact, my appetite is so
good that Brown says I will eat him out of house
and home.”
“How many boxes have you taken, Mrs.
Brown ? ”
“ I have taken seven boxes in all, and I have so
much belief in them that I intend always to keep
a box by me, and I shall recommend them to all
my friends. You are quite at liberty to publish
this statement, as I believe in giving credit to
whom credit is due.”
The reporter then withdrew, and reflected as he
walked slowly away that here at least was another
case in which the many virtues of this marvellous
preparation had been exhaustively and conclu-
sively illustrated.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are not like other
medicines, and their effects are permanent. Noth-
ing else is so prompt in pulling up the system
when, from some temporary depression or other-
wise, tone is needed. They have cured more than
fifty-five hundred cases of diseases arising from
impoverished blood, such as anaemia, pale and
sallow complexion, muscular weakness, depression
of spirits, loss of appetite, palpitation of the heart,
shortness of breath, pains in the back, nervous
headache, loss of memory, early decay, all forms
of female weakness, hysteria, paralysis, locomotor
ataxy, rheumatism and sciatica, scrofula, rickets,
hip-joint diseases, chronic erysipelas, consumption
of the bowels and lungs.
The genuine pills are sold only in wooden boxes,
about two inches in length, in a white wrapper
with the full name, Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People, printed in red. They are never
sold in bulk, or from glass jars, and any dealer
who offers substitutes in this form should be
avoided. In case of doubt it is better to send
direct to the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Company,
Queen’s Chambers, Queen’s Place, Sydney, enclos-
ing the price, 2s. 9d. a boz, or six boxes for 14s. 9d.
These pills are not a purgative, and they contain
nothing that could injure the most delicate. *

A little of the secret history of Queen Vic-
toria’s reign was revealed by Greville, that Saint
Simon of our century, and more can already be
gleaned. Prince Albert was virtually King of
England for 20 years. The last thing he did was
amending the Premier’s draft of a despatch to
America, at the utmost tension between the U.S.
and Britain. The grudge of the Queen and her
husband to Palmerston was eminently disastrous.
It plunged the nation in the Crimean war through
Palmerston’s removal from the Foreign Office.
This was because he did not consult Prince Albert
before recognising the coup d’6tat of Napoleon
111. However, after Albert’s death, Palmerston
became the Queen’s political idol. Disraeli had
succeeded him, as the particular object of aver-
sion, but managed to transfer the animus to Glad-
stone.

Dear Bulletin, —A ray of light on the
crow. There are two crows—the white-eyed Aus-
tralian crow that distributes himself impartially
over this continent, and the brown-eyed crow
( corvus coronoides) of the Gulf of Carpentaria. The
brown-eyed crow, like brown-eyed women, has
many loveable qualities, and wears the soft, sym-
pathetic, far-away, sad-and-weary expression of
an amateur poet wrestling with the awful conun-
drum of a suitable rhyme for pumpkin. He is
largely frugivorous and a meat diet disorganises
his bingie. His voice is a baritone, and when re-
hearsing his own operatic music he arches himself
into a boomerang until his beak touches the tip of
his tail. I have not time at present to enumerate
the many virtues of the brown-eyed crow, but I
have said enough to save him from the curse which
all bush-dwelling Australia hurls at the head of
his white-eyed kinsman. My experience of crows
dates back to 1859. I have seen all types of crow-
character, and each type was worse than the
others. I have seen the white-eyed crow from
where he was gorging stranded fish on an island
of the Barrier Reef, to where he rose from a dead
rat in a polygonum thicket on the edge of a dry
waterhole in the lone west. His character is
blacker than his feathers, and the only virtue he
possesses is an imaginary one discovered by people
who know nothing about him. As an article of
diet we learn in Sturt’s journal that an old starved
myall came to the camp and stayed there until he
fattened on crows shot by the whites. Mark
Twain tells us that he has eaten boiled crow, but
never afterwards hankered after it. For table
purposes he would doubtless make a pleasant
change in the menu of an average boarding-house.
Many ticks have been found on crows, but were
all dead sick enough to wish for death. And yet
the crow was the Prometheus or “ fire bringer ” of
the blacks. He stole the fire from the seven gins
in the Pleiades. If a crow were 24 hours without
a chance to steal something he would commit
suicide. He places no value on anything
acquired honestly. In Japanese mythology a
crow with eight-feet wings was sent by the god
Michi Omi to guide the army of Jimmu Tennu,
the first Emperor of Japan. In Australia the
most widely-distributed aboriginal names for the
crow were wahga, wahgan, warr and wahwa. The
town of Wagga-Wagga retains the crow’s name
in the Kamilroi and Wiradjerie dialects.
An ancient race sacrificed crocodiles in the temple
of Typhon the Evil Genius. The Scythians sacri-
ficed asses in the temple of Mars. Why not erect
a temple to the white-eyed Evil Genius of Aus-
tralia and soak the altar in the gore of the asses
who have been known to remark, in speech or
print, that “there is a good deal to be said in
favor of the crow ? ” The last crow I shot rose
from the bottom of a gully where he was so busy
with a dead lamb that he didn’t see me until I
was right over him. Then he rose and “ whizzed ”
for about 20 yards, looking round with his white
eyes dilated with terror, and kicking the air with
his feet in the vain hope of increasing his pace.
The No. 3 caught him suddenly, and the recording
angel credited me with one more noble deed. —A.
Meston, Brisbane.

An English court decided last month that
the Statute of Limitations applies to breaches of
promise. An Australian case of a few years ago
might have been successfully defended on this
ground, but the defendant’s legal adviser pooh-
poohed the plea.
The stereotyped excuse of Q. “drunks’’
is now : “ Please, yer worsh’p, I took a little
whisky to ease the pain of the dingo fever, an’,
not bein’ used to the drink, yer worsh’p,” &c., &c.
The dengue fever is a mighty social and political
power in Q. just now. It can keep a politician
out of a nasty Parliamentary division, and it can
get an honest man excused from serving on a jury.
To the embezzler who has “ the books ” safely at
home, it is simply invaluable, what time he rounds
up his friends.
H. : “ Owning a bit of Melb. suburban
property, I am deeply grateful to Chief-Commis-
sioner Chomley for the magnificent, and, I take it,
invariably sober, disentangled young policeman
placed in charge of my vicinage. One night I was
seized with a brilliant literary idea, and quickened
my pace. Bobby dropped from the clouds, as in
the shadow pantomime, with, ‘Hi! How do I
know but you came out of that backyard ? ’ and I
had to go with him and reconnoitre. Another
time I was hurrying home when he stopped me
with, ‘ Hi! How do I know but you’ve got a pal
in that house over there ? ’ Again I was under
arrest a minute and a half. ‘By thunder,’ said
I, ‘ you’ll be Chief-Commissioner yet.’ ‘ Ah,
you’re pocking borak.’”
