A City of Plagues .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Scene—Hay-street, Perth, Westralia. Time —an
early-winter day. Pedestrians crowding on to the
shop-fronts to escape mud-splashes from vehicles
tumbling along the slush-laden street. A funeral
cortege in sight, headed by a scratch crowd play-
ing “The Dead March” (poor Billy Jinkins !).
Odious stenches permeate the air, and pedestrians,
as they move listlessly along, keep lifting a
hand to their foreheads as if to clear away a heavy
load. Faces—deep-lined and sallow or pale,
tinged with a hectic flush on the wasted cheeks,
and an expression like a parson’s at an execution.
Dry, husky coughs and offensive expectorations in
great evidence. The funeral passes ; then one
pedestrian singles out another :
First Ped. : “Hullo, Jack ! Seen Bill?”
Second Ped. : “Christ! didn’t you hear?’*
First Ped. : “ No : what ? ”
Second Ped. : “ Dead ! ”
First Ped. : “ Gor’struth ! ” (And his hand
goes up to h is forehead ; a shadow of pain crosses
his face as it drops heavily to his side again.)
Sec. Ped.: “ Seen anything of Tony ? ”
First Ped.: “Ah! I was just goin’ to tell
you
Sec. Ped. (eagerly and huskily): “ Oh, !
not him, Ted ! ”
First Ped.: “ We —we buried him yesterday. ”
Sec. Ped. : “My God! what’ll his mother do?
I promised to look after him over here for her, too.
My school-mate —her only son —poor woman !
poor woman ! But I couldn’t help it, Ted, I
couldn’t help it! I only came out of the hospital
myself this morning.”
First Ped. : “ My God, it’s horrible ! ”
Here a third pedestrian joins them. There’s a
list in his walk, and a slight hesitancy in his
speech ; some people might call it a hiccough. He
greets them : “ ’Lo Ted ! ’Lo Jack ! Orl rite
agin, ole chap ? . . . Been (’ic) up to see Jerry
Mao—hospital— dyiW (’ic). Got a load ‘ shypoo ’
—kill germs. Know (’ic) that young bloke sat
’tween you cornin’ over in the boat ? ”
Sec. Ped. : “ What! little Billy Green, who
used to sing the comic songs ? ”
Third Ped.: “ Pegged out (’ic). Fever can’t
hurt me, though—drunk too much—have wet ? ”
The three shuffle into a threep’ny bar. They
call for three “shypoos”—second and third peds.
drinkheartily—first ped. just touches liquor with his
lips, then sets the glass down and raises his hand
to his forehead. A deep sigh escapes him, and he
speaks huskily:—
“ By , Jack, I feel anyhow ! ”
His cheeks have become suddenly livid, his jaw
drops, then shuts with a clash, then drops again.
He feels the counter for support with his hands, as
though there was no sight in his glittering eyes.
He gives vent to a half-articulated appeal, then
reels and falls heavily.
“ It’s only a faint,” says the third ped.
“ Dead drunk ! ” says the barman ; but one who
knows goes out for a cab. In a few moments the
ufferer is placed carefully in it. The cabby asks
no questions. He’s been there before. The Hos-
pital! Luckily a bed is vaoant (somebody had
just died). The patient is admitted. A little
while later the second and third peds. re-appear.
(’ic) “What was it he said?”
“Fever and pneumonia !”
“Any hope ?”
“ None /”
Perth.

Sydney Tory press alleges that the Hyde
Park railway-station controversy is merely a
question of railway sleepers versus human sleepers.
Hoe and Co., of London, are now building
for Sydney Daily Telegraph two up-to-date
machines, each capable of printing, cutting, fold-
ing, delivering, and numbering 36,000 eight-page
papers an hour.
A variation on the good old ‘ ‘ what might
have been a very serious accident.” In all solem-
nity a Tas. paper says :
As the runaway came careering round the corner, a hoy
took off his boots and threw one of them at the horse,
bringing the animal to the ground. The boy then seized
the opportunity and the horse’s head, holding it securely
until help arrived. The trap and harness was uninjured.
Just so. Then the boy gathered up his boots and
went round to the office to write up the above
local. No mistake about that boy’s future.
Dear Bulletin, —Paisley, the birthplace
of Premier Reid, is always spoken of in a con-
temptuous way, very much as Geelong is referred
to by the inhabitants of Smellbourne. “Edin-
burgh people, Glasgow folk, and Paisley bodies ”
is a common form of comparison. One Paisley
“body” in Australia, on being asked his birth-
place, replied, “near Glasgow, something like a
suburb ”; but, being pressed, admitted that Pais-
ley was the locality, and said : “ But dinna tell
anybody, for, as sure’s death, I couldna help it.”
—One o’ the Glesca Folk.
Condensation of the recent speech of a
member of Mackay (Q.) Harbor Board: “It’s
better for a Q. port to have a bad harbor than a
good one. Gladstone has the best harbor in the
province, and it is the poorest coast town. Port
Denison (Bowen) comes second, and is the next
poorest. Cooktown comes third, and is about
equal to Bowen in poverty. Townsville has the
worst harbor I ever saw ; it is a live, growing,
and thriving town. Rockhampton and Brisbane
have only third-rate harbors ; both big, and still
growing—not because of, but in spite of, their
harbors.”
Dear Bulletin, —If my memory serves
me, the firm from which the Nizam of Hyderabad
bought the allegedly stolen =£300,000 diamond, is
the firm a member of which was, half-a-dozen
years back, acquitted of embezzling a large lump
of same potentate’s coin over another diamond-
deal. In any case, a stealing more or less is no-
thing in the histories of the big Oriental diamonds,
which have all been so pertinaciously prigged and
re-prigged for centuries that it’s impossible to say
who is or was the rightful owner of any of them.
Even the boasted Kohinoor, as far as it can be
said to belong to anybody, is the property, not of
the British Crown, but of whoever now represents
the former Panjabi reigning family. Might’s the
only right, after all ! When it comes to hard cash,
the “ secret of England’s greatness ” is carefully
stowed away on a top-shelf.— Macassar.
A Terrible Kiss.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ropia, the wife of Wairuru, and Apia, her
daughter, hid themselves in the manuka and ate
fern-root to satisfy their hunger. They had seen
all their tribe fall before the guns of the N gapuhi,
and Hongi himself had set the raupo torch to the
whares. The two women alone were saved out of
all the great tribe of Ngawaruru.
Apia went down to the water to drink, and as
she stooped a Ngapuhi warrior sprang from cover
and seized her. “You are my captive,” he said,
“ and my slave.”
Apia uttered a cry, and, as her mother ran
quickly towards her, the Ngapuhi said : “ Another
captive! ”
The old woman spat at him. “Dog of a
Ngapuhi,” she cried, “ Apia, my daughter, shall
not be thy slave. Take me and I will hoe thy
kumeras. ”
“Nay,” said the Ngapuhi, “but I will take
both ; and Apia shall share my whare.”
“ Cursed be thy head,” screamed Ropia. “ Were
it not for the guns your people stole from the
pakehas my tribe would have filled the ovens with
Ngapuhi warriors. To Apia thou art but dirt.”
“Good dirt,” exclaimed the Ngapuhi; “and
Apia shall do my will.”
“ Flee quickly, Apia,” whispered the old woman
as she seized the Ngapuhi around the neck.
Apia sprang away, leaving her robe in the hands
of the warrior.
“ Come back,” shouted the man, “ or I will slay
thy mother ! ” and the girl halting, naked, saw the
tomahawk raised to strike.
“ Let me die, Apia ; but run—run quickly.”
“Nay, mother, I will not leave thee;” and
Apia approached the Ngapuhi and said, “Spare
my mother and I will do thy will.”
“ Is that how a daughter of Wairuru speaks ? ”
asked the old woman, bitterly ; but Apia whis-
pered in her mother’s ear an awful command, and
Ropia sank to the ground apparently insensible.
Then the warrior, holding the girl’s wrist, drew
her away, and uttered threats and promised bribes
in his passion, and Apia suddenly put her arms
about the warrior’s neck and kissed him, and
cried, in tones of strange meaning, “Thy captive,
O chief, and thy slave!” She saw that her mother
stood over the reclining man with his tomahawk
upheld.
“Thy captive, O chief; thy slave!” cried Apia
again, and she pressed her lips tightly to his and
clung to him with all her strength, for she saw the
tomahawk descend. But the blow was struck
with an old woman’s strength : it missed the
warrior’s head and gashed his shoulder. The
Ngapuhi, maddened with pain and fear, struggled
to free himself, but Apia held his face to hers.
“Thy captive, thy slave!” she cried again, and
again the tomahawk descended. The Ngapuhi,
in desperation, seized the girl’s lips with his
teeth and bit savagely, but the girl clung
to him with arms and legs, and though the
man’s teeth tore her cruelly she would not
loose her hold. Again the tomahawk de-
scended, and again, until the Ngapuhi ceased to
struggle and rolled over dead ; but still his teeth
were set through Apia’s lips until the mother
opened the strong jaws with the edge of the toma-
hawk, and though to speak was agony, the girl
whispered : “Am I not a daughter of Wairuru ? ”
and the mother replied : “ Aye, and thy kiss will
be remembered. We will take the Ngapuhi’s
cloak and his weapons, and thou shalt wear them.”
So the captives stripped the captor and burned
his body, and tramped southward to the Waikato
where Apia afterwards became the wife of Panui,
and the scar on her lips was held as a mark of
honor, and her terrible kiss was built into songs.
M.L.
F. ROLLETT.

The after-effects of influenza and rheumatism
are a combination well calculated to make any life
miserable. Influenza weakens the whole nervous
system, frequently leaving the victim with a con-
stant feeling of depression, fickle appetite, head-
ache, heart palpitation, and a feeling of exhaustion
as the result of the slightest exertion. As a cure
for the depressing after-effects of influenza, Dr.
Williams Pink Pills have met with more marvel-
lous success -than any other medicine known to
science. They act directly upon the blood and
nerves, renewing the one and fortifying the other
thus driving disease from the system. Among
those who speak in grateful terms of the wonder-
ful creative powers of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills is
Mr. William Carroll, of Sydney, Manitoba. Mr.
Carroll has been a sufferer from rheumatism to a
considerable extent for upwards of twenty years,
and to add to his sufferings, for two successive
winters he was attacked with influenza in all its
severity, and was left in a weak and despondent
condition. Mr. Carroll says:—“Following the
second attack of influenza my whole system was
so weakened that it was with difficulty I could
do any work. In this condition I was an
easy prey to my old enemy, rheumatism, and I
suffered the agony of its pains in almost every
bone in my body. I doctored a great deal, but
without any beneficial results, and I spent seven-
teen dollars for one medicine largely advertised as
a cure for rheumatism, without the least benefit.
I naturally felt despondent, and thought the
trouble had fastened itself upon mein permanent
form. I had read so much of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills that I at last determined to give them a trial.
I can t say that I felt anything in the way of bene-
ficial results until I had used the third box, but at
that juncture the encouragement was so great that
I determined to continue the treatment, and I
used the Pink Pills throughout the winter, con-
stantly gaining in strength. When spring arrived
I found myself better than I had been for years,
every vestage of rheumatism had disappeared, and
although ten months have now passed since I took
the last pill, I have not had the slightest return of
the trouble. I therefore think it is but right that
I should make known the good I have received
from the famous Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and I
hope my experience may be the means of helping
some other sufferer on the road to renewed health.”
An Accident at the
Sydney Docks .
A SAILORS’ NARROW ESCAPE.
HEAD STRUCK A PILE WHILE ROW-
ING ASHORE WITH THE LINE
FROM A SHIP, AND FELL INTO
THE WATER BRUISED AND IN-
SENSIBLE.
When a newspaper reporter went in search of
Mr. John Smith, of 4 Wilmot-street, Sydney, he
fortunately found him at home, and on his re-
marking that the object of his visit was to obtain
&
*
particulars of a narrow escape from death he had
, r \ Smith had lately experienced, found no
aithculty in obtaining the same.
Yes, it is quite true that X did have a narrow
escape said Mr. Smith, “and if you want to
hear about it I will tell you with pleasure. I was
down at Howard Smith’s wharf on the 19th
November last, where I was generally employed
and at the time I met with my accident was tak-
ing a line ashore from an incoming steamer.
Lhrough colliding with a post while in the boat I
got thrown into the water, receiving severe injury
to my head, in addition to being half drowned by
the quantity of dirty water I swallowed. I was
taken to the Sydney Hospital, where I remained
three weeks.”
“ Did they do you much good, Mr. Smith ?”
“ No, indeed ; they only patched me up for the
time being, and it was lucky for me that I got a
bit of an easy job minding the gate at the wharf
tor 1 was so desperately weak and debilitated that
i. could not attempt to take on hard work. How-
ever, I managed to stay on for five weeks, and
then returned home for three or four days’rest,
which I was badly in need of. I then went to see
the Government Medical Officer, who said my
case was incurable, and wanted me to go to Liver-
pool Asylum ; but this I refused to do, so I ulti-
mately returned to Sydney Hospital, where I
remained three weeks.”
“Did you feel any better after that, Mr.
Smith ? ”
“ No, decidedly worse ; in fact, twice over
worse, and was glad to return to my home again.”
‘‘How did you feel affected, Mr. Smith ? ”
“ Chiefly spitting of blood, which would keep
me awake day and night. Mrs. Rees, my land-
lady, can tell you how bad I was ; in fact, she
thought it was all over with me and that I should
not be long for this world.”
“ How did you hear of Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills, which we are told was what pulled you to-
gether again ? ”
“ Through a pamphlet being left at the house.
I thought at first that the Pills were too expensive
for me, but Mrs. Rees persuaded me to try them,
so I got a box and started to take them according
to printed directions. After the first box I felt
much better, the blood-spitting almost left me, my
appetite returned; indeed, it was better than be-
fore my accident. It is a remarkably rare thing
for me to spit blood at all now.”
‘‘ Do you attribute this beneficial effect to Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills ? ”
“ I do entirely ; in fact, in must be so, for I have
taken no other medicine. Before my accident I
always enjoyed the best of health, having led a
healthy outdoor life, so it proves my accident must
have been a very serious one to knock nie up as it
did. I have the firmest belief in the virtues of
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and so has Mrs. Rees,
my landlady. (Mrs. Rees here certainly corrobo-
rated Mr. Smith’s statements in every particular).
I have,” continued Mr. Smith, “ strongly recom-
mended these pills to all my friends and acquaint-
ances, and they, have also a strong advocate in
Mrs. Rees, who is a firm believer in them.”
(Signed) JOHN SMITH,
4 Wilmot-street,
Off Liverpool-street,
Sydney.

Mrs. J. H. M'Millan, South Finch, Ont., says :
“ For seven years I was affected with what is
generally termed female weakness, and was at
times for weeks unable to leave my bed. My
friends all thought that I was going into a decline
and had consumption. My complexion was
sallow, my appetite was almost gone, and I was
terribly emaciated; my head gave me great trouble,
and at times the pain was so great that I feared
it would affect my reason. I had tried many
medicines without avail, and began to despair of
recovery, when I was urged to try Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. The result has been marvellous.
From a sickly, despairing woman I have been
transformed into one full of health and energy
and I feel more thankful than I can say for the
wonders Pink Pills have done for me.”

Mr. Smellingscheck.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We met him in a sixpenny restaurant—
“ All meals, 6d.— Good beds, Is.” That was
before sixpenny restaurants rose to a third-class
position, and became possibly respectable places
to live in, through the establishment, beneath
them, of fourpenny hash-houses (good beds, 6d.),
and, beneath them again, of three- penny “dining-
rooms— clean beds, 4d.”
There were five beds in our apartment, the head
of one against the foot of the next, and so on
round the room, with a space where the door and
washstand were. I chose the bed the head of which
was near the foot of his—because he looked like a
man who took his bath regularly. I would like,
in the interests of sentiment, to describe the place
as a miserable, filthy, evil-smelling garret; but I
can t because it wasn’t. The room was large and
airy ; the floor was scrubbed and the windows
cleaned at least once, a week, and the beds kept
fresh and neat, which is more—a good deal more
than can be said of many genteel private boarding-
houses. The lodgers were mostly respectable un-
employed, and one or two—the fortunate men !
in work ; it was the casual boozer, the professional
loafer, and the occasional spieler—the one-shilling-
“ men—who made the place objectionable, not
the hard-working people who paid .£l5 a week for
the house ; and, but for the one-night lodgers and
big' gilt black-and-red bordered and “ shaded ”
bd in the window—which made me glance
guiltily up and down the street, like a burglar
about to do a job, before I went in—l was prettv
comfortable there.
* • * *
They called him “Mr. Smellingscheck, ”
as near as I can remember to spell it, and the
people of the house treated him with a peculiar
kind of respect, or deference, the reason for
which they themselves were doubtless unable
to explain or even understand. The haggard
woman who made the beds called him “Mr.
Smell-’is-cheek. ” Poor fellow! I didn’t think
by the look of him, that he’d smelt his cheque or
anyone else’s, or that anyone else had smelt his ’for
many a long day. He was a fat man, slow and
placid. He looked like a “typical” monopolist
who had, in some unaccountable manner, got into
a suit of ciothes belonging to a Domain unem-
ployed, and hadn t noticed, or had entirely for-
gotten, the circumstance in his business cares—if
such a word as care could be connected with such
a calm, self-contained nature. He wore a suit of
cheap slops of some kind of shoddy “tweed.”
The coat was too small and the trousers too short
and they were drawn up to meet the waistcoat—
which they did with painful difficulty, now and
then showing, by way of protest, two pairs of
brass buttons and the ends of the brace-straps,
and they seemed to blame the irresponsive
waistcoat or the wearer for it all. Yet he
never gave way to assist them. A pair of
burst ’lastic-sides were in full evidence, and a rim
of cloudy sock, with a hole in it, showed at every
But he put on his clothes and wore them like—
like a gentleman. He had two white shirts, and
they were both dirty. He’d lay them out on the
bed, turn them over, regard them thoughtfully
choose that which appeared to his calm understand-
ing to be the cleaner, and put it on, and wear it until
it was unmistakeably dirtier than the other, then
he’d wear the other ’till it was dirtier than the
first. He managed his three collars the same way.
His handkerchiefs were washed in the bathroom,
and dried, without the slightest disguise, in the
bedroom. He never hurried in anything. The
way he cleaned his teeth, shaved, and made his
toilet almost transformed the place, in my imagin-
ation, into a gentleman’s dressingroom.
* * • •
He talked politics and such tilings in the
abstract—always in the abstract —calmly in the
abstract. He was an old-fashioned Conservative
of the Victorian-deadlock style. When he was
moved by an extra shower of aggressive demo-
cratic cant—which was seldom —he defended
Capital, but only as if it needed no defence, and
as if its opponents were merely thoughtless, ignor-
ant children, whom he condescended to set right
because of their inexperience and for their own
good. He stuck calmly to his own order—the
order which had, or would have if he had given it
the chance, dropped him like a foul thing when
the bottom dropped out of his boom—whatever
that was. Tor he never talked of his misfortunes.
* * * «
He took his meals, at the little greasy
table in the dark corner downstairs, just as if he
were dining at the Exchange. He had a chop—
rather well-done — and a sheet of the Herald for
breakfast. He carried two handkerchiefs; he
used one for a handkerchief and the other for a
table-napkin, and sometimes folded it absently
and laid it on the table. He rose slowly putting
his chair back, took down his battered old green
hat, and regarded it thoughtfully (in the abstract)
as though it had just occurred to him in a calm,
casual way that he’d drop into his hatter, if he
had time, on his way down town, and get it
blocked —or else send the messenger round with it
during business hours. He’d draw his stick out
from behind the next chair, plant it, and, if you
hadn’t quite finished your side of the conversation,
stand politely waiting until you were done. Then
he’d look (for a suitable reply) into his hat, put
it on, give it a twitch to settle it on his head
as gentlemen do a “ chimney-pot ” —step
out into the gangway, turn his face to the door,
and walk slowly out on to the middle of the pave-
ment —looking more placidly well-to-do than ever.
The saying is that clothes make a man, but he
made his almost respectable, and improved them
just by wearing them. Then he’d consult his
watch he stuck to the watch all through, and it
seemed a good one—l often wondered why he
didn t pawn it—then he’d turn slowly, right turn,
and look down the street. Then slowly back, left-
about turn, and take a cool survey in that direc-
tion, as if calmly undecided whether to take a cab
and drive to the Exchange, or, it being a very fine
morning, and he having half-an-hour to spare,
vralk there and drop in at his club on the way.
He d conclude to walk. I never saw him go any-
where in particular, but he walked and stood as if
he could.
* * * *
Coming quietly into the room one day, I
surprised him sitting at the table with his arms
lying on it and his face resting on them. I heard
something like a sob. He rose hastily, and
gathered up some papers which were on the
table ; then he turned round, rubbing his forehead
and eyes with his forefinger and thumb, and told
us that he suffered from—something, I forget the
name of it, but it was a well-to-do ailment. His
manner seemed a bit jolted and hurried for a
minute or so, and then he was himself again. He
told me he was leaving for Melbourne next day.
He left while I was out, and left an envelope
downstairs for me. There was nothing in it
except a pound-note.
♦ * * *
I saw him in Brisbane afterwards, well-
dressed, getting out of a cab at the entrance of
one of the leading hotels. But his manner was
no more self-contained and well-to-do than it had
been in the old sixpenny days—because it couldn’t
be. We had a well-to-do whisky together, and he
talked of things in the abstract. He seemed just
as if he’d met me in the Australia.
HENRY LAWSON.
A fine sample of that scarce and rapacious
carnivorous marsupial the “native tiger,” which
has a beautiful spotted and banded skin, some-
thing like the native cat’s, was shot at Lilliemur,
Wimmera, Vic., the other day, by Mr. Hawker,
a farmer, after it had killed and partly eaten two
collie pups. The “tiger” was 4Jft. long and
30in. high. Perhaps the “Tantanoola tiger” was
not a myth after all.
In Richmond (Vic.) court the other day
a man was charged with having had im-
proper relations with a girl under the Age of
Consent. Just as the bench was about to launch
the thunders of the law against the guilty one,
a telegram arrived from the girl’s father (who was
in Westralia) sanctioning her marriage to defen-
dant. The police-inspector at once withdrew the
charge, and the bench, with a “ Bless-you-my-
children ” smile, dismissed the case. “ The young
couple then left the court to seek a neighboring
clergyman. ” Some time ago a similar case occurred
in southern N.S.W., but on that occasion the
bench didn’t bother asking the parents but simply
gave its consent to the marriage.
A young lady, now in trouble in Sydney, is
alleged to have been a bigamist in a large way of
business. In 1890 she pleaded guilty to marrying
one Heath while another husband, Martin, was
still alive. The charge this time is that she married
Coulston while Martin and Heath were still alive,
and it is also alleged that she married Bradley in
between times. Bradley appears to be a matter
of uncertainty, but the other three are admitted.
Apparently Martin was a failure, having a habit
of getting into gaol, and his young wife had to go
round marrying other men at intervals for the
sake of a home. After all, bigamy is perhaps the
wisest course for a lady so situated. It is neces-
sary for a woman to put in three years as a de-
serted wife before she can get a divorce and find
another man to lawfully support her, and three
years spent in hustling for an honest livelihood
ages a woman considerably, so that when she is
available the other man probably doesn’t want her.
Or, if he does marry her, he may also disappear,
and then another three years’ hustle has to be put
in before she can get free, and that is certain to
leave her very old indeed. Bigamy seldom means
more than three months for a woman who pleads
desertion as a mitigating circumstance, and be-
tween the chance of three months’ confinement
and the certainty of three years’ hard labor
at plain sewing or the like, the advantage
seems to lie with bigamy every time. And, if
the second partner of the lady-bigamist deserts
her, she can simply go and “big” again. So
long as she is clever and reasonably attractive,
it is a resource that is always open, while divorce
is a pool of Siloam which the law agitates with
its expensive brief once in three years only, and
not always then.
Never a Foe!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They preach and they patter of peace, do the wise,
As though Truth ever kissed with the Lovers of
Lies;
They bid us be meek and they bid us give hand
To the caitiff and craven that creep in the land;
And the battle that putteth the man to the test,
And sunders the base as a dross from the best,
They tell how it worketh but evil and woe,
But I wot that each man with a soul hath a foe !
Now gather thy friends as a flock to thy fold,
And garner their love in thy heart as a hold ;
Be cheery of brow, but be chary of hand,
And hope for no truce with the sycophant band.
Dost call thyself comrade, yet palter and pale ?
Dost prate of thy love and yet falter and fail ?
Wouldst thou bind me as steel thou must back me
with blow —
He hath never a friend who hath never a foci
So ready with hand on the hilt, brother mine ;
To-day we drink deep of Bellona’s red wine.
Dost hear how the trumpets all joyously swell?
Dost see the grim front of the armies of Hell ?
Aye ! Life is no Sybarite rose-bed, I ween,
But a battle to-day as it ever hath been ;
For from Heaven above to Gehenna below
Must the true man be tried—in the fire of a foe.
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIG.
From Pleasant Creek (Vic.) News :
At the business place of Messrs. Trend and Brown yes-
terday we had shown to us a butcher’s-cart which has
just been repaired and painted for Mr. Cock. The artist,
Mr. Speary, has displayed considerable ability and taste
with the brush. The firm are to be congratulated on hav-
ing secured the services of such an artist. The cart is
beautifully decorated with flowers, and the name of the
proprietor is executed in bold letters.
A Roma (Q.) publican was sick—apparently
suffering a recovery after more or less d.t. He was
in a very low condition, and to be alone was intoler-
able to his shattered nerves. His nurse left his
bedroom for a few minutes, thinking him asleep.
He jumped out of bed, and, in his shirt, walked
round a verandah into the bar, apparently making
for the room behind the bar. He was not in pub-
lic view for one minute, and was not responsible
for his act. Two constables arrested him in the
bar, took him to the lock-up, and placed him in a
padded cell. Frantically he entreated that some-
one might stay with bim. Petition unheeded.
He died in the cell, alone and unattended. No
enquiry was held.
